The Best of Generations and the Order of the Caliphs
Al-Laqqani presents the hierarchy of the Companions' virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of virtue among the Companions and the basis for ranking the first four caliphs.

Core Idea
The Companions' virtue follows the order of the caliphate.

Al-Laqqani begins by affirming that the Companions are the best generation, then specifies that the best among them are those who held the caliphate—Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali. He then lists other groups in descending order of virtue. This lesson examines this ranking and its sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key ranking
Read the first line: 'Khayr al-qurun' and note that the best are those who undertook the caliphate.
This establishes the principle that caliphal order reflects virtue.
2
List the groups
The poet mentions six groups completing ten: the caliphs, then the people of Badr, Uhud, Pledge of Ridwan, and the 'foremost ones'.
The phrase 'completing ten' may refer to the ten promised Paradise.
3
Understand the basis
Reflect that this ranking is based on textual evidence and consensus.
The passage says their virtue is known by text (nass).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصَحْبُهُ خيرُ القُرونِ فَاسْتَمِعْ فَتَابِعِي فتابع لِمَنْ تَبِعْ وخيرهم من وُلِّيَ الخِلافَة وأَمْرُهُم في الفَضْلِ كالخِلافَة يَليه يليهمُ قَومٌ كِرَامَ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُم سِت تمامُ العَشَرة فأهلُ بَدْرٍ العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ فأهلُ أُحْدٍ فَبَيْعَةِ الرَّضْوانِ والسابقون فَضلُهُم نصا عُرف هذا وفي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قد اختلف وأوَّلِ التَشَاجُرَ الَّذِي وَرَدْ إنْ خُضْتَ فيه واجْتَنِبْ داءَ الحَسَدُ ومالك وسائر الأئِمَّة كذا أَبُو الْقَاسِم هُداةُ الأُمَّة فواجبٌ تَقليدُ حَبْرٍ مِنهُمُ كَذَا حَكَى القَوْمُ بِلَفْظِ يُفْهَمُ
And his Companions are the best of generations, so listen: so follow those who follow. And the best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate, and their matter in virtue is like the caliphate. Then follow them a noble people, pious; their number is six, completing ten. So the people of Badr of great status, then the people of Uhud, then the Pledge of Ridwan. And the foremost ones, their virtue is known by text; in their identification there is disagreement. And as for the discord that occurred, if you delve into it, avoid the disease of envy. And Malik and the rest of the imams, likewise Abu al-Qasim, guides of the community. So it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among them, as the people narrated in a phrase that is understood.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses the virtues of the Companions (sahaba) and the proper attitude towards them. The poet begins by stating that the Companions are the best of generations (khayr al-qurun), and commands to follow those who follow them. The best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate (khalifa), which refers to Abu Bakr, then Umar, Uthman, Ali in order of virtue. Their ranking in virtue matches the order of the caliphate. Next, he mentions the next best group: the people of Badr, then Uhud, then the Pledge of Ridwan. The 'foremost ones' (al-sabiqun) have known virtue but specific identification may differ. The poet warns against delving into the discord (tashajur) that occurred among the Companions, advising to avoid envy (hasad). Then he mentions the great imams: Malik, Abu al-Qasim (possibly al-Shafi'i or another), as guides of the umma. Finally, he states that it is obligatory (wajib) to follow a scholar (hibr) from among them, as transmitted.

Key Terms
صَحْب
خَيْر القُرُون
خِلافَة
أهل بَدْر
أهل أُحُد
بَيْعَة الرِّضْوَان
سَابِقُون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the ranking of the Companions' virtue?
A) Their wealth
B) Their participation in battles
C) The order of the caliphate
D) Their age

Key Takeaway
The Companions' virtue is hierarchically organized, with the caliphs at the top, followed by participants in major events like Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson places the caliphs above all other Companions. Is your love and respect for Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali truly in that order, or have you allowed modern influences to blur this hierarchy? What one change will you make today to honor them as taught?
?
The passage ranks the people of Badr above the people of Uhud and the Pledge of Ridwan. Do you find yourself giving equal reverence to all Companions, ignoring the specific merits Allah granted them? How can you actively cultivate a heart that acknowledges and internalizes these distinctions?
?
Al-Laqqani mentions that the sabiqun's virtue is textually established but their identification is disputed. Have you ever felt uncertain about the Companions' statuses due to disagreements among scholars? What step will you take this week to strengthen your reliance on the clear, agreed-upon hierarchy taught by Ahl al-Sunnah?

Avoiding Discord and Following the Imams
Al-Laqqani warns against engaging in Companion disputes and commands following the imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper attitude towards the historical disagreements among the Companions and the obligation to follow the early imams.

Core Idea
Do not delve into the Companions' disputes; instead, follow the imams.

The poet warns against engaging in discussions about the discord (tashajur) that occurred among the Companions, advising to avoid envy (hasad). He then mentions the great imams like Malik and Abu al-Qasim, who are guides of the community, and asserts that it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among them. This lesson explains these directives.

Guided Path
1
Heed the warning
Read the line about 'tashajur' and note the command to avoid it and the disease of envy.
2
Identify the imams
The passage names Malik and 'Abu al-Qasim' (likely Imam al-Shafi'i), calling them guides of the umma.
Abu al-Qasim is a kunya for al-Shafi'i.
3
Understand the obligation
The poet says it is obligatory (wajib) to follow a scholar (hibr) from among them, as narrated.
This emphasizes taqlid of a qualified imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصَحْبُهُ خيرُ القُرونِ فَاسْتَمِعْ فَتَابِعِي فتابع لِمَنْ تَبِعْ وخيرهم من وُلِّيَ الخِلافَة وأَمْرُهُم في الفَضْلِ كالخِلافَة يَليه يليهمُ قَومٌ كِرَامَ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُم سِت تمامُ العَشَرة فأهلُ بَدْرٍ العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ فأهلُ أُحْدٍ فَبَيْعَةِ الرَّضْوانِ والسابقون فَضلُهُم نصا عُرف هذا وفي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قد اختلف وأوَّلِ التَشَاجُرَ الَّذِي وَرَدْ إنْ خُضْتَ فيه واجْتَنِبْ داءَ الحَسَدُ ومالك وسائر الأئِمَّة كذا أَبُو الْقَاسِم هُداةُ الأُمَّة فواجبٌ تَقليدُ حَبْرٍ مِنهُمُ كَذَا حَكَى القَوْمُ بِلَفْظِ يُفْهَمُ
And his Companions are the best of generations, so listen: so follow those who follow. And the best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate, and their matter in virtue is like the caliphate. Then follow them a noble people, pious; their number is six, completing ten. So the people of Badr of great status, then the people of Uhud, then the Pledge of Ridwan. Commentary
This passage discusses the virtues of the Companions (sahaba) and the proper attitude towards them. The poet begins by stating that the Companions are the best of generations (khayr al-qurun), and commands to follow those who follow them. The best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate (khalifa), which refers to Abu Bakr, then Umar, Uthman, Ali in order of virtue. Their ranking in virtue matches the order of the caliphate. Next, he mentions the next best group: the people of Badr, then Uhud, then the Pledge of Ridwan. The 'foremost ones' (al-sabiqun) have known virtue but specific identification may differ. The poet warns against delving into the discord (tashajur) that occurred among the Companions, advising to avoid envy (hasad). Then he mentions the great imams: Malik, Abu al-Qasim (possibly al-Shafi'i or another), as guides of the umma. Checkpoint
What attitude does the poet prescribe towards the discord among the Companions?
A) Research it thoroughly
B) Avoid discussing it and guard against envy
C) Take sides
D) Ignore it completely

Key Takeaway
The proper response to historical disagreements among the Companions is to refrain from delving into them, avoid envy, and instead follow the guidance of the early imams.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear others arguing about the Companions, do you find it easy to stay silent, or does your heart lean to join? What would it cost you to walk away?
?
The poet calls envy a 'disease'. Is there any trace of jealousy in your heart towards a Companion or a scholar? How will you cut it out today?
?
Are you truly committed to following a living scholar from the Ahl al-Sunnah, or do you pick and choose opinions based on your comfort? What one step can you take to attach yourself to a reliable guide?

The Hierarchy of Companion Virtues
Enumeration of virtue ranks among Companions and early generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of virtue among the Companions as outlined by Sanusi, including the caliphs, the ten promised Paradise, and the participants of key events.

Core Idea
Virtue among the Companions follows a divinely established order: caliphs first, then the ten, then participants of Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge.

These verses from Sanusi's poem establish a clear ranking of virtue among the Companions and the early generations. The poet begins by affirming that the Companions are the best generation, then specifies that the caliphs are the best among them, followed by a group of six who complete the ten promised Paradise, and finally the participants of specific battles and pledges. This lesson unpacks that hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the poetic structure: each verse adds a new category.
2
Identify the four groups in order
List the groups mentioned: Companions (generation), caliphs, six additional companions completing ten, and participants of Badr, Uhud, and Pledge of Ridwan.
3
Understand the link between caliphate and virtue
Verse 76 explains that the best of the Companions are those who assumed the caliphate, and their order in virtue corresponds to the order of their rule.
This ties political leadership to spiritual rank.
4
Recognize the six completing the ten
Verse 77 mentions a noble group of six who, together with the four caliphs, make up the ten Companions promised Paradise.
These six are the remaining 'ashara mubashshara.
5
Reflect on the specific events
Verse 78 highlights the participants of Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan as having special merit, after the ten.
This shows the heightened status of those who were present at these pivotal moments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٥ - وصَحْبُهُ خيرُ القُرونِ فَاسْتَمِعْ فَتَابِعِي فتابع لِمَنْ تَبِعْ ٧٦ - وخيرهم من وُلِّيَ الخِلافَة وأَمْرُهُم في الفَضْلِ كالخِلافَة ٧٧ - يَليه يليهمُ قَومٌ كِرَامَ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُم سِت تمامُ العَشَرة ٧٨ - فأهلُ بَدْرٍ العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ فأهلُ أُحْدٍ فَبَيْعَةِ الرَّضْوانِ
75. And his Companions are the best of generations, so listen: the follower and the successor are for one who follows. 76. And the best of them are those who assumed the caliphate, and their order in virtue is like the caliphate. 77. Next to them, there is a noble righteous group, their number is six completing ten. 78. So the people of Badr of great status, then the people of Uhud, then the people of the Pledge of Ridwan.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
These verses describe the virtues of the Companions. Verse 75 states that the Companions (ṣaḥbuhu) are the best of all generations, and then the tābi'ūn (followers) and those who follow them in goodness. Verse 76 establishes that the best of the Companions are the caliphs (the first four), and their rank in virtue corresponds to the order of their caliphate. Verse 77 mentions a noble group of six who complete the ten (the ten promised Paradise): the four caliphs plus six others? Actually, 'six completing ten' likely refers to the ten Companions promised Paradise (the `Ashara Mubashshara), though the poet mentions six in addition to the four caliphs? Verse 78 then lists specific groups: the participants of the Battle of Badr (of great status), the participants of Uhud, and those who pledged allegiance at Hudaybiyya (Bay'at al-Ridwan).

Key Terms
الصحابة
الخلفاء
أهل بدر
أهل أحد
بيعة الرضوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the best of the Companions are not necessarily the caliphs, or that virtue is unrelated to political leadership.
Correction: The poet explicitly states that the best of the Companions are those who assumed the caliphate, and their order in virtue mirrors the order of their rule. This establishes a clear hierarchy where the caliphs are the most virtuous.

Checkpoint
According to verse 76, how does the order of virtue among the Companions relate to the caliphate?
A) Virtue is unrelated to caliphate
B) Virtue is like the order of caliphate
C) The caliphs are the worst
D) Only the first caliph is virtuous

Key Takeaway
The caliphs are the most virtuous of the Companions, followed by the other ten promised Paradise, then the participants of Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that the caliphs are ranked by divine order, how do you feel about the first three caliphs? Have you ever harbored any doubt about their virtue? What would it take for you to fully accept this hierarchy as truth?
?
If the Companions are the best generation, are you personally striving to emulate their qualities? Which specific virtue of the Companions mentioned in this lesson do you need to cultivate in your own life starting today?
?
The poet commands you to 'listen' and accept this order. Are there any teachings about the Companions that you find difficult to accept? How can you train yourself to submit to what revelation has established, even when it challenges your inclinations?

The Forerunners, Discord, and the Imams
Three verses on forerunners, discord, and Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's guidance on the forerunners, the first fitnah, and the Imams as guides.

Core Idea
Know the virtue of the forerunners, avoid envy about discord, and follow the Imams.

This short poem presents three key teachings: the forerunners' virtue is clear but their exact identity is debated, the first discord should be approached without envy, and the Imams are the guides of the community. Let's explore each verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the three verses
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the poetic form. Then read the literal translation.
2
Understand each verse's message
Break down each verse: verse 79 on the forerunners, verse 80 on discord, verse 81 on the Imams. Note the key terms and the poet's advice.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the poet warns against delving into the first fitnah with envy and how the Imams serve as guides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٩ - والسابقون فَضلُهُم نصا عُرف هذا وفي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قد اختلف ٨٠ - وأوَّلِ التَشَاجُرَ الَّذِي وَرَدْ إنْ خُضْتَ فيه واجْتَنِبْ داءَ الحَسَدُ ٨١ - ومالك وسائر الأئِمَّة كذا أَبُو الْقَاسِم هُداةُ الأُمَّة
79. And the forerunners, their virtue is known by text; this is known, but regarding their identification, there is disagreement. 80. And the first discord that occurred, if you delve into it, avoid the disease of envy. 81. And Malik and all the Imams, likewise Abu al-Qasim, are the guides of the Ummah.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
Verse 79 refers to the 'sābiqūn' (forerunners in faith), whose virtue is established by the Qur'an and hadith (naṣṣan), but there is disagreement among scholars about exactly who they are (their identification, ta'yīn). Verse 80 warns about the first fitnah (strife) that occurred among the Companions; if one must examine it, they should avoid the disease of envy (ḥasad). Verse 81 then mentions specific Imams: Imam Malik and 'all the Imams', and Abu al-Qasim (likely referring to a scholar, perhaps al-Qasim ibn Muhammad or another early authority), stating that they are the guides of the Ummah (hudāt al-umma).

Key Terms
السابقون
التشاجر
الحسد
الأئمة
مالك
أبو القاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first fitnah is a topic for casual debate or that it is permissible to speak about it with enmity.
Correction: The poet warns that discussing early discord must be done without envy, indicating a need for caution and good intent.

Checkpoint
What does the poet warn against when discussing the first discord?
A) Speaking too much
B) The disease of envy
C) The caliphs
D) The forerunners

Key Takeaway
The poet teaches that the virtue of the forerunners is clear but their exact number is disputed, the first discord must be approached without envy, and the Imams are the guides of the Ummah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about disagreements among early Muslims, do you find yourself feeling envy or resentment towards any group? How can you purge that from your heart right now?
?
Which of the Imams mentioned (Malik or Abu al-Qasim) do you follow in your daily practice? If none, what steps will you take to learn and follow their guidance?
?
The poet says the virtue of the forerunners is known by text, but their exact identity is disputed. Are you more focused on debating who they were, or on emulating their virtue? What change can you make today?

Imamate: Obligation, Obedience, and Removal
Sanusi states the obligation, basis, and limits of imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the obligation of appointing an imam, its basis in Shari'ah, and the limits on obedience and removal.

Core Idea
Obedience to the imam is required unless he commands disbelief; only then may his covenant be abandoned.

This passage from Sanusi's work on creed clarifies the role of imamate in Islamic polity. It distinguishes between obligations derived from divine law and those based on reason, and sets clear boundaries for obedience and removal of a ruler. Let's examine the text to understand these important principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the key phrases like 'بالشَّرع' and 'إِلا بِكُفْرٍ'.
The Arabic has an ellipsis at the end, indicating continuation.
2
Identify the main claims
Note that the passage makes four claims: (1) appointment of a just imam is obligatory by Shari'ah, not reason; (2) imamate is not a pillar of creed; (3) obedience is required unless disbelief is commanded; (4) removal is only permissible in that case.
3
Examine the exception for disbelief
Focus on the phrase 'إِلا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِدَنَّ عَهْدَهُ' – it specifies that only clear disbelief justifies abandoning the imam's covenant.
This guards against the misunderstanding that any sin or injustice permits rebellion.
4
Reflect on the practical implications
Consider how this teaching balances stability and accountability. The imam commands obedience normally, but the community must resist if he commands disbelief, trusting in Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وواجب نَصْبُ إمام عدل بالشَّرع فاعلم لا بحكم العقل فليسَ رُكْنا يُعْتَقَدْ في الدِّينِ ولا تزغ عـن أمـره الـمـبـيـن إِلا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِدَنَّ عَهْدَهُ فالله يكفينا أذاهُ وَحْدَهُ بغير هذا لا يباحُ صَرْفُهُ وليسَ يُعْزَلَ انْ يَزُولَ وصفه ...
And obligatory is the appointment of a just imam by the Shari'ah, so know, not by the judgment of reason. So it is not a pillar creed-wise in the religion, nor turn away from his clear command except with disbelief, then be quick to abandon his covenant, for Allah alone suffices us against his harm. Other than this, his removal is not permissible, and he is not removed if his description ceases...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage outlines the Ash'ari position on imamate (leadership of the Muslim community). It states that appointing a just imam is an obligation (wajib) by Shari'ah (divine law), not by reason alone. The imamate is not a fundamental pillar of faith (rukn) in terms of creed, but it is a practical necessity. Obedience to the imam is required unless he commands disbelief; in that case, his covenant of obedience is to be abandoned. The community may not depose him merely for losing qualifications (e.g., physical deficiency); removal is only allowed for apostasy. God will protect the community from the imam's harm.

Key Terms
إمام عدل
نَصْب
بالشَّرع
رُكْناً يُعْتَقَدْ
كُفْر
عهده
صَرْفُهُ
عُزْل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any sin or injustice by the imam justifies rebellion or removal.
Correction: The passage specifies that only clear disbelief (kufr) permits abandoning the imam's covenant; other faults do not justify removal.

Checkpoint
What is the only reason that permits abandoning the covenant of the imam according to the text?
A) If he is unjust
B) If he becomes physically unable
C) If he commands disbelief
D) If he is corrupt

Key Takeaway
Appointment of a just imam is obligatory by Shari'ah, but obedience is required only as long as he does not command disbelief; only then may his covenant be abandoned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your leader commands something you dislike but it is not disbelief, this passage requires obedience. Where in your life have you struggled to obey a legitimate authority because it conflicted with your own desires? How does this lesson call you to surrender your will to Allah's law even when it is difficult?
?
This teaching says removal of the imam is not allowed even if he loses his qualifications—only clear apostasy justifies it. Does your heart chafe against this? Are you willing to trust Allah to deal with unjust rulers, or do you seek a different solution? What concrete step can you take today to align your attitude with this teaching?

Emulating the Righteous and Avoiding Innovation
The poet encourages emulating the righteous predecessors and warns against innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that all good lies in following the Salaf and all evil in innovation (bid‘ah).

Core Idea
Follow the Salaf; avoid every innovation.

This lesson presents a foundational couplet from the poem that encapsulates the Sunni approach to religious guidance: look to the early generations. The poet directs us to be like the best of creation, the Prophet and his Companions, and warns that every good is in their path, while every evil stems from later innovations. Let's unpack this powerful principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the couplet and translation
Read the Arabic couplet slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the contrasting structure: 'every good' vs. 'every evil'.
2
Understand the model: 'like the best of creation'
Identify what the poet says about the best of creation: they were 'allies of forbearance' and 'followers of truth'. This sets the standard for emulation.
3
Grasp the principle: good in following, evil in innovation
The second line states a binary: all good is in 'following those who passed' (Salaf) and all evil in 'innovating of those who came after' (Khalaf). Reflect on the absolute nature of this claim.
4
Consider the theological implication
Ask yourself why this principle is central: it anchors religious authority in the early community and rejects later deviations.
This connects to the common misunderstanding about 'good innovation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُنْ كَمَا كَانَ خيارُ الخلقِ حَلِيفَ حِلْمٍ تابعا للحقِّ

Commentary
This passage sets a model: be like the best of creation (the Prophet and Companions) in being an ally of forbearance (حليف حلم) and a follower of truth. Then it establishes a principle: all good is in following the Salaf (those who passed), and all evil is in the innovations of the Khalaf (those who came later). This is a foundational statement in Sunni theology regarding the authority of the early generations.

Key Terms

ابتداع من خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe there can be 'good innovation' (bid‘a hasana) in religion.
Correction: The poem states flatly that every evil is in innovation, leaving no room for a 'good' category. Sunni orthodoxy generally holds that all religious innovations are misguidance, though some later scholars distinguished between linguistic and religious innovation.

?

How does that lack of forbearance disconnect you from the character of the best of creation? What specific step will you take today to become more like them in that area?

Following the Prophet and Avoiding Innovation
The poet commands following the Prophet's guidance and avoiding innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the imperative to follow the Prophet's guidance, adhere to what is permitted, and avoid innovation by following the righteous Salaf.

Core Idea
The Prophet's guidance is supreme; do the permitted, leave the rest.

This lesson presents a concise poetic command from the matn, emphasizing the centrality of the Prophet's guidance and the importance of distinguishing between permitted and prohibited actions. It also underscores the duty to follow the righteous predecessors (Salaf) and steer clear of innovation (bid‘ah). These principles form a foundational attitude for the believer in matters of creed and practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic lines
Read the two lines of poetry carefully, noticing the rhyme and rhythm. Then read the literal translation to grasp the plain meaning.
The Arabic uses imperative verbs: افعل (do) and دع (leave).
2
Identify the two commands
The first line commands doing what is permitted and leaving what is not. The second line commands following the righteous predecessors and avoiding innovation. Note the parallelism.
The second line pairs 'follow the righteous' with 'avoid innovation'.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'كل هدي'
The phrase 'every guidance of the Prophet' asserts that all prophetic guidance is preponderant. Consider why this is the foundation for the commands that follow.
This establishes the authority of the Sunnah.
4
Apply the teaching
Think about how you can implement this in your own life: seeking prophetic guidance, checking permissibility, and being cautious about innovations.
This connects creed to daily action.

Then it reiterates the command to follow the righteous Salaf and avoid the innovations of the Khalaf. This emphasizes the authority of the Sunnah and the Salaf.

Definition and Scope of Ahl al-Sunnah
Commentator defines Ahl al-Sunnah and clarifies its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Ahl al-Sunnah, its inclusive scope covering Ash'aris and Maturidis, and the meaning of Sunnah according to the early scholars.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah are followers of the Prophet's way, including both Ash'aris and Maturidis.

The commentator begins his work by defining the term 'Ahl al-Sunnah,' establishing it as the central identity of the orthodox community. He clarifies that it refers to those who strictly follow the Prophet's path without innovation, and importantly, it encompasses both the Ash'ari and Maturidi theological schools. This passage sets the stage for the rest of the commentary by affirming an inclusive understanding of Sunnism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the commentator unpacks the term 'Ahl al-Sunnah' step by step.
Pay attention to the phrase 'والمراد بأهل السنة: ما يشمل الأشاعرة والماتريدية'.
2
Identify the definition of Ahl al-Sunnah
Find the part where the commentator defines Ahl al-Sunnah as those who followed the Prophet and did not introduce innovations. Note that deviant sects are excluded.
The definition is at the beginning.
3
Note the inclusive scope
The commentator explicitly states that Ahl al-Sunnah includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis. This is a key point against narrow interpretations.
This is a major takeaway for understanding Sunni identity.
4
Understand the definition of Sunnah
The commentator defines Sunnah as the way of the Prophet, based on the Quran and hadith, and mentions the hadith 'His character was the Quran'.
Sunnah is not merely custom but divinely guided practice.
5
Examine the rebuttal of a misconception
Read the part where the commentator refutes the idea that Ahl al-Sunnah avoided calling themselves 'Ahl al-Kitab' to avoid confusion with Jews and Christians.
This clarifies the reasoning behind the name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (الأهل السنة) أي: أصحاب طريقة النبي الله، وهم الذين اتبعوه ولم يُحدثوا ما يخالفه، وخرجت فرق الضلال، والمراد بأهل السنة: ما يشمل الأشاعرة والماتريدية؛ وإن كانت المقدمة موضوعة على مُختار الأولى، وفي ذكره لأهل السنة في المقدمة براعة استهلال، والسنة: طريقة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكان كما في الحديث [صحيح مسلم ٧٤٦] «خُلقه القرآن» وهي التي كان عليها السلف الصالح استندت لكتاب أو حديث؛ فليس المراد بها ما قابل الكتاب حتى يُحتاج لما نقله شيخنا العدوي عن المؤلف في حاشيته مِن أنهم سموا أهل سنةٍ ولم يسموا أهل كتاب مع استنادهم لكل لإيهام اليهود والنصارى؛ فإنهم اشتهروا بأهل الكتاب. الأمير والشنواني.
His saying: (Ahl al-Sunnah) meaning: the companions of the way of the Prophet of Allah, and they are those who followed him and did not introduce anything contradicting him, and the deviant sects are excluded. And what is meant by Ahl al-Sunnah: that which includes the Ash'aris and the Maturidis; even though the introduction is placed according to the preferred of the two, and in his mention of Ahl al-Sunnah in the introduction there is a felicitous opening (bara'at istihlal). And al-Sunnah: the way of Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and it was as in the hadith [Sahih Muslim 746] 'His character was the Quran,' and it is that which the righteous predecessors (al-salaf al-salih) were upon, based on a Book or a hadith; so it is not meant by it that which is opposite to the Book such that it would be needed for what our shaykh al-'Adawi transmitted from the author in his gloss that they called themselves 'People of Sunnah' and did not call themselves 'People of the Book' despite their reliance on each, to mislead the Jews and Christians; for they are famous as 'People of the Book.' Al-Amir and al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'Ahl al-Sunnah' as used by the author. The commentator begins by stating that Ahl al-Sunnah are the companions of the Prophet's way, those who followed him and did not introduce innovations. He then explicitly states that the term includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis, noting that the introduction is based on the preferred view. He mentions the literary device 'bara'at istihlal' in mentioning Ahl al-Sunnah at the start. Next, he defines al-Sunnah as the Prophet's way, which is embodied by his character being the Quran, and that the righteous predecessors relied on the Book and hadith. He addresses a potential misunderstanding: that Ahl al-Sunnah is not a term opposite to 'Ahl al-Kitab,' refuting the idea that they avoided calling themselves 'Ahl al-Kitab' to avoid confusion with Jews and Christians, since the latter are known as 'People of the Book.' The footnote ends with citations to al-Amir and al-Shanwani.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that Ahl al-Sunnah includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis, and that Sunnah is based on the Quran and hadith, not opposed to the Book.

Defining Innovation: The Salaf's Methodology
Defines innovation and gives an example from Imam Malik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of innovation (bid'a) in theological discussions and how the early scholars (salaf) approached issues like the meaning of Allah's attributes without speculative inquiry.

Core Idea
Innovation in theology is asking or asserting what the salaf did not ask or assert.

When new questions arose that had not been asked by the early Muslims, scholars had to define what constitutes innovation. This lesson examines Imam Malik's famous response to a question about Allah's 'istiwa' and clarifies the salaf's principle of avoiding unnecessary speculation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the definition of bid'a and the Imam Malik example.
2
Understand the definition of bid'a
The passage defines innovation as bringing something without precedent. Note that this includes new theological questions not raised by the salaf.
3
Analyze Imam Malik's response
He says: al-istiwa' is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation. Identify these three components.
4
Reflect on the salaf's principle
The salaf did not ask about the modality of attributes; they entrusted knowledge to Allah. This is the basis for calling the question an innovation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما احتاج إلى البيان ؛ لأنَّ كلامَ الأوائل كانَ مقصورًا على الذَّات ، والصفات ، والنُّبوَّات ، والسَّمعيَّات، فلما حدثت المُبتدعةُ ، وكثر جدالهم معَ علماء الإسلام ، وأوردوا شُبها على ما قرَّرَهُ عنه . الابتداع: الإتيان بالشيء الذي لم يسبق نظيره ، والمُبْتَدِع الآتي بذلك، ولما كان السلف الصَّالح على المنهج الذي ذكره الشارح كان الذين جاؤوا بعدهم يتتبعون الشُّبه ويثيرونها ويخلطونها بقواعد الفلسفة مبتدعين بمعنى أنهم أتون بما لم يسبق في محاورات السلف مثله ، أنَّ رجلا سأل الإمام مالك بن أنس عن قوله تعالى: (الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه: ه ] ، فقال : الاستواء معلوم، والكيف مجهول ، والسُّؤال عنه بدعة ، أخرجوا عنّي هذا المبتدع ، فذكرَ أنَّ السُّؤال عن معنى الاستواء بدعة ، وذلك لأنَّ السَّلف ما كانوا يسألون ال بل كانوا يفوضون العلم فيه إلى الله تعالى، وذكر أنَّ السائل مبتدع ؛ لأنه آت بما لم يكن السلف يأتون به ، وحكى العلامة سعد الدين التفتازاني أنَّ أول من أظهر الخلاف هو واصل عطاء الذي صار رئيس المعتزلة من أهل طبقته ، وكان واصل في مجلس الحسن البصري، فسأل رجل الحسنَ قائلا: يا إمام الدِّين، زعم أناس أنَّ مَن فعل كبيرة من الكبائر فقد كفر ، وقال آخرون لا تضرُّ مع الإيمان معصية أصلا ، كما لا تنفع مع الكفر طاعة أصلا ، فما الحق في ذلك ؟ فأطرق الحسن البصري يفكّر في المسألة ، وسارع واصل فأثبت منزلة بين الكفر والإيمان، وقال: النَّاس ثلاثة أقسام مؤمنٌ ، وكافرُ ، ولا مؤمن ولا كافر، ثم اعتزل مجلس الحسن وعقد لنفسه مجلسا يقرر فيه مذهبه ، فقال الحسن اعتَزَلَنا واصل. ومن ذلك اليوم سمي هو وأصحابه الذين اعتزلوا معه مجلس شيخهم الحسن البصري (المعتزلة) ، ثم تفاقم الخطب ، وتعاظم الأمر لما أمر المأمونُ الخليفة العباسي بتعريب كتب الفلسفة اليونانية ؛ فإنَّ المعتزلة ترسموا خطوات الفلاسفة، وتتبَّعوا قواعدهم وانتحلوا الكثير منها، وطبقوه على المسائل الكلامية ؛ ألا تری أنَّ هؤلاء المعتزلة إنما نفوا صِفاتِ المعاني تطبيقا لقاعدة من قواعد الفلسفة اليونانية، وهي: أنَّ الرؤية لا تكون إلا بأشعة تتصل بالمُبْصَر وقالوا: إِنَّ الله تعالى لا يرى يوم القيامة ، وقدموا القاعدة الفلسفية على الآية الكريمة: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذِ نَاضِرَة إلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ) [القيامة ] ، وهكذا مما ستطلع عليه في مسائل هذا العلم. محي.
And he only needed clarification because the speech of the early [scholars] was confined to the Essence, the Attributes, prophethoods, and sam'iyyat (audible matters). Then when the innovators (mubtadi'a) appeared, and their argumentation with the scholars of Islam increased, and they brought forward doubts against what he had established from him. Al-ibtida' (innovation) is bringing something that has no precedent similar to it, and al-mubtadi' (innovator) is the one who does that. And since the righteous predecessors (salaf) were upon the methodology that the commentator mentioned, those who came after them would pursue doubts and raise them and mix them with the rules of philosophy, innovating in the sense that they brought what had no precedent similar to it in the discourses of the salaf. Indeed, a man asked Imam Malik ibn Anas about the saying of Allah Most High: 'The Most Merciful, upon the Throne He established' [Ta Ha: 5], so he said: 'The establishing (al-istiwā') is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation; take this innovator away from me.' So he mentioned that asking about the meaning of al-istiwā' is an innovation, and that is because the salaf did not ask about it; rather they entrusted knowledge of it to Allah Most High. And he mentioned that the asker is an innovator because he brought what the salaf did not bring. And the scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani narrated that the first to manifest opposition was Wasil ibn 'Ata', who became the leader of the Mu'tazila among his contemporaries. Wasil was in the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri, so a man asked al-Hasan saying: 'O Imam of the religion, some people claim that whoever commits a major sin has disbelieved, and others say that no disobedience harms with faith, just as no obedience benefits with disbelief. So what is the truth in that?' So al-Hasan al-Basri bowed his head thinking about the matter, and Wasil hurried and affirmed a station between disbelief and faith, and said: 'People are three categories: believer, disbeliever, and neither believer nor disbeliever.' Then he withdrew from al-Hasan's circle and held his own circle to establish his doctrine, so al-Hasan said: 'Wasil has separated from us.' From that day, he and his companions who withdrew with him from the circle of their teacher al-Hasan al-Basri were called 'al-Mu'tazila'. Then the matter became worse and greater when al-Ma'mun, the Abbasid caliph, ordered the translation of books of Greek philosophy. For the Mu'tazila followed the steps of the philosophers, pursued their rules, adopted much of them, and applied them to kalam issues. Do you not see that these Mu'tazila negated the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) applying a rule from Greek philosophy: that vision (al-ru'ya) only occurs through rays connecting with the visible? And they said that Allah Most High will not be seen on the Day of Resurrection. They prioritized the philosophical rule over the noble verse: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyama: 22-23]. And so on with what you will encounter in the issues of this science. Mayh.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage explains why the science of usul al-din (principles of religion) required detailed clarification (al-bayan). The commentator begins by noting that the early scholars (al-awā'il) confined their discourse to the core topics: the Divine Essence, Attributes, prophethoods, and sam'iyyat (matters heard from revelation, like the afterlife). However, innovators (mubtadi'a) appeared, multiplied their arguments with Muslim scholars, and raised doubts against the established creed. The commentator then defines innovation (al-ibtida') as bringing something without precedent. He cites the example of a man asking Imam Malik about the meaning of Allah's istiwā' (establishment upon the Throne). Imam Malik's response—'the istiwā' is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is innovation'—illustrates the salaf's approach of avoiding speculative inquiry. The commentator then narrates a historical account from al-Taftazani about the origin of the Mu'tazila. Wasil ibn 'Ata', a student of al-Hasan al-Basri, introduced a third category (the 'intermediate position') regarding grave sinners, effectively splitting from his teacher. This was the beginning of the Mu'tazila. The crisis deepened when the Abbasid caliph al-Ma'mun ordered the translation of Greek philosophy. The Mu'tazila adopted philosophical rules—such as that vision requires rays connecting with the visible object—and applied them to kalam, denying that Allah will be seen on the Day of Resurrection, contradicting the Quranic verse. This historical development necessitated a more sophisticated science of kalam to respond to these innovative arguments. The commentator ends by noting that such issues will be encountered in the course of this science.

Key Terms
المبتدعة
البدعة
السلف
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think innovation only refers to new acts of worship, not theological questions.
Correction: The passage shows that asking about the modality of an attribute, which the salaf did not ask, is also considered innovation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Imam Malik call the asker an innovator?
A) Because he asked about the meaning of istiwa'
B) Because he brought up a question that the salaf did not ask
C) Because he doubted the attribute
D) Because he was from the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Innovation in theological discussion includes asking questions that the early Muslims did not ask, as exemplified by Imam Malik's response to the question about Allah's istiwa'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself insisting on knowing the 'how' of an attribute of Allah, despite knowing that the salaf did not ask? What does that reveal about your heart's submission to revelation?
?
When you hear a scholar say, 'asking that question is an innovation,' does your soul immediately accept it in humility, or does it rebel with the desire for explanation? How can you train yourself to be like Imam Malik's student?
?
What questions do you ask about Islam that have no precedent among the salaf? Are you willing to abandon them for the sake of following their path, even if they seem beneficial?

The Rise of the Mu'tazila and the Impact of Greek Philosophy
Narrates the origin of Mu'tazila and their adoption of Greek philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical emergence of the Mu'tazila through Wasil ibn 'Ata's split from al-Hasan al-Basri and how the translation of Greek philosophy led them to adopt rationalist positions such as denying the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila began with a theological dispute over grave sinners and later adopted Greek philosophical methods to challenge traditional beliefs.

The first major theological sect in Islam, the Mu'tazila, originated in the study circle of al-Hasan al-Basri. This lesson traces their origin through a dispute about the status of grave sinners and shows how later exposure to Greek philosophy led them to deny the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the story of Wasil and the later philosophical influence.
2
Understand the initial dispute
The question about the grave sinner's status and Wasil's innovation of a middle station (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn).
3
Trace the naming of Mu'tazila
Al-Hasan's statement 'Wasil has separated from us' (i'tazala) and how the group got its name.
4
Examine the role of Greek philosophy
How al-Ma'mun's translation movement provided philosophical rules that the Mu'tazila applied, especially the denial of vision of Allah.
5
Reflect on the method
They prioritized philosophical reasoning over explicit Quranic verses, as seen in the verse about seeing Allah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما احتاج إلى البيان ؛ لأنَّ كلامَ الأوائل كانَ مقصورًا على الذَّات ، والصفات ، والنُّبوَّات ، والسَّمعيَّات، فلما حدثت المُبتدعةُ ، وكثر جدالهم معَ علماء الإسلام ، وأوردوا شُبها على ما قرَّرَهُ عنه . الابتداع: الإتيان بالشيء الذي لم يسبق نظيره ، والمُبْتَدِع الآتي بذلك، ولما كان السلف الصَّالح على المنهج الذي ذكره الشارح كان الذين جاؤوا بعدهم يتتبعون الشُّبه ويثيرونها ويخلطونها بقواعد الفلسفة مبتدعين بمعنى أنهم أتون بما لم يسبق في محاورات السلف مثله ، أنَّ رجلا سأل الإمام مالك بن أنس عن قوله تعالى: (الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه: ه ] ، فقال : الاستواء معلوم، والكيف مجهول ، والسُّؤال عنه بدعة ، أخرجوا عنّي هذا المبتدع ، فذكرَ أنَّ السُّؤال عن معنى الاستواء بدعة ، وذلك لأنَّ السَّلف ما كانوا يسألون ال بل كانوا يفوضون العلم فيه إلى الله تعالى، وذكر أنَّ السائل مبتدع ؛ لأنه آت بما لم يكن السلف يأتون به ، وحكى العلامة سعد الدين التفتازاني أنَّ أول من أظهر الخلاف هو واصل عطاء الذي صار رئيس المعتزلة من أهل طبقته ، وكان واصل في مجلس الحسن البصري، فسأل رجل الحسنَ قائلا: يا إمام الدِّين، زعم أناس أنَّ مَن فعل كبيرة من الكبائر فقد كفر ، وقال آخرون لا تضرُّ مع الإيمان معصية أصلا ، كما لا تنفع مع الكفر طاعة أصلا ، فما الحق في ذلك ؟ فأطرق الحسن البصري يفكّر في المسألة ، وسارع واصل فأثبت منزلة بين الكفر والإيمان، وقال: النَّاس ثلاثة أقسام مؤمنٌ ، وكافرُ ، ولا مؤمن ولا كافر، ثم اعتزل مجلس الحسن وعقد لنفسه مجلسا يقرر فيه مذهبه ، فقال الحسن اعتَزَلَنا واصل. ومن ذلك اليوم سمي هو وأصحابه الذين اعتزلوا معه مجلس شيخهم الحسن البصري (المعتزلة) ، ثم تفاقم الخطب ، وتعاظم الأمر لما أمر المأمونُ الخليفة العباسي بتعريب كتب الفلسفة اليونانية ؛ فإنَّ المعتزلة ترسموا خطوات الفلاسفة، وتتبَّعوا قواعدهم وانتحلوا الكثير منها، وطبقوه على المسائل الكلامية ؛ ألا تری أنَّ هؤلاء المعتزلة إنما نفوا صِفاتِ المعاني تطبيقا لقاعدة من قواعد الفلسفة اليونانية، وهي: أنَّ الرؤية لا تكون إلا بأشعة تتصل بالمُبْصَر وقالوا: إِنَّ الله تعالى لا يرى يوم القيامة ، وقدموا القاعدة الفلسفية على الآية الكريمة: وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذِ نَاضِرَة إلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ) [القيامة ] ، وهكذا مما ستطلع عليه في مسائل هذا العلم. محي.
And he only needed clarification because the speech of the early [scholars] was confined to the Essence, the Attributes, prophethoods, and sam'iyyat (audible matters). Then when the innovators (mubtadi'a) appeared, and their argumentation with the scholars of Islam increased, and they brought forward doubts against what he had established from him. Al-ibtida' (innovation) is bringing something that has no precedent similar to it, and al-mubtadi' (innovator) is the one who does that. And since the righteous predecessors (salaf) were upon the methodology that the commentator mentioned, those who came after them would pursue doubts and raise them and mix them with the rules of philosophy, innovating in the sense that they brought what had no precedent similar to it in the discourses of the salaf. Indeed, a man asked Imam Malik ibn Anas about the saying of Allah Most High: 'The Most Merciful, upon the Throne He established' [Ta Ha: 5], so he said: 'The establishing (al-istiwā') is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation; take this innovator away from me.' So he mentioned that asking about the meaning of al-istiwā' is an innovation, and that is because the salaf did not ask about it; rather they entrusted knowledge of it to Allah Most High. And he mentioned that the asker is an innovator because he brought what the salaf did not bring. And the scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani narrated that the first to manifest opposition was Wasil ibn 'Ata', who became the leader of the Mu'tazila among his contemporaries. Wasil was in the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri, so a man asked al-Hasan saying: 'O Imam of the religion, some people claim that whoever commits a major sin has disbelieved, and others say that no disobedience harms with faith, just as no obedience benefits with disbelief. So what is the truth in that?' So al-Hasan al-Basri bowed his head thinking about the matter, and Wasil hurried and affirmed a station between disbelief and faith, and said: 'People are three categories: believer, disbeliever, and neither believer nor disbeliever.' Then he withdrew from al-Hasan's circle and held his own circle to establish his doctrine, so al-Hasan said: 'Wasil has separated from us.' From that day, he and his companions who withdrew with him from the circle of their teacher al-Hasan al-Basri were called 'al-Mu'tazila'. Then the matter became worse and greater when al-Ma'mun, the Abbasid caliph, ordered the translation of books of Greek philosophy. For the Mu'tazila followed the steps of the philosophers, pursued their rules, adopted much of them, and applied them to kalam issues. Do you not see that these Mu'tazila negated the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) applying a rule from Greek philosophy: that vision (al-ru'ya) only occurs through rays connecting with the visible? And they said that Allah Most High will not be seen on the Day of Resurrection. They prioritized the philosophical rule over the noble verse: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyama: 22-23]. And so on with what you will encounter in the issues of this science. Mayh.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage explains why the science of usul al-din (principles of religion) required detailed clarification (al-bayan). The commentator begins by noting that the early scholars (al-awā'il) confined their discourse to the core topics: the Divine Essence, Attributes, prophethoods, and sam'iyyat (matters heard from revelation, like the afterlife). However, innovators (mubtadi'a) appeared, multiplied their arguments with Muslim scholars, and raised doubts against the established creed. The commentator then defines innovation (al-ibtida') as bringing something without precedent. He cites the example of a man asking Imam Malik about the meaning of Allah's istiwā' (establishment upon the Throne). Imam Malik's response—'the istiwā' is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is innovation'—illustrates the salaf's approach of avoiding speculative inquiry. The commentator then narrates a historical account from al-Taftazani about the origin of the Mu'tazila. Wasil ibn 'Ata', a student of al-Hasan al-Basri, introduced a third category (the 'intermediate position') regarding grave sinners, effectively splitting from his teacher. This was the beginning of the Mu'tazila. The crisis deepened when the Abbasid caliph al-Ma'mun ordered the translation of Greek philosophy. The Mu'tazila adopted philosophical rules—such as that vision requires rays connecting with the visible object—and applied them to kalam, denying that Allah will be seen on the Day of Resurrection, contradicting the Quranic verse. This historical development necessitated a more sophisticated science of kalam to respond to these innovative arguments. The commentator ends by noting that such issues will be encountered in the course of this science.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
واصل بن عطاء
الحسن البصري
صفات المعاني
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila emerged solely due to political reasons.
Correction: The passage shows they emerged from a theological dispute and later adopted philosophical methods.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the immediate cause for Wasil ibn 'Ata's separation from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle?
A) Disagreement over the vision of Allah
B) His answer about the status of a grave sinner
C) The translation of Greek philosophy
D) A question about Allah's attributes

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila originated from a dispute over grave sinners and later adopted Greek philosophical reasoning, leading them to deny the vision of Allah and other orthodox beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Why do I sometimes trust my own reasoning over a clear command from Allah and His Messenger? Can I think of a specific area in my life where I am rationalizing a disobedience?
?
What would it take for me to genuinely submit to a verse of the Quran that challenges my habits or worldview, even if I don't fully understand it?
?
If I were in al-Hasan al-Basri's circle when Wasil proposed his new view, would I have had the courage to remain patient and seek the truth, or would I have rushed to an answer to appear clever?

Azar: The Uncle of Abraham, Not His Father
Clarification that Azar was Abraham's uncle, not father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Azar was not Abraham's biological father but his uncle, and the use of 'father' was an Arab custom.

Core Idea
Azar was Abraham's uncle, called 'father' by custom, not biology.

The passage begins by clarifying a common confusion about the relationship between Abraham and Azar. It states that Azar was actually his uncle, but the term 'father' was used according to Arab cultural norms. This correction is important because some weak reports claim otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's claim settle.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the author asserts Azar was Abraham's uncle (عم) and not his biological father.
3
Understand the justification
The term 'father' was used as a cultural practice among the Arabs. This is why Abraham called him 'father' in the Quran.
4
Recognize the dismissal of other reports
Any report suggesting otherwise is weak or abrogated, and should not be relied upon.
5
Conclude the correct belief
This is the truth with which we meet Allah, as the author emphasizes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما آزر فكان عم إبراهيم، وإنما دعاه بالأب على عادة العرب ولا يعول على غيرها ولا يلتفت إليه أصلا لضعفه أو نسخه ، هذا هو الحق الذي نلقى الله به. وأما ما نقل عن أبي حنفية في الفقه الأكبر من أن والدي المصطفى كانا كذلك فهو مكذوب عليه ، وحاشاه أن يقول في والدي المصطفى ذلك على الإطلاق، فهو مدسوس عليه بفرض «الفقه الأكبر» له ، وأما على أنه ليس له وإنما نسب إليه فلا يحتاج للجواب عنه، أو يقال إنهما ماتا في زمن الكفر بمعنى الجاهلية وإن كانا ناجيين ، وغلط ملّا علي القاري يغفر له في كلمة شنيعة قالها ، ومن العجائب ما نُسب له مع ذلك من إيمان فرعون، وأما ما ورد من نهي الله عن استغفاره لهما فمحمول على أنه قبل إخباره بحالهما، أو لئلا تقتدي به أولاد الكفار الماضين، والحاصل أن أهل الفترة فيهم ثلاثة أقوال: أولها: وهو الصحيح أنهم لا يعذبون وإن غيروا وبدلوا أو عبدوا الأصنام.
As for Azar, he was the uncle of Abraham; he only called him 'father' according to the Arab custom; no attention is paid to any other (report) at all due to its weakness or abrogation. This is the truth with which we meet Allah. As for what is transmitted from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the parents of the Chosen One were like that (i.e., disbelievers or punished) it is a forgery against him. Far be it from him to say such about the parents of the Chosen One absolutely. It is interpolated upon him under the assumption that al-Fiqh al-Akbar is his. And as for it not being his, rather being attributed to him, there is no need for a response. Or it is said: they died in a time of disbelief in the sense of Jahiliyyah, even though they are saved. The error of Mulla Ali al-Qari—may Allah forgive him—in an ugly word he said, and among the wonders is what is attributed to him of the faith of Pharaoh. And as for what came of Allah's prohibition of seeking forgiveness for them, it is interpreted as being before He informed (the Prophet) of their state, or so that the children of past disbelievers do not follow suit. The conclusion is that concerning ahl al-fatrah, there are three opinions: The first, which is correct: they are not punished even if they changed, altered, or worshipped idols.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct belief about the Prophet's parents. It first clarifies that Azar was Abraham's uncle, not father, and calling him 'father' was a custom. Then it addresses a false claim attributed to Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the Prophet's parents were disbelievers. The author vehemently denies this, stating the attribution is a forgery; Abu Hanifa would never say such a thing. Alternatively, if the book is misattributed, no response is needed. Another possibility is that they died in the time of 'disbelief' meaning Jahiliyyah, but they are saved. The author criticizes Mulla Ali al-Qari for a shameful statement, and also refutes the idea that Pharaoh believed. The prohibition of seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents is explained: it was before the Prophet knew their final state, or to prevent others from following. The passage ends by restating the first opinion on ahl al-fatrah: they are not punished.

Key Terms
آزر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Azar was Abraham's biological father.
Correction: He was his uncle; 'father' was used as a cultural term.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was Azar's relationship to Abraham?
A) Father
B) Uncle
C) Brother
D) Cousin

Key Takeaway
Azar is established as Abraham's uncle, and the use of 'father' is a linguistic custom, not a biological fact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says this is 'the truth with which we meet Allah.' Do you hold any beliefs about the prophets with such certainty? What would it take for you to revise a belief if you found it was based on weak reports?
?
In this lesson, a single linguistic custom changes the understanding of a family relationship. What other areas of your understanding of Islam might be influenced by cultural customs rather than literal meanings? How will you investigate?
?
You may have heard a different version of this story. When you encounter conflicting religious narratives, do you check the strength of the evidence? Will you commit to verifying reports before accepting them as truth?

The Forgery Against Abu Hanifa About the Prophet's Parents
Refutation of the false attribution to Abu Hanifa about the Prophet's parents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the claim that the Prophet's parents were disbelievers, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is a forgery and that Abu Hanifa is innocent of such a statement.

Core Idea
Attribution of disbelief to Prophet's parents is a forgery against Abu Hanifa.

The author now addresses a serious false claim that has been attributed to the great imam Abu Hanifa. Some people have claimed that in his book al-Fiqh al-Akbar, he stated that the Prophet's parents died in disbelief. The author vehemently denies this, calling it a forgery and explaining why Abu Hanifa would never say such a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the claim
Read the Arabic segment about the alleged statement from al-Fiqh al-Akbar, then its translation.
2
Understand the author's stance
The author declares the statement a forgery (مكذوب عليه) and denies that Abu Hanifa could ever say such a thing.
3
Consider the alternative possibilities
The author offers two options: either the book is misattributed to Abu Hanifa, or the parents died in a state of 'disbelief' meaning Jahiliyyah but are saved.
4
Note the critique of Mulla Ali al-Qari
The author criticizes Mulla Ali al-Qari for a shameful statement regarding this issue.

As for Azar, he was the uncle of Abraham; he only called him 'father' according to the Arab custom; no attention is paid to any other (report) at all due to its weakness or abrogation. And as for it not being his, rather being attributed to him, there is no need for a response. Or it is said: they died in a time of disbelief in the sense of Jahiliyyah, even though they are saved. The error of Mulla Ali al-Qari—may Allah forgive him—in an ugly word he said, and among the wonders is what is attributed to him of the faith of Pharaoh. And as for what came of Allah's prohibition of seeking forgiveness for them, it is interpreted as being before He informed (the Prophet) of their state, or so that the children of past disbelievers do not follow suit. The conclusion is that concerning ahl al-fatrah, there are three opinions: The first, which is correct: they are not punished even if they changed, altered, or worshipped idols.

The Prohibition of Seeking Forgiveness and the Salvation of Ahl al-Fatrah
Interpretation of prohibition and correct opinion on ahl al-fatrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation of the prohibition on seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents and the three opinions on the state of ahl al-fatrah, with the correct view that they are not punished.

Core Idea
Prohibition interpreted; ahl al-fatrah are not punished.

The passage now moves to a related point: why did Allah command the Prophet not to seek forgiveness for his parents? The author offers two interpretations: either it was before the Prophet knew their state, or to prevent others from emulating. This leads to the general discussion of ahl al-fatrah, people who lived in a period without prophetic guidance. The author presents three opinions and affirms that the correct view is that they are not punished.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the prohibition
Read the Arabic and translation about the prohibition of seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The prohibition is interpreted as being before the Prophet learned about their final state, or to prevent children of past disbelievers from following suit.
3
Note the transition to ahl al-fatrah
The author generalizes to the category of ahl al-fatrah, those who lived in a period without prophetic guidance.
4
Recognize the three opinions
There are three opinions regarding ahl al-fatrah. The passage mentions the first and correct opinion: they are not punished even if they worshipped idols.
The other two opinions are not detailed here.

Source Text

As for Azar, he was the uncle of Abraham; he only called him 'father' according to the Arab custom; no attention is paid to any other (report) at all due to its weakness or abrogation. This is the truth with which we meet Allah. As for what is transmitted from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the parents of the Chosen One were like that (i.e., disbelievers or punished) it is a forgery against him. Far be it from him to say such about the parents of the Chosen One absolutely. It is interpolated upon him under the assumption that al-Fiqh al-Akbar is his. And as for it not being his, rather being attributed to him, there is no need for a response. Or it is said: they died in a time of disbelief in the sense of Jahiliyyah, even though they are saved. The error of Mulla Ali al-Qari—may Allah forgive him—in an ugly word he said, and among the wonders is what is attributed to him of the faith of Pharaoh. And as for what came of Allah's prohibition of seeking forgiveness for them, it is interpreted as being before He informed (the Prophet) of their state, or so that the children of past disbelievers do not follow suit. The conclusion is that concerning ahl al-fatrah, there are three opinions: The first, which is correct: they are not punished even if they changed, altered, or worshipped idols.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct belief about the Prophet's parents. It first clarifies that Azar was Abraham's uncle, not father, and calling him 'father' was a custom. Then it addresses a false claim attributed to Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the Prophet's parents were disbelievers. The author vehemently denies this, stating the attribution is a forgery; Abu Hanifa would never say such a thing. Alternatively, if the book is misattributed, no response is needed. Another possibility is that they died in the time of 'disbelief' meaning Jahiliyyah, but they are saved. The author criticizes Mulla Ali al-Qari for a shameful statement, and also refutes the idea that Pharaoh believed. The prohibition of seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents is explained: it was before the Prophet knew their final state, or to prevent others from following. The passage ends by restating the first opinion on ahl al-fatrah: they are not punished.

Key Terms
آزر
أبو حنيفة
الفقه الأكبر
والدي النبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prohibition of seeking forgiveness means the Prophet's parents were disbelievers and punished.
Correction: The prohibition is interpreted as either before knowledge of their state or to prevent emulation; they are saved.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one explanation for Allah's prohibition of seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents?
A) They were disbelievers
B) It was before the Prophet knew their state
C) The Prophet disobeyed
D) It is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The prohibition on seeking forgiveness for the Prophet's parents is interpreted, and ahl al-fatrah are not punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received clear guidance through the Prophet ﷺ and the Quran. Are you taking that guidance seriously, or are you living as if you are like ahl al-fatrah, without excuse? What is one area where you need to align your life more closely with what you know?
?
The Prophet was prohibited from seeking forgiveness for his parents despite his love for them. What does that tell you about submitting to Allah's commands even when they conflict with your emotions? Is there something in your life where you are prioritizing your feelings over what Allah has commanded?
?
Ahl al-fatrah are excused due to lack of prophets. You have no such excuse. What action will you take today to act on the knowledge you have been given, ensuring you are not among those who neglect the guidance available to them?

Two Positions: Nutq as Condition or Part of Iman
Enumeration of the two positions: nutq as condition or as part of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on whether verbal attestation (nutq) is a condition or a part of iman, and the practical implication of the first position for applying worldly Islamic rulings.

Core Idea
The status of your testimony of faith determines the worldly rulings applied to you.

A foundational debate in Islamic theology concerns the role of the tongue's utterance (nutq) in faith. Does it merely unlock the worldly rulings for believers, or is it an actual component of iman? This lesson presents the two major positions from the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools as explained by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice that the poetic line mentions two views, but the commentary only expands on the first.
The second view (nutq as part) is not detailed in this passage.
2
Identify the two positions
From the poetic line find the words 'shart' (condition) and 'shatr' (part). These are the two opposing views on nutq in relation to iman.
3
Understand the first position
The commentary explains that the verifiers among Ash'aris and Maturidis hold that nutq from a capable person is a condition (shart) for applying the worldly rulings of believers (ahkam al-mu'minin al-dunyawiyya).
This means without nutq, a person is not treated as a believer in this world (e.g., regarding marriage, inheritance, funeral).
4
Reflect on the second position
The poetic line also mentions that some say nutq is a part (shatr) of iman. While not elaborated here, this sets up a theological difference: is nutq essential (part) or merely external (condition)?
This debate has implications for the definition of iman itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقِيْلَ شَرْطُ كَالعَمَلْ وَقِيلَ بَلْ شَطْرُ والإِسْلَامَ اشْرَحَنَّ بِالعَمَلْ وفصَّلَ الخِلافَ بقوله : (فَقِيلَ) أي: فقال مُحققو الأشاعرة والماتريدية وغيرهم: النطق من القادر (شَرْطٌ) فى إجراء أحكام المؤمنين الدنيوية
So it was said: a condition like action, and it was said: rather a part, and Islam — explain by action. And he detailed the disagreement with his saying: (so it was said) i.e., the verifiers among the Ash'aris, Maturidis, and others said: the utterance from the capable is a condition in applying the worldly rulings of the believers.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the two main positions on the status of verbal attestation (nutq) in relation to iman. The poetic line states that some say it is a condition (shart) like action, and others say it is a part (shatr) of iman. The commentator explains that the first group — the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among the Ash'aris, Maturidis, and others — hold that nutq from a capable person is a condition for applying the worldly Islamic rulings (ahkam al-mu'minin al-dunyawiyya) upon him.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that nutq (verbal attestation) is not required at all for iman, or that it is only a recommended practice.
Correction: The passage shows that, at minimum, nutq is a condition (shart) for applying worldly Islamic rulings. Without it, a capable person is not treated as a believer in this life. The debate is not about whether nutq is required, but about its precise role.

Checkpoint

D) It is not required at all

Key Takeaway
The verifiers among the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools hold that verbal attestation (nutq) from a capable person is a condition (shart) for applying worldly Islamic rulings, not a part of iman itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you are capable of speech but have remained silent about your faith in a situation where a word could have clarified your identity, what does this lesson say about your accountability? Will you speak up now?

The Mu'tazila View on Nutq as a Condition for Iman's Validity
Sanusi reports the Mu'tazila position on nutq as a condition for sihhat al-iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the minority view of the Mu'tazila that verbal attestation (nutq) is a condition for the validity of iman itself, not merely for its worldly rulings.

Core Idea
For some, saying the words is not just proof of faith — it is part of faith's validity.

In discussions on faith (iman), scholars debate what exactly constitutes its validity. The majority hold that inner conviction suffices, but a minority — notably the Mu'tazila — argue that verbal declaration is a necessary condition for iman to be valid. This lesson introduces that minority position as recorded by Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the phrase 'huwa fahm al-aqall' (it is the understanding of the minority).
This phrase indicates that this is a minority view, not the consensus.
2
Identify the claim
The passage states that nutq is a condition for the validity (sihha) of iman. Understand that this means without verbal declaration, iman is not considered valid in the sight of Allah.
This is a stronger claim than saying nutq is required for worldly rulings.
3
Consider who holds this view
The commentary attributes this view to the Mu'tazila. Reflect on why they might require verbal expression for the validity of faith.
The Mu'tazila emphasize the role of speech in affirming belief.

Source Text

وقيل: إنه شرط في صحة الإيمان، وهو فهم الأقلّ.
And it was said: it is a condition for the validity of iman, and it is the understanding of the minority.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage states the minority view (qil) attributed to the Mu'tazila: that verbal attestation (nutq) is a condition for the validity (sihha) of iman itself, not merely for applying worldly rulings. The commentator notes that this is the understanding of the few (al-aqall).

Key Terms
mu'tazila

al-aqall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all scholars require nutq for iman's validity.
Correction: Only a minority (al-Mu'tazila) hold this view; the majority consider inner conviction sufficient for validity.

Checkpoint

A) It is a condition for worldly rulings
B) It is a condition for the validity of iman itself
C) It is not required
D) It is a part of iman

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila minority view considers verbal declaration a condition for the validity of iman, unlike the majority who see it as sufficient for outward rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight, would your verbal declaration of faith be enough to satisfy Allah's judgment according to the Mu'tazila view? Does your daily speech reflect the iman in your heart?
?
The Mu'tazila considered nutq a condition for the validity of iman. Do you ever find yourself silent about your faith when you should be speaking it? What does that silence reveal about the state of your iman?
?
This lesson highlights a minority view that takes outward expression seriously. In your own practice, do you treat the shahada as a mere formality or as the very condition of your faith's validity before Allah? How can you renew your verbal commitment today?

Three Matters of Disbelief in Philosophy
Lists three philosophical doctrines leading to disbelief and includes a poetic summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific doctrines of the philosophers that, according to Ash'ari theology, constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Three philosophical doctrines lead to takfir: denying divine knowledge of particulars, claiming world's eternity, and denying bodily resurrection.

The author now turns to refute the philosophers (falāsifa) on three foundational issues that contradict Islamic creed. These are not minor disagreements but core matters where the philosophers' positions are considered tantamount to disbelief. This short passage lists them clearly and even includes a poetic summary. As you read, note how each issue directly opposes a fundamental Islamic belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the three matters listed.
The passage begins by linking to the previous discussion on divine knowledge.
2
Identify the three matters
Identify the three doctrines for which the philosophers are deemed disbelievers. Write them down in your own words.
They are: denial of divine knowledge of particulars, claim of the world's eternity, and denial of bodily resurrection.
3
Reflect on why these are considered disbelief
Consider why each of these positions contradicts essential Islamic beliefs. For example, if God does not know particulars, His omniscience is denied. If the world is eternal, it competes with God's role as Creator. If bodies are not resurrected, the afterlife is undermined.
This passage does not provide proofs; it simply establishes the rulings.
4
Examine the poetic summary
Read the poem that summarizes the three matters. Notice its concise wording and how it aids memorization.
The poem is a pedagogical tool used in traditional texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل فيما يتعلق به العلم علمه تعالى، فهو سبحانه يعلم أنَّ له عِلماً . وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أنه سبحانه لا يعلم الجزئيات، وهو أحد مسائل ثلاث كُفّروا بها، وثانيها: ادّعاؤهم قِدَمَ العالم، وثالثها: إنكار بعث الأجساد وادعاؤهم أن الذي يُحشر هو الأرواحُ ، وقد نَظَمَ هذه المسائل بعضهم في قوله: بِثَلَاثَةٍ كَفَرَ الفَلَاسِفَةُ العِدَى إِذْ أَنْكَرُوهَا وَهِيَ عِلْمٌ بِجُزْئِيَّ ، حُدُوتُ عَوَالِم حَشْرٌ لأَجْسَادِ وَكَانَتْ مَيِّتَه اهـ محي .
And included in what knowledge relates to is His own knowledge, exalted, so He, glorified, knows that He has knowledge. And the philosophers held that He, exalted, does not know particulars, and this is one of three matters by which they are disbelieved. The second is their claim of the eternity of the world, and the third is the denial of the resurrection of bodies and their claim that what will be gathered is only spirits. And someone has versified these matters in his saying: 'By three things the hostile philosophers disbelieved, when they denied them, and they are: knowledge of a particular, origination of worlds, and resurrection of bodies that were dead.' End.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophic denial of God's knowledge of particulars and lists the three issues for which philosophers are considered disbelievers. First, denial of divine knowledge of particulars; second, claim of the world's eternity; third, denial of bodily resurrection, claiming only souls are resurrected. The author includes a poetic summary of these three points. This is a standard Ash'ari refutation of the falāsifa, who are often declared kāfir for these positions. The author also mentions that God's knowledge includes His own knowledge, underscoring its comprehensiveness.

Correction: The text explicitly lists it as one of three major reasons for takfir, showing its severity and centrality to Islamic creed.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers are declared disbelievers for three specific doctrines: denying God's knowledge of particulars, asserting the world's eternity, and denying bodily resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers denied that God knows the particulars of your life. Do you truly live as if every detail of your actions, thoughts, and intentions is witnessed by Him, or do you sometimes act as if some things are hidden from His knowledge? What would change if you fully internalized that He knows every particular?
?
The philosophers claimed the world is eternal. Reflect: do you ever act as if this world will last forever, investing your heart in it more than in the Hereafter? In what specific area of your life do you need to remind yourself that this world is originated and will end?
?
Do you genuinely await the resurrection of your body, or do you subconsciously think that only your spiritual legacy matters? How does the denial of bodily resurrection affect the way you treat your body and your physical actions, and what would it mean for you to truly believe that every limb will be held accountable?
