The Quran is Uncreated: Doctrine and Warning
Warns against calling the Quran created and clarifies the doctrinal nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna insist that the Quran (as Allah's attribute) is uncreated, and why they avoided the phrase 'the Quran is created' even when referring to the recited words.

Core Idea
The Quran is Allah's eternal speech, not a created thing — and saying otherwise invites divine vengeance.

The passage begins by reaffirming the Sunni doctrine that Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated, contrary to the Mu'tazila. It then issues a stark warning: beware of Allah's vengeance if you say it is created. This lesson unpacks the theological nuance behind that warning, clarifying the two senses of 'Quran' and the reason for extreme caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic assertion that Allah's speech is eternal, and note how it directly opposes the Mu'tazila view.
The author uses 'qadim' for eternal, not 'azali'.
2
Understand the warning
The line 'beware of His vengeance' is a direct warning against saying the Quran is originated (created). Identify what 'huduth' means in this context.
The marginal note explains that 'huduth' here is synonymous with 'khalq' due to poetic necessity.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of 'Quran'
The author explains that 'Quran' can refer to the eternal attribute in Allah's essence or to the recited words. Only the recited words are created.
4
See the reason for avoiding the phrase
Despite the createdness of the recited words, the phrase 'Quran is created' is avoided except in teaching because it might imply the eternal attribute is created, which is the Mu'tazila error.
This is why Ahmad ibn Hanbal and others refused to say it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام الله تعالى قديم عند أهل السنة خلافًا للمعتزلة
أيها المكلف أن تنزه القرآن أي: كلامه النفسي الأزلي القائم بذاته تعالى (عن الحدوث) أي: الوجود بعد العدم ; فليس مخلوقا، ولا قائما بمخلوق، بل هو صفة ذاته العلي ; لما علم من امتناع قيام الحوادث بذاته، ولضرورة النظم عبر بالحدوث عن الخلق (1) .

وقالوا: إن معنى خلق الكلام في بعض الأجرام كالجبل أو الشجرة أو نحوهما. ومراد المصنف هنا أن يقول: إن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى أي: بمعنى الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، مما يجب على المكلف أن يعتقده قدما، وينزهه عن الحدوث كما هو مذهب أهل السنة، وأما القرآن بمعنى اللفظ الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق، ومع كونه مخلوقا أن يقال: القرآن مخلوق ويراد اللفظ الذي نتلوه إلا في مقام التعليم ; لأنه ربما أوهم أن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى، أي: صفته القديمة مخلوق، ولهذا الإيهام امتنعت من القول بخلق القرآن، وقد وقع في هذا الموضوع امتحان كبير لخلق كثير، وأشهر من امتحن بذلك الإمام الناصر لسنة رسول الله أحمد بن حنبل رضي الله تعالى عنه، فقد حبس وضرب على أن يقول: «القرآن مخلوق» فأبى، ولم يقبل أن يقول هذه الكلمة - مع كونها صحيحة بأحد معنيين - مخافة أن تحمل على المعنى غير الجائز، وممن ابتلي بهذه المحنة أبو يعقوب يوسف ابن يحي المصري، المعروف بالبويطي، صاحب الإمام الشافعي، فإنه حمل من مصر إلى بغداد وطلب إليه أن يقول: «القرآن مخلوق» فلم يجب إلى ما دعي إليه، وقال: القرآن هو كلام الله غير مخلوق، فحبس، ومات في السجن يوم الجمعة قبل الصلاة سنة إحدى وثلاثين ومائتين وخرج البخاري إمام أهل الحديث فارا من هذه الفتنة، وقال: اللهم اقبضني إليك غير مفتون، فمات بعد أربعة أيام، وسجن عيسى بن دينار عشرين سنةً، وسئل الشعبي عن هذا، فقال: التوراة والإنجيل والزبور والفرقان، هذه الأربعة حادثة، ورفع أصابع يده الأربعة مشيرًا، فتركوه، وهذا من المعاريض التي فيها مندوحة عن الكذب، وينسب مثل هذه الحكاية إلى الإمام الشافعي، رضي الله تعالى عنه.
محي .
(1) قوله: ( ولضرورة النظم احتاج لهذا ; لأنّ المشهور بين القوم التعبير بالخلق. الأمير.
The speech of Allah Most High is eternal according to the People of the Sunna, contrary to the Mu'tazila. O responsible person, you must declare the Qur'an free, i.e., His essential, pre-eternal speech subsisting in His essence Most High, from being originated (al-huduth), i.e., existence after non-existence; so it is not created, nor subsisting in a created thing, but rather it is the attribute of His most high essence, because it is known from the impossibility of originated things subsisting in His essence, and due to the necessity of the poem he expressed origination as creation (1). = And beware of His vengeance) i.e., the vengeance of Allah upon you and His punishment for you if you say it is originated. The imams said that the meaning of the creation of speech in some bodies like the mountain or the tree or the like. And the intent of the author here is to say that the Qur'an in the sense of His speech Most High, i.e., in the sense of the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence Most High, is what the responsible person must believe to be eternal, and declare it free from origination as is the doctrine of the People of the Sunna. As for the Qur'an in the sense of the word that we read, it is created. And despite it being created, one should not say 'the Qur'an is created' meaning the word we recite except in a teaching context, because it might give the illusion that the Qur'an in the sense of His speech Most High, i.e., His eternal attribute, is created. Because of this illusion, they refrained from saying that the Qur'an is created. And there occurred in this subject a great trial for many. The most famous of those tried was the Imam, the supporter of the Sunna of the Messenger of Allah, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah Most High be pleased with him. He was imprisoned and beaten to say 'the Qur'an is created', but he refused, and he did not accept to say this word - despite it being correct in one of the two meanings - for fear that it would be understood in the impermissible sense. Among those afflicted with this trial was Abu Ya'qub Yusuf ibn Yahya al-Misri, known as al-Buwayti, the companion of Imam al-Shafi'i. He was carried from Egypt to Baghdad and asked to say 'the Qur'an is created', but he did not respond to what he was called to, and said: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, not created.' So he was imprisoned and died in prison on Friday before prayer in the year 231 AH. And al-Bukhari, the imam of Hadith, left fleeing from this fitna, and said: 'O Allah, take me to You untried,' and he died after four days. And Isa ibn Dinar was imprisoned for twenty years. And al-Sha'bi was asked about this, and he said: 'The Torah, the Gospel, the Psalms, and the Criterion (Qur'an), these four are originated,' and he raised his four fingers pointing, so they left him. This is from the ambiguous expressions (ta'rid) in which there is a way out from lying. And a similar story is attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah Most High be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage serves as a warning and a historical account regarding the doctrine of the Quran's status. It begins by restating that the speech of Allah (al-Kalam al-nafsi) is eternal and uncreated, and that the responsible person must declare it free from origination. The author explains that 'origination' here means creation, due to the poetic necessity. Then comes the warning: 'Beware of His vengeance' if one says it is originated. The text then clarifies that the Mu'tazila interpreted the creation of speech as happening in some bodies, like a mountain or tree. The author reasserts that the Quran as the eternal attribute must be believed eternal, while the recited words are created. However, one should not say 'the Quran is created' without clarification because it may be misunderstood to mean the eternal attribute is created. This caution is the reason Ahl al-Sunna refrained from that phrase. The passage then recounts the great trial (Mihna) that many faced. The most famous is Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who was imprisoned and beaten for refusing to say 'the Quran is created'. He refused even though the phrase could be correct in one sense, because it would be taken in the wrong sense. Others who suffered include al-Buwayti, who died in prison; al-Bukhari, who fled and died shortly after; Isa ibn Dinar, imprisoned twenty years; and the story of al-Sha'bi who used an ambiguous expression (ta'rid) by saying all four books are 'haditha' (originated) and raising four fingers, which allowed him to avoid lying. This shows the extreme caution of the Salaf in defending the doctrine.

Key Terms
كلام الله
القرآن
الحدوث
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Sunna believe the Quran is entirely uncreated in every sense, including the recited words.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the recited words are created, but the phrase 'the Quran is created' is avoided because it can be misunderstood to refer to the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should one not say 'the Quran is created' even when referring to the recited words?
A) Because the recited words are also uncreated
B) Because it might give the illusion that the eternal attribute is created
C) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it
D) Because the word 'Quran' only refers to the eternal attribute

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm the Quran (as Allah's attribute) is eternal and uncreated, and they avoid saying 'the Quran is created' even for the recited words due to potential misunderstanding.

The Mihna: The Trial of the Uncreated Quran
Recounts the Mihna and the steadfastness of the Salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical persecution of scholars who refused to say the Quran was created, and the use of ta'rid (ambiguous speech) to avoid lying.

Core Idea
The Salaf endured imprisonment and death rather than compromise on the Quran's uncreatedness, using clever ambiguity when necessary.

After establishing the doctrine, the passage recounts the great trial (Mihna) that befell many scholars. This lesson focuses on their steadfastness and the strategies they used to avoid falsehood while still protecting their lives, such as al-Sha'bi's ambiguous response.

Guided Path
1
Read about Imam Ahmad's ordeal
Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal was imprisoned and beaten for refusing to say 'the Quran is created' even though the phrase could be correct in one sense. Understand why he refused.
His refusal was based on fear of misinterpretation, not doctrinal ignorance.
2
Note the fate of al-Buwayti and al-Bukhari
Al-Buwayti was brought from Egypt to Baghdad, refused, and died in prison. Al-Bukhari fled and died shortly after asking Allah to take him untried.
These examples show the severity of the persecution.
3
Examine the ta'rid of al-Sha'bi
When asked, al-Sha'bi said the four books (Torah, Gospel, Psalms, Quran) are 'haditha' (originated) and raised four fingers. This ambiguous expression allowed him to avoid lying without stating the forbidden phrase.
Ta'rid was used to escape persecution while maintaining truthfulness.
4
Reflect on the lesson
The Salaf prioritized protecting the doctrine even at great personal cost, using permissible means to avoid falsehood.
This highlights the importance of theological precision and courage.

وقالوا: إن معنى خلق الكلام في بعض الأجرام كالجبل أو الشجرة أو نحوهما. ومراد المصنف هنا أن يقول: إن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى أي: بمعنى الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، مما يجب على المكلف أن يعتقده قدما، وينزهه عن الحدوث كما هو مذهب أهل السنة، وأما القرآن بمعنى اللفظ الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق الذي نقرؤه فهو مخلوق، ومع كونه مخلوقا أن يقال: القرآن مخلوق ويراد اللفظ الذي نتلوه إلا في مقام التعليم ; لأنه ربما أوهم أن القرآن بمعنى كلامه تعالى، أي: صفته القديمة مخلوق، ولهذا الإيهام امتنعت من القول بخلق القرآن، وقد وقع في هذا الموضوع امتحان كبير لخلق كثير، وأشهر من امتحن بذلك الإمام الناصر لسنة رسول الله أحمد بن حنبل رضي الله تعالى عنه، فقد حبس وضرب على أن يقول: «القرآن مخلوق» فأبى، ولم يقبل أن يقول هذه الكلمة - مع كونها صحيحة بأحد معنيين - مخافة أن تحمل على المعنى غير الجائز، وممن ابتلي بهذه المحنة أبو يعقوب يوسف ابن يحي المصري، المعروف بالبويطي، صاحب الإمام الشافعي، فإنه حمل من مصر إلى بغداد وطلب إليه أن يقول: «القرآن مخلوق» فلم يجب إلى ما دعي إليه، وقال: القرآن هو كلام الله غير مخلوق، فحبس، ومات في السجن يوم الجمعة قبل الصلاة سنة إحدى وثلاثين ومائتين وخرج البخاري إمام أهل الحديث فارا من هذه الفتنة، وقال: اللهم اقبضني إليك غير مفتون، فمات بعد أربعة أيام، وسجن عيسى بن دينار عشرين سنةً، وسئل الشعبي عن هذا، فقال: التوراة والإنجيل والزبور والفرقان، هذه الأربعة حادثة، ورفع أصابع يده الأربعة مشيرًا، فتركوه، وهذا من المعاريض التي فيها مندوحة عن الكذب، وينسب مثل هذه الحكاية إلى الإمام الشافعي، رضي الله تعالى عنه.
محي .
(1) قوله: ( ولضرورة النظم احتاج لهذا ; لأنّ المشهور بين القوم التعبير بالخلق. الأمير.
The speech of Allah Most High is eternal according to the People of the Sunna, contrary to the Mu'tazila. O responsible person, you must declare the Qur'an free, i.e., His essential, pre-eternal speech subsisting in His essence Most High, from being originated (al-huduth), i.e., existence after non-existence; so it is not created, nor subsisting in a created thing, but rather it is the attribute of His most high essence, because it is known from the impossibility of originated things subsisting in His essence, and due to the necessity of the poem he expressed origination as creation (1). = And beware of His vengeance) i.e., the vengeance of Allah upon you and His punishment for you if you say it is originated. The imams said that the meaning of the creation of speech in some bodies like the mountain or the tree or the like. And the intent of the author here is to say that the Qur'an in the sense of His speech Most High, i.e., in the sense of the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence Most High, is what the responsible person must believe to be eternal, and declare it free from origination as is the doctrine of the People of the Sunna. As for the Qur'an in the sense of the word that we read, it is created. And despite it being created, one should not say 'the Qur'an is created' meaning the word we recite except in a teaching context, because it might give the illusion that the Qur'an in the sense of His speech Most High, i.e., His eternal attribute, is created. Because of this illusion, they refrained from saying that the Qur'an is created. And there occurred in this subject a great trial for many. The most famous of those tried was the Imam, the supporter of the Sunna of the Messenger of Allah, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah Most High be pleased with him. He was imprisoned and beaten to say 'the Qur'an is created', but he refused, and he did not accept to say this word - despite it being correct in one of the two meanings - for fear that it would be understood in the impermissible sense. Among those afflicted with this trial was Abu Ya'qub Yusuf ibn Yahya al-Misri, known as al-Buwayti, the companion of Imam al-Shafi'i. He was carried from Egypt to Baghdad and asked to say 'the Qur'an is created', but he did not respond to what he was called to, and said: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, not created.' So he was imprisoned and died in prison on Friday before prayer in the year 231 AH. And al-Bukhari, the imam of Hadith, left fleeing from this fitna, and said: 'O Allah, take me to You untried,' and he died after four days. And Isa ibn Dinar was imprisoned for twenty years. And al-Sha'bi was asked about this, and he said: 'The Torah, the Gospel, the Psalms, and the Criterion (Qur'an), these four are originated,' and he raised his four fingers pointing, so they left him. This is from the ambiguous expressions (ta'rid) in which there is a way out from lying. And a similar story is attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah Most High be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage serves as a warning and a historical account regarding the doctrine of the Quran's status. It begins by restating that the speech of Allah (al-Kalam al-nafsi) is eternal and uncreated, and that the responsible person must declare it free from origination. The author explains that 'origination' here means creation, due to the poetic necessity. Then comes the warning: 'Beware of His vengeance' if one says it is originated. The text then clarifies that the Mu'tazila interpreted the creation of speech as happening in some bodies, like a mountain or tree. The author reasserts that the Quran as the eternal attribute must be believed eternal, while the recited words are created. However, one should not say 'the Quran is created' without clarification because it may be misunderstood to mean the eternal attribute is created. This caution is the reason Ahl al-Sunna refrained from that phrase. The passage then recounts the great trial (Mihna) that many faced. The most famous is Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who was imprisoned and beaten for refusing to say 'the Quran is created'. He refused even though the phrase could be correct in one sense, because it would be taken in the wrong sense. Others who suffered include al-Buwayti, who died in prison; al-Bukhari, who fled and died shortly after; Isa ibn Dinar, imprisoned twenty years; and the story of al-Sha'bi who used an ambiguous expression (ta'rid) by saying all four books are 'haditha' (originated) and raising four fingers, which allowed him to avoid lying. This shows the extreme caution of the Salaf in defending the doctrine.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the scholars were punished because they refused to admit a simple fact, but the passage shows they were protecting a core doctrine from being misunderstood.
Correction: The scholars recognized that the phrase could be true in one sense, but they avoided it to prevent people from thinking the eternal attribute is created.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf endured the Mihna with steadfastness and used permissible ambiguity (ta'rid) to avoid compromising the doctrine of the Quran's uncreatedness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Sha'bi used an ambiguous gesture rather than outright lying. Is there an area in your life where you are tempted to lie to avoid trouble? How can you use permissible ta'rid or wisdom to uphold truth without deceit?

The Certainty of Allah's Promise
Defines Allah's promise and argues it cannot be broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that Allah's promise of reward is absolutely certain and cannot be broken.

Core Idea
Allah's promise is unbreakable; breaking it would imply imperfection.

Before exploring the disagreement over the threat, Sanusi establishes a point of consensus: Allah's promise to believers is absolutely trustworthy. This lesson examines the definition of the promise, the Quranic evidence, and the rational argument that breaking it is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the promise
Read the Arabic passage that defines the promise and states it is legally obligatory to believe Allah fulfills it. Then read the translation.
Notice that the promise is only for those whom Allah intends good.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
The author cites two verses (30:6 and 3:9). Reflect on how these verses establish the certainty of the promise.
3
Understand the rational argument
The passage argues that breaking the promise would lead to impossible attributes like lying and foolishness. Grasp why that makes the promise unbreakable.
This is a key example of kalam reasoning: if X leads to impossible, X is impossible.
4
Confirm the consensus
Note that Ash'aris and Maturidis agree on this point. This sets the stage for the later disagreement on the threat.
The disagreement is only about the threat, not the promise.

ولَمَّا اختلف الأشاعرة والماتريديَّة فى الوَعْدِ والوَعِيد أشار إلى ذلك بقوله: (وَ) مِمَّا يجب شرعاً اعتقاده أنَّ الله تعالى (مُنْجِزٌ ) أي : مُعْطِ ( لِمَنْ أَرَادَ) به خيرًا (وَعْدَهُ ) الذي سبقتْ به إرادته في الأزل، ومفعول (أراد) محذوف تقديره: الخير. ومعنى الكلام: أنَّ وعد الله تعالى المؤمنين بالجنة والثواب لا يتخلَّفُ شرعاً ، وهذا أمر مقطوع به لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ الله لا يُخلِفُ اللهُ ﴾ [الروم: ٦] ، وقوله جل ذكره: إنَّ اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ ) [آل عمران: ٩] والمراد بالميعاد الوعد كما قاله المفسرون ، وبيان وجه كونه مقطوعا به : أنَّه لو تخلّف إعطاء الله تعالى الموعود به لزم الكذب والسَّفَهُ والخُلْف والنقص، وكل واحد من هذه الأشياء محال على الله تعالى ؛ فما أدى إليها أو إلى بعضها فهو محالٌ أيضا، وهذا المقدار متفق عليه بين كل من الأشاعرة والماتريدية ، وأما الوعيد ـ وهو تعذيب الله الكفار والعصاة وإدخالهم النَّار - فذهب الأشاعرة إلى أنه يجوز أنْ يغفر الله الذَّنوب ، ولا يدخل مرتكبها النَّار ولا يعذِّبه ، كما يجوز أن يعاقبه ويدخله النار ، واستدلوا على ذلك بنحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ الله يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا [الزمر: ٥٣] ، وبقوله سبحانه على لسان عيسى : وإِن تُعَذِّبْهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ عِبَادُكَ وَإِن تَغْفِرْ لَهُمْ فَإنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ﴾ [المائدة: ۱۱۸] ولولا أن عيسى يعلم أنَّ غُفران الذُّنوبِ جائز شرعاً لما قال هذا، وأيضاً فإن إخلاف الإنسانِ ما توعد به وهدد به لا يُعدُّ نقصاً ، بل يُعدُّ كَرَماً وحُسنَ خُلُقٍ ، وقد تمدح به النَّاس ؛ فمن ذلك قول الشاعر: وَإِنِّي وَإِنْ أَوْعَدْتُهُ أَوْ وَعَدْتُهُ لَمُخْلِفُ إِيعَادِي وَمُنْجِزُ مَوْعِدِي فلما اختلفَ أمرُ الخُلف في الوعد والوعيد ، فعدَّ العقلاء إخلاف الوعد نقصا، وعدوا إخلاف الوعيد كَرَما ، ولما كان الله موصوفا بكلّ كمال، جاز أنْ يُخلِفَ الإيعاد، ولم يجز أنْ يُخلف الوعد. وذهب المعتزلة إلى أنه لا يجوز عليه سبحانه أن يخلِفَ الوعيد كما لا يجوز عليه أن يُخلف الوعد. واستدلوا على ذلك بأنَّه لو جاز تخلُّف الوعيد للزم عنه مفاسد كثيرة: منها: وقوع الكذب في خبره تعالى الذي أخبر فيه بأنه يعاقب من اقترف الآثام، والكذب في خبره تعالى محال . ومنها: تبدلُ القول ، وهو يقول : مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى وَمَا أَنَا بِظَلَمِ لِلْعَبِيدِ ) [ق: ٢٩]. ومنها: أنه يستتبع القول بعدم خلودِ الكُفار في النَّار ؛ حيث جوزنا أن يعفو الله ، وخلود الكفار في النَّار مما قامت الأدلة المتوافرة عليه .
And when the Ash'aris and Maturidis disagreed concerning the promise and the threat, he alluded to that by his statement: (And) from what is legally obligatory to believe is that Allah, the Exalted, is (fulfiller) meaning: giver (to whom He intended) good (His promise) which was preceded by His will in eternity. The object of 'arada' is omitted, its implied meaning: good. And the meaning of the speech: that the promise of Allah, the Exalted, to the believers of Paradise and reward is not withheld legally, and this is a matter decisive, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'The promise of Allah: Allah does not fail in His promise' (al-Rum 30:6), and His statement, may His mention be exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not fail in His promise' (Aal 'Imran 3:9). What is meant by 'al-mi'ad' is the promise as the exegetes said. And the explanation of its being decisive: that if the giving by Allah, the Exalted, of what was promised were to be withheld, it would necessitate lying, foolishness, breach of promise, and imperfection, and each one of these things is impossible for Allah, the Exalted; so what leads to them or to some of them is also impossible. And this amount is agreed upon between both the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. As for the threat – which is Allah's punishing the disbelievers and sinners and entering them into the Fire – the Ash'aris held that it is permissible for Allah to forgive sins, and not enter the perpetrator into the Fire nor punish him, just as it is permissible for Him to punish him and enter him into the Fire. And they argued for that with the like of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' (al-Zumar 39:53), and with His statement, the Exalted, on the tongue of Jesus: 'If You punish them, indeed they are Your servants; but if You forgive them, indeed You are the Exalted in Might, the Wise' (al-Ma'idah 5:118). And were it not that Jesus knew that forgiveness of sins is legally permissible, he would not have said this. Also, that a human's breaking what he threatened and warned of is not considered a deficiency, but rather is considered generosity and good character, and people have boasted of that; from that is the saying of the poet: 'And indeed I, if I threaten him or promise him, am one who breaks my threat and fulfills my promise.' So when the matter of breaking differs with respect to promise and threat, and reasonable people considered breaking a promise a deficiency, and they considered breaking a threat generosity, and since Allah is described with every perfection, it is permissible for Him to break the threat, and it is not permissible for Him to break the promise. And the Mu'tazila held that it is not permissible for Him, glory be to Him, to break the threat just as it is not permissible for Him to break the promise. And they argued for that that if breaking the threat were permissible, it would necessitate many corruptions: from them: the occurrence of falsehood in His statement, the Exalted, in which He informed that He punishes those who commit sins, and falsehood in His statement, the Exalted, is impossible. And from them: the change of the statement, and He says: 'My word is not changed, and I am not unjust to My servants' (Qaf 50:29). And from them: that it entails the statement of non-eternity of the disbelievers in the Fire, since we permit that Allah may pardon, and the eternity of the disbelievers in the Fire is that for which abundant evidence has been established.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a comparative theological discussion on the topic of 'al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (the promise and the threat). It begins by noting the disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis on this issue, but quickly clarifies that the disagreement is only about the threat, not the promise. The commentator defines the promise as Allah's statement that He will reward believers, and states that this promise is absolutely certain and cannot be broken, citing Quranic verses (al-Rum 30:6 and Aal 'Imran 3:9). He argues that breaking the promise would imply deficiencies like lying and foolishness, which are impossible for Allah. This point is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. Then it shifts to the threat, defined as Allah's warning of punishment for disbelievers and sinners. The Ash'ari position is that Allah may forgive sinners and not punish them, or may punish them, as it is permissible for Him to do either. They support this with Quranic verses (al-Zumar 39:53 and al-Ma'idah 5:118) and a rational argument from human custom: breaking a threat is seen as generosity, not a flaw, while breaking a promise is seen as a flaw. Since Allah is perfect, He may break the threat but not the promise. The Mu'tazila disagree, arguing that breaking a threat is impossible for Allah because it would imply falsehood, change of word (Qaf 50:29), and would entail that disbelievers might not be eternally punished, contradicting decisive evidence of eternal punishment. This passage outlines the key arguments of both schools without resolving the dispute.

Key Terms
الوعد (al-wa'd)
إخلاف (ikhlaaf)
الأشاعرة (al-Ash'ari)
الماتريدية (al-Maturidi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that Allah cannot break His promise?
A) Because breaking a promise is always impossible for Allah.
B) Because breaking a promise would imply lying, foolishness, and imperfection.
C) Because the Qur'an says He will never break a promise.
D) Because they disagree on the threat but not the promise.

Key Takeaway
Allah's promise of reward is absolutely certain and unbreakable, agreed upon by Ash'aris and Maturidis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's promise to forgive you upon repentance is absolutely certain, even when your sins feel overwhelming? If you are certain, why do you still delay turning back to Him? If you doubt, what does that reveal about your understanding of His promise?
?
The passage argues that breaking a promise implies imperfection. When you break promises to others, what does it say about your character? How seriously do you take your own promises, knowing that Allah—who is perfect—never breaks His?
?
If Allah's promise of reward for sincere sacrifice is unbreakable, what holds you back from giving more of your time, wealth, or comfort for His sake? Examine your heart: is it a lack of trust in His promise, or something else?

The Disagreement over Allah's Threat
Presents Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views on the threat of punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions on whether Allah must fulfill His threat of punishment, and the key arguments for each view.

Core Idea
Ash'aris permit breaking the threat; Mu'tazilis forbid it.

While both schools agree on the certainty of the promise, they differ sharply on the threat of punishment. The Ash'aris argue that Allah may forgive or punish as He wills, while the Mu'tazilis insist that He must punish sin. This lesson presents their scriptural and rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the threat
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما الوعيد' to the end. Then read the translation. Focus on the different positions.
The passage defines the threat as punishment for disbelievers and sinners.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position and proofs
The Ash'aris say it is permissible for Allah to forgive or punish. Note the Quranic verses (39:53 and 5:118) and the rational argument from human custom.
The key rational point: breaking a threat is seen as generosity, not a flaw.
3
Identify the Mu'tazili position and proofs
The Mu'tazilis say Allah must fulfill the threat. Note their arguments: falsehood, change of word, and implications for eternal punishment.
They argue that breaking the threat would contradict God's word and justice.
4
Compare the two positions
Reflect on how the two schools use reason and scripture differently. Consider why this disagreement matters for theology.
This disagreement has implications for understanding God's justice and mercy.

ولَمَّا اختلف الأشاعرة والماتريديَّة فى الوَعْدِ والوَعِيد أشار إلى ذلك بقوله: (وَ) مِمَّا يجب شرعاً اعتقاده أنَّ الله تعالى (مُنْجِزٌ ) أي : مُعْطِ ( لِمَنْ أَرَادَ) به خيرًا (وَعْدَهُ ) الذي سبقتْ به إرادته في الأزل، ومفعول (أراد) محذوف تقديره: الخير. ومعنى الكلام: أنَّ وعد الله تعالى المؤمنين بالجنة والثواب لا يتخلَّفُ شرعاً ، وهذا أمر مقطوع به لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ الله لا يُخلِفُ اللهُ ﴾ [الروم: ٦] ، وقوله جل ذكره: إنَّ اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ ) [آل عمران: ٩] والمراد بالميعاد الوعد كما قاله المفسرون ، وبيان وجه كونه مقطوعا به : أنَّه لو تخلّف إعطاء الله تعالى الموعود به لزم الكذب والسَّفَهُ والخُلْف والنقص، وكل واحد من هذه الأشياء محال على الله تعالى ؛ فما أدى إليها أو إلى بعضها فهو محالٌ أيضا، وهذا المقدار متفق عليه بين كل من الأشاعرة والماتريدية ، وأما الوعيد ـ وهو تعذيب الله الكفار والعصاة وإدخالهم النَّار - فذهب الأشاعرة إلى أنه يجوز أنْ يغفر الله الذَّنوب ، ولا يدخل مرتكبها النَّار ولا يعذِّبه ، كما يجوز أن يعاقبه ويدخله النار ، واستدلوا على ذلك بنحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ الله يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا [الزمر: ٥٣] ، وبقوله سبحانه على لسان عيسى : وإِن تُعَذِّبْهُمْ فَإِنَّهُمْ عِبَادُكَ وَإِن تَغْفِرْ لَهُمْ فَإنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ﴾ [المائدة: ۱۱۸] ولولا أن عيسى يعلم أنَّ غُفران الذُّنوبِ جائز شرعاً لما قال هذا، وأيضاً فإن إخلاف الإنسانِ ما توعد به وهدد به لا يُعدُّ نقصاً ، بل يُعدُّ كَرَماً وحُسنَ خُلُقٍ ، وقد تمدح به النَّاس ؛ فمن ذلك قول الشاعر: وَإِنِّي وَإِنْ أَوْعَدْتُهُ أَوْ وَعَدْتُهُ لَمُخْلِفُ إِيعَادِي وَمُنْجِزُ مَوْعِدِي فلما اختلفَ أمرُ الخُلف في الوعد والوعيد ، فعدَّ العقلاء إخلاف الوعد نقصا، وعدوا إخلاف الوعيد كَرَما ، ولما كان الله موصوفا بكلّ كمال، جاز أنْ يُخلِفَ الإيعاد، ولم يجز أنْ يُخلف الوعد. وذهب المعتزلة إلى أنه لا يجوز عليه سبحانه أن يخلِفَ الوعيد كما لا يجوز عليه أن يُخلف الوعد. واستدلوا على ذلك بأنَّه لو جاز تخلُّف الوعيد للزم عنه مفاسد كثيرة: منها: وقوع الكذب في خبره تعالى الذي أخبر فيه بأنه يعاقب من اقترف الآثام، والكذب في خبره تعالى محال . ومنها: تبدلُ القول ، وهو يقول : مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى وَمَا أَنَا بِظَلَمِ لِلْعَبِيدِ ) [ق: ٢٩]. ومنها: أنه يستتبع القول بعدم خلودِ الكُفار في النَّار ؛ حيث جوزنا أن يعفو الله ، وخلود الكفار في النَّار مما قامت الأدلة المتوافرة عليه .
And when the Ash'aris and Maturidis disagreed concerning the promise and the threat, he alluded to that by his statement: (And) from what is legally obligatory to believe is that Allah, the Exalted, is (fulfiller) meaning: giver (to whom He intended) good (His promise) which was preceded by His will in eternity. The object of 'arada' is omitted, its implied meaning: good. And the meaning of the speech: that the promise of Allah, the Exalted, to the believers of Paradise and reward is not withheld legally, and this is a matter decisive, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'The promise of Allah: Allah does not fail in His promise' (al-Rum 30:6), and His statement, may His mention be exalted: 'Indeed, Allah does not fail in His promise' (Aal 'Imran 3:9). What is meant by 'al-mi'ad' is the promise as the exegetes said. And the explanation of its being decisive: that if the giving by Allah, the Exalted, of what was promised were to be withheld, it would necessitate lying, foolishness, breach of promise, and imperfection, and each one of these things is impossible for Allah, the Exalted; so what leads to them or to some of them is also impossible. And this amount is agreed upon between both the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. As for the threat – which is Allah's punishing the disbelievers and sinners and entering them into the Fire – the Ash'aris held that it is permissible for Allah to forgive sins, and not enter the perpetrator into the Fire nor punish him, just as it is permissible for Him to punish him and enter him into the Fire. And they argued for that with the like of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' (al-Zumar 39:53), and with His statement, the Exalted, on the tongue of Jesus: 'If You punish them, indeed they are Your servants; but if You forgive them, indeed You are the Exalted in Might, the Wise' (al-Ma'idah 5:118). And were it not that Jesus knew that forgiveness of sins is legally permissible, he would not have said this. Also, that a human's breaking what he threatened and warned of is not considered a deficiency, but rather is considered generosity and good character, and people have boasted of that; from that is the saying of the poet: 'And indeed I, if I threaten him or promise him, am one who breaks my threat and fulfills my promise.' So when the matter of breaking differs with respect to promise and threat, and reasonable people considered breaking a promise a deficiency, and they considered breaking a threat generosity, and since Allah is described with every perfection, it is permissible for Him to break the threat, and it is not permissible for Him to break the promise. And the Mu'tazila held that it is not permissible for Him, glory be to Him, to break the threat just as it is not permissible for Him to break the promise. And they argued for that that if breaking the threat were permissible, it would necessitate many corruptions: from them: the occurrence of falsehood in His statement, the Exalted, in which He informed that He punishes those who commit sins, and falsehood in His statement, the Exalted, is impossible. And from them: the change of the statement, and He says: 'My word is not changed, and I am not unjust to My servants' (Qaf 50:29). And from them: that it entails the statement of non-eternity of the disbelievers in the Fire, since we permit that Allah may pardon, and the eternity of the disbelievers in the Fire is that for which abundant evidence has been established.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a comparative theological discussion on the topic of 'al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (the promise and the threat). It begins by noting the disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis on this issue, but quickly clarifies that the disagreement is only about the threat, not the promise. The commentator defines the promise as Allah's statement that He will reward believers, and states that this promise is absolutely certain and cannot be broken, citing Quranic verses (al-Rum 30:6 and Aal 'Imran 3:9). He argues that breaking the promise would imply deficiencies like lying and foolishness, which are impossible for Allah. This point is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. Then it shifts to the threat, defined as Allah's warning of punishment for disbelievers and sinners. The Ash'ari position is that Allah may forgive sinners and not punish them, or may punish them, as it is permissible for Him to do either. They support this with Quranic verses (al-Zumar 39:53 and al-Ma'idah 5:118) and a rational argument from human custom: breaking a threat is seen as generosity, not a flaw, while breaking a promise is seen as a flaw. Since Allah is perfect, He may break the threat but not the promise. The Mu'tazila disagree, arguing that breaking a threat is impossible for Allah because it would imply falsehood, change of word (Qaf 50:29), and would entail that disbelievers might not be eternally punished, contradicting decisive evidence of eternal punishment. This passage outlines the key arguments of both schools without resolving the dispute.

Key Terms
الوعيد (al-wa'id)
الأشاعرة (al-Ash'ari)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
إخلاف (ikhlaaf)
الكذب (al-kadhib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position denies that Allah punishes sinners at all.
Correction: The Ash'ari position only affirms that Allah is not obligated to punish; He may punish or forgive as He wills.

Checkpoint
What rational analogy do the Ash'aris use to argue that Allah may break His threat?
A) Breaking a threat is seen as generosity in human custom.
B) Breaking a threat is seen as a deficiency in human custom.
C) Breaking a threat is impossible because God is perfect.
D) Breaking a threat would make God unjust.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris argue Allah may break His threat (forgive or punish), while Mu'tazilis argue He must fulfill it, each using scriptural and rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your sins, do you find yourself more relying on Allah's threat of punishment or on His mercy? How does the Ash'ari position on the threat—that Allah may forgive even without obligation—challenge you to balance hope and fear in your daily life? What is one concrete way you will adjust your attitude toward sin this week?
?
The Mu'tazilis insist that Allah must fulfill His threat to avoid falsehood. If you truly believe that God's warnings are certain, does that change how you view your smaller, 'private' sins? Are there any sins you are neglecting to repent from because you assume forgiveness is easy? Commit to seeking forgiveness for one such sin today.
?
The Ash'aris argue that breaking a threat is seen as generosity in human custom. Do you truly believe that Allah's withholding of punishment is an act of generosity towards you, or do you take it for granted? How can you cultivate a deeper gratitude for the times Allah has forgiven you despite your deserving punishment?

Maturidi and Ash'ari on Wa'id and Du'a
Sanusi contrasts Maturidi and Ash'ari views on wa'id and the permissibility of supplicating for forgiveness of all sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical consequence of the Maturidi position on divine threats for supplication, and how it contrasts with the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari view threats as conditional, while Maturidis see them as binding, affecting how we pray.

This lesson explores a concrete disagreement between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools: whether one may ask God to forgive all sins for all people. This disagreement stems from deeper views on whether divine threats (wa'id) are binding or conditional. You will see how theological principles translate into everyday practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two distinct parts: the ruling on du'a and the underlying reasoning.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Maturidi and Ash'ari answers to the question: is it permissible to pray 'O Allah, forgive me and all sins'?
3
Understand the reasoning
For each school, understand why they hold their position: Ash'aris allow it because threats are conditional on God's will; Maturidis forbid it because threats are binding.
The Ash'ari view on threats being broken as generosity is key.
4
Recognize the practical implication
Reflect on how this disagreement affects everyday supplication and the interpretation of Quranic threat verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبني على هذا الخلاف أنَّه هل يجوز أن يقول الإنسان في دعائه: اللهم اغفر لي ولجميع الذَّنوب، أَوْ لَا يجوز ذلك؟ : فأما الماتريدية فيقولون: لا يجوز الدعاء بهذه العبارة وأمثالها. وأما الأشاعرة فيقولون: يجوز، والله تعالى أعلى وأعلم. محي.
عليه سبحانه أن لا يفي به مَن أوعَدَهُ إِيَّاه; لأنَّ الخُلْفَ في الوَعِيد لا يُعدَّ نقصا، بل يُعدُّ كَرَما يُتَمدَّحُ به، والكريم إذا أخبر بالوعيد فاللائق بكرمه أنْ يبني إخباره به على المشيئة، وإن لم يصرّح بها، بخلاف الوَعْد; فإنّ اللائق إخباره به على الجزم، هذا ما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة، وذهب الماتريديَّةً إلى امتناع تخلّف الوعيد كالوعد، وجعلوا الآيات الواردة بعموم الوعيد مخصوصة بالمؤمن المغفور له(١)
And upon this disagreement is based the question: Is it permissible for a person to say in his supplication: 'O Allah, forgive me and all sins,' or is it not permissible? As for the Maturidis, they say: Supplication with this phrase and its likes is not permissible. As for the Ash'aris, they say: It is permissible. And Allah is Higher and Knower. (Finished). It is incumbent upon Him, exalted, that He not fulfill (the threat) for the one He threatened, because breaking the threat is not considered a deficiency, but rather is considered generosity to be praised. And the generous one, when he informs of a threat, it is befitting to his generosity that he builds his information on the will, even if he does not explicitly state it, unlike the promise; for it is befitting that his information about it be on certainty. This is the view of the Ash'aris. The Maturidis, however, hold the impossibility of the threat lagging like the promise, and they consider the verses that come with general threat to be specified (makhsusa) to the believer who is forgiven.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis regarding threats (wa'id). It first presents a practical implication: should a person pray 'O Allah, forgive me and all sins'? The Maturidis forbid such a general request because they believe God's threats are binding for those not specifically forgiven; thus asking for forgiveness for all sins implies that even the unforgiven might be forgiven, which contradicts the binding nature of the threat. The Ash'aris permit it because they hold that threats are conditional on God's will, so He may forgive anyone. The text then restates the Ash'ari position: God is not obligated to fulfill His threat because breaking it is not a deficiency but an act of generosity; the threat is built on the will, whereas a promise is built on certainty. It then states the Maturidi view: they consider it impossible for the threat to lag (i.e., go unfulfilled) just as a promise cannot lag, and they interpret the general threatening verses as being specified (takhsis) to the believer who is forgiven—meaning the threat applies only to those not forgiven.

Key Terms
دعاء (du'a)
مغفرة (maghfira)
تخصيص (takhsis)
الماتريدية (al-Maturidiyya)
الأشاعرة (al-Ash'ariyya)
وعيد (wa'id)
وعد (wa'd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis on wa'id is just theoretical.
Correction: In fact, it determines whether one can pray for forgiveness for all sins, as Maturidis forbid the general supplication while Ash'aris permit it.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, why is it not permissible to ask God to forgive all sins for all Muslims?
A) Because God cannot forgive all sins
B) Because the general threat verses are specified to the forgiven believer, so some unfulfilled threats remain
C) Because asking for forgiveness implies disbelief
D) Because the Ash'aris do not allow it

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis forbid the general supplication for forgiveness of all sins because they consider divine threats binding and see general threat verses as specified to the forgiven believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for forgiveness for all your sins, do you truly trust in Allah's mercy, or are you relying on a theological position that allows it? How does the Maturidi perspective challenge you to take Allah's threats seriously in your own life?
?
If you knew that God's threats are binding and your prayer for forgiveness might imply disbelief in that, would you change your prayer? What does your current practice say about your view of divine justice?
?
The Ash'aris see breaking threats as generosity. Does that view make you less fearful of sin? Should it? How do you balance hope and fear in your relationship with Allah?

The Eternality of Happiness and Misery
The poet affirms the eternity and immutability of happiness and misery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni theology, the ultimate states of happiness and misery are fixed in God's eternal knowledge and do not change from one to the other.

Core Idea
Your final destiny is eternally fixed and does not transfer.

This lesson introduces a foundational doctrine from the Jawharat al-Tawhid: the eternity of happiness and misery. The poem line concisely states that the happy person's bliss and the miserable person's wretchedness are determined in eternity and never change. We'll explore what this means and how it relates to broader discussions about God's promises and threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line
Read the Arabic heading and poem line once. Notice the direct assertion that happiness and misery are with Allah in eternity and do not transfer.
The line is from the matn (text) itself.
2
Understand 'fixed in eternity'
Reflect on what it means for something to be 'in eternity' (al-azal). This indicates God's timeless decree, not something that changes over time.
This is a key concept in kalam: divine predestination.
3
Explore the footnote debate
The footnote explains a nuance: Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that the threat (of punishment) can lag, but differ on whether the verse about forgiveness is conditioned on God's will or specified to particular believers.
This shows how the doctrine of fixed states interacts with discussions of God's justice.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think about how this doctrine relates to God's omniscience and justice. If final states are fixed, human actions are still real but within God's decree.
This is a common point of reflection in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[السعادة والشقاوة والخلاف فيهما هل تتبدلان أو لا؟]
٤٧ - فوز فوزُ السَّعِيدِ عِنْدَهُ في الأَزَلِ كَذا الشَّقِيُّ ثُمّ لَمْ يَنَتِقل
وأشار إلى اختلافهما أيضًا في السَّعَادة والشَّقاوة بقوله: ومما يجب
(١) قوله: (مخصوصة بالمؤمن الباء سببية، أي: مخرج من عمومها المؤمن المغفور له، والمعنى: أنه لا يجوز تخلف الوعيد إلا في المؤمن المغفور له، وأما المؤمن الذي لم تحصل له المغفرة من ربه فلا بد من نفوذ الوعيد فيه، فظهر أن الأشاعرة والماتريدية متفقون على أن الوعد لا يتخلف، والوعيد يتخلف، وإنما النزاع في أمر لفظي وهو: هل الآية يُقدَّر فيها المشيئة أو يلاحظ فيها التخصيص. الشنواني. وعند الأمير: وقد يقال على أنه مُعلّق بالمشيئة: يجوز العفو عن جميع العصاة، وعلى أنه مخصوص: لا بد للعام من شيء يتحقق فيه لأن التخصيص لا يستغرق، ألا ترى قولهم: إن الاستثناء المُستغرق باطل، ولو استغرق التخصيص لكانَ نسخًا وإزالة لا تخصيصا، فظهر أنَّ الخلاف حقيقي، وأن قولهم لا بد من إنفاذ الوعيد ولو في واحد إنما يظهر على كلام الماتريدية، ويصح على مقتضى الأشاعرة طلب الغفران لجميع المسلمين من غير ملاحظة التخصيص بما عدا من يتحقق فيه الوعيد، ولا أنه يتحقق في زان مثلا كافر، فليتأمل بإنصاف، نعم في أحاديث الشفاعة ونحوها ما يقضي بدخول بعض الموحدين النار، لكنه مدرك آخر فليلاحظ. الأمير.
[Happiness and misery and the disagreement about them: do they change or not?]
47 - The happiness of the happy (al-sa'id) is with Him in eternity, likewise the miserable (al-shaqi), then it does not transfer.
And he alluded to their disagreement also in happiness and misery by his saying: 'And what is obligatory.'
(1) His saying: ('specific to the believer'—the letter ba' is sababiyya, i.e., the believer who is forgiven is excluded from the general meaning. And the meaning is that it is not permissible for the threat to lag except in the case of the believer who is forgiven. As for the believer who has not obtained forgiveness from his Lord, the threat must take effect in him. So it appears that the Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that the promise does not lag, and the threat lags, and the dispute is only a verbal matter: whether the verse is conditioned by the will or whether specification (takhsis) is observed. Al-Shanwani said. And according to al-Amir: It may be said that on the view that it is conditioned by the will, it is permissible to forgive all sinners; and on the view that it is specified, there must be some in whom the threat is realized because specification does not exhaust the general. Do you not see their statement that exhaustive exception (al-istithna' al-mustaghriq) is invalid, and if specification exhausted it would be abrogation and removal, not specification. So it appears that the dispute is real, and their statement that the threat must be executed even in one person appears only on the Maturidi view, and on the Ash'ari view it is valid to seek forgiveness for all Muslims without paying attention to specification except for those in whom the threat is realized, and it is not realized in, for example, an adulterer who is a disbeliever? So let him reflect with fairness. Yes, in the hadiths of intercession and the like there is evidence that some monotheists enter the Fire, but that is another point; so let it be noted. Al-Amir.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) and whether they can change. The heading poses the question. The poem line (line 47 from the Jawharat al-Tawhid) states a key doctrine: the happiness of the happy (the one who is destined for bliss) is fixed with God in eternity, and likewise the misery of the miserable, and then it does not transfer—meaning the final state does not change from one to the other. The commentator then notes that the poet alludes to the disagreement (presumably between Ash'aris and Maturidis) also in this matter by his saying: 'And what is obligatory' (which may be the beginning of the next line). The lengthy footnote explains the connection between this and the earlier discussion of threats (wa'id). It clarifies that the specification of threats to the forgiven believer means that only the forgiven believer is exempt from the threat; for the believer not forgiven, the threat must be carried out. The footnote then draws a comparison: Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that promise does not lag and threat lags, but the dispute is verbal: whether the verse is interpreted as conditioned on the will (mashī'a) or as specified (takhsis). The author (al-Amir) argues that the difference is real: if conditioned on will, forgiveness of all sinners is possible; if specified, there must be at least one person in whom the threat is realized, because specification does not exhaust the general. He cites a point about exhaustive exception being invalid, and notes that on the Maturidi view, one must affirm that the threat is executed in at least one person, while on the Ash'ari view one can pray for all Muslims. He adds that hadiths about intercession show that some monotheists enter the Fire, which is a separate consideration.

Key Terms
سعادة (sa'ada)
شقاوة (shaqawa)
أزل (azal)
انتقال (intiqal)
تخصيص (takhsis)
مشيئة (mashī'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One's final state can change through actions alone, e.g., eternal happiness can be lost by sin or eternal misery can be escaped by repentance.
Correction: The passage teaches that happiness and misery are fixed in God's eternal decree and do not transfer. While actions have consequences, the ultimate outcome is determined by God's eternal knowledge and will, not by human effort alone.

Checkpoint
What does the poem line state about happiness and misery?
A) They can change through repentance
B) They are fixed in eternity and do not transfer
C) They are determined by human effort
D) They are unknown to God until the afterlife

Key Takeaway
Happiness and misery are eternally decreed by Allah and do not transfer from one person to another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your final state of happiness or misery was eternally fixed before you were born, does that make you more humble or more reckless in seeking Allah's mercy? What does your daily repentance say about your belief?
?
The footnote shows that even on the Ash'ari view, forgiveness is not automatic — it depends on God's will. Are you actively seeking that forgiveness as if it depends on your effort, or do you take it for granted?
?
When you see a Muslim suffering in this world, do you assume they are among the miserable in the afterlife? How does the teaching that happiness and misery do not transfer prevent you from judging people's eternal fate based on their worldly condition?

The Verbal Dispute on Saying 'I am a Believer if Allah Wills'
Discusses the permissibility of saying 'ana mu'min in sha Allah' and the verbal dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris permit saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' while the Maturidis do not, and why the disagreement is considered verbal.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute on 'if Allah wills' is really about definitions, not faith.

This lesson examines a classic difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools regarding the permissibility of adding 'if Allah wills' when professing one's faith. The passage reveals that the disagreement stems from different definitions of happiness (sa'adah) and misery (shaqawah), and concludes that the dispute is ultimately verbal. By understanding this nuance, you will appreciate how theological schools can appear to disagree yet fundamentally align.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate where the Ash'ari view is presented (permitting the statement with a view to the final outcome) and where the Maturidi view is presented (rejecting it with a view to the present state).
3
Understand the verbal dispute
Ask yourself why the author says the dispute is verbal. The commentary will help you see that both schools agree on the underlying rulings but differ in how they define happiness and misery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا يصح أن تقول: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله تعالى نظرا للمآل، وعند الماتريدية لا يصح ذلك، نظرا للحال؛ إذ السعيد عندهم هو المسلم، والشقي: هو الكافر، والسعادة: الإسلام، والشقاوة: الكفر؛ فيُتصوَّر في السعيد أن يشقى؛ بأن يرتدّ بعد الإيمان، ويسعد الشقي بأن يؤمن بعد الكفر، فليس كل من السعادة والشقاوة أزليا، بل تتغيّران وتتبدلان، والخلف لفظي.
And upon this it is permissible for you to say: 'I am a believer if Allah wills, exalted be He,' with a view to the final outcome. And according to the Maturidis, that is not permissible, with a view to the present state; because the happy one according to them is the Muslim, and the miserable one is the disbeliever, and happiness is Islam, and misery is disbelief. So it is conceivable for the happy one to become miserable by apostatizing after faith, and for the miserable one to become happy by believing after disbelief. So neither happiness nor misery is eternal, rather they change and alter, and the dispute is verbal.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' and clarifies the difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions. It begins by stating that on the basis of the Ash'ari definition of sa'adah (happiness) as dying upon faith, the statement 'I am a believer if Allah wills' is permissible (يصح) when said with a view to the final outcome (نظرا للمآل), because one's final end is unknown. However, according to the Maturidis, this statement is not permissible because they look at the present state (نظرا للحال): for them, a believer is currently a Muslim, so there is no reason to suspend it. The Maturidi definition differs: happiness is being a Muslim in this world, misery is being a disbeliever, and they hold that a believer can become a disbeliever and vice versa, so sa'adah and shaqawah are not eternal but can change. The author concludes that the dispute between the two schools is verbal (الخلف لفظي), meaning they agree on the underlying rulings but differ on how the terms 'sa'adah' and 'shaqawah' are defined.

Key Terms
أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله
المآل
الحال
الماتريدية
الأشاعرة
الخلاف لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' implies doubt about one's current faith.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Ash'ari allow it because of uncertainty about the final outcome, not because of doubt about the present state.

Checkpoint
Why do the Maturidis consider saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' impermissible?
A) Because it implies ignorance of Allah's knowledge.
B) Because it disrespects Allah's decree.
C) Because they look at the present state, and the person is currently a believer.
D) Because they believe sa'adah and shaqawah are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute over saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' is verbal, stemming from different definitions of happiness and misery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'In sha Allah' about your faith, do you truly feel your absolute dependence on Allah for your final end, or is it just a habit? Pause right now and ask Allah to keep you firm upon faith until death.
?
This lesson shows that a believer can become a disbeliever through apostasy. Have you ever taken your current state of faith for granted? Identify one area where you feel spiritually vulnerable and commit to a specific action this week to strengthen it.
?
The dispute is verbal, yet the reality is that your final outcome is known only to Allah. How does that truth affect how you will live this day? Will you increase in good deeds out of hope or abandon sin out of fear? Make a concrete intention for one deed today that reflects both.

Kasb: The Ash'ari Middle Path on Human Action
Sanusi refutes Jabriyya and resolves the 'one act, two powers' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) affirms human responsibility while preserving Allah's sole role as Creator, and how it resolves the logical objection that one act cannot be caused by two agents.

Core Idea
One act, two agents—different aspects; Allah creates, the servant acquires.

The Ash'ari position must walk a tightrope: rational proof shows that Allah alone creates actions, yet scripture and conscience affirm human accountability. The Jabriyya denied any human role, while the Mu'tazila granted humans creative power. Sanusi here presents the Sunni middle path—kasb—and answers the philosophical objection that one act cannot have two efficient causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from refutation to objection to answer.
The passage contains a dialectical structure: thesis, objection, response.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where Sanusi states the Sunni position (kasb) and the Jabriyya rejection. Understand what kasb means: not creation but acquisition.
The key phrase: 'the servant is not an effecter in the sense of invention.'
3
Locate the objection
The objection: 'one thing within capacity cannot fall under two powers.' Why is this a problem?
If God creates everything, how can the servant have any real role?
4
Follow the answer
The answer: the act is under two powers from different aspects—creation (God) and acquisition (servant). Notice how this resolves the contradiction without making the servant a creator.
The term 'jihhah' (aspect) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُفهم من قوله: «كُلَّفا (٦) ردُّ مذهب الجَبْريَّة. (وَلَمْ يَكُنْ) العبدُ (مَؤَثِّرًا) في المَقدُور تأثير اختراع وإيجاد له، ومُراد النَّاظم : أنَّ مذهب أهل السُّنَّة: أنَّ للعبدِ كسباً لأفعاله، يتعلَّق به التكليف. فإن قلت: يرد على مذهب أهل السنة أنه قد قام البرهان على وجوب استقلاله تعالى بخلق الأفعال ، والمقدور الواحد لا يدخل تحت قدرتين. أجيب بأنا نقول لما ثبت بالبرهان أن الخالق هو الله تعالى، وبالضرورة أن لقدرة العبد وإرادته مدخلا في بعض الأفعال كحركة البطش ، دون البعض كحركة الارتعاش.. احتجنا إلى التخلص من هذا المضيق للقول بأن الله خالق والعبد كاسب، وتحقيق الجواب: أنَّ صرف العبد قدرته وإرادته إلى الفعل كسب ، وإيجاد الله تعالى الفعل عقب ذلك الصرف خلق ، والمقدور الواحد داخل تحت قدرتين بجهتين مختلفتين، فتحت قدرة الله تعالى بجهة الخلق ، وتحت قدرة العبد بجهة الكسب . الشنواني.
And it is understood from his saying: ‘charged’ (6) the refutation of the doctrine of Jabriyya. And the servant is not an effecter in the thing within capacity with the effect of invention and bringing into existence. And the intent of the author is that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna is that the servant has acquisition of his acts, and obligation is attached to it. For if you say: An objection arises against the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna that the proof has already established the necessity of His independence in creating actions, and one thing within capacity cannot fall under two powers. The answer: we say since it is established by proof that the Creator is God, and by necessity that the servant’s power and will have an entry in some acts like the movement of striking, not others like the movement of trembling, we needed to escape from this strait by saying that God is Creator and the servant is acquirer. And the realization of the answer: that the servant’s directing his power and will to the act is acquisition, and God’s bringing the act into existence after that directing is creation, and the one thing within capacity falls under two powers from two different aspects: under God’s power by aspect of creation, and under the servant’s power by aspect of acquisition. Al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage addresses two interconnected issues: the refutation of the Jabriyya (those who deny human agency entirely) and the resolution of a logical objection against the Ash'ari position. First, the author notes that the line 'charged' (كلفا) indicates a rejection of Jabriyya, because charging implies responsibility, which would be meaningless if the servant had no role. Then the author states the Sunni position: the servant is not an effecter in the sense of inventing or creating the action; rather, the servant has kasb (acquisition) of his acts, and obligation (taklif) is attached to that kasb. An objection is then raised: rational proof shows God alone creates actions, and one action cannot be produced by two agents (since that would entail contradiction or redundancy). How then can both God and the servant be involved? The answer explains that the servant's directing (sarf) of his power and will toward the act is called kasb, and God's bringing the act into existence after that directing is called khalq (creation). Thus, the same act falls under two powers from two different aspects (bi-jihhatayn mukhtalifatayn): under God's power by the aspect of creation, and under the servant's power by the aspect of acquisition. This preserves God's sole creativity while affirming human involvement as the basis of responsibility, without making the servant a creator. The commentator al-Shanawani is cited for this response.

Key Terms
الجبرية
كسب
خلق
مؤثر
قدرة
إرادة
صرف
جهتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kasb means the servant creates his own actions.
Correction: Kasb is not creative; only God creates. The servant directs his power and will, but existence comes from Allah.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari response resolve the objection that one act cannot be under two powers?
A) By denying that God creates human acts.
B) By asserting that human power is illusory.
C) By stating the act is under two powers from different aspects: creation and acquisition.
D) By claiming the objection applies only to the Jabriyya.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari solution to the 'one act, two powers' problem is to distinguish between creation (khalq) by Allah and acquisition (kasb) by the servant, allowing the same act to be caused from two different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform is both from you and from Allah? How does this affect your sense of responsibility and reliance on Allah? What is one habit you can change today to align your will with His creation?
?
When you commit a sin, do you blame Allah's decree or your own choice? According to this lesson, your act is acquired by you—how does that make you accountable? Will you repent now for any sin you have excused by fate?
?
The passage says the servant directs his power and will to the act. Is your will sincerely directed towards obedience? What is one area where you are not fully directing your will to please Allah, and what step will you take to correct it?

The Mu'tazilite First Statement: God Must Do al-Salah
Refutation and explanation of the first Mu'tazilite statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to perform what is good (al-Salah) and avoid corruption (al-Fasad), and the author's refutation of this claim.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila imposed a duty on God that He does not impose on Himself.

The author begins by refuting the Mu'tazilite principle that God must do what is best for His servants. This lesson focuses on their first statement, which obligates God to do what is good (al-Salah) and avoid what is corrupt (al-Fasad). By understanding this claim, you will see how the Mu'tazila reasoned and why the author declares it false.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage once, noting the author's decisive refutation (زور) and the commentator's explanation of the first statement.
The passage includes the author's verse and the footnote that sets up the two statements.
2
Identify the core claim of the first statement
The first statement says that God must do al-Salah (the good) for His servants. Notice how al-Salah is opposed to al-Fasad (corruption), like faith versus disbelief.
3
Understand the logical implication
The Mu'tazila claim that if there are two options—one good and one corrupt—God is obliged to choose the good and shield the servant from the corrupt. This imposes a moral obligation on God.
4
Reflect on the author's refutation
The author dismisses this as false (زور). Ask yourself why this claim is problematic from an Ahl al-Sunnah perspective: it limits God's will and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض قاعدة الصلاح والأصلح الاعتزالية
والأصلح ، فقال : (وَقَوْلُهُمْ) أي : المعتزلة ، وإن لم يتقدَّم لهم ذكرٌ ; لِشُهرة هذا المذهب عنهم (إِنَّ الصَّلاحَ) يعني فعله بالعباد وَاجِبٌ عَلَيْهِ تعالى ; فتركُه بُخل وسَفهُ يَستحق به الذَّمَّ، وفعله حكمةٌ ومصلحةٌ يَستحق به المَدْحَ (زُورٌ )
(١) الضمير في قول المصنف: «وقولهم» عائد إلى المعتزلة كما بينه الشارح، وإن لم يتقدَّم في الكلام ذكرهم ; لشهرة هذا المذهب عنهم . واعلم أن للمعتزلة :عبارتين: أولاهما قولهم: فعل الصلاح واجب على الله تعالى» والصلاح في هذه العبارة يقابل الفساد، كالإيمان في مقابل الكفر .
وحاصل مذهبهم على ما تؤديه هذه العبارة : أنه إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر فساد وجبَ على الله تعالى أن يفعل مع العبدِ الصلاح منهما وأنْ يُجنّبه الفساد ، والعبارة الثانية قولهم: فعل الأصلح واجب على الله تعالى للعباد والأصلح في هذه العبارة معناه: الأقوى والأشد في الصلاحية ، ويقابله الصلاح، ككون العبد في أعلى مراتب النعيم في مقابلة كونه في أول مراتبه .
وحاصل مذهبهم على ما تؤديه هذه العبارة : أنه إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر أصلح منه ; وجب على الله تعالى أن يفعل الأصلح منهما دُونَ ما هو صلاح. وللمعتزلة فيما بينهم خلاف في المراد بالصلاح والأصلح ، وسنبينه لك إن شاء الله تعالى
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle of al-salah and al-aslah. And al-aslah, so he said: 'And their saying' meaning the Mu'tazila, even if they were not previously mentioned, due to the fame of this doctrine from them: 'Indeed al-salah' meaning His action towards the servants is obligatory upon Him, Exalted; so abandoning it is stinginess and foolishness deserving blame, and doing it is wisdom and benefit deserving praise, is false. (1) The pronoun in the author's saying 'and their saying' refers to the Mu'tazila as the commentator explains, even if they were not previously mentioned in the discourse, due to the fame of this doctrine from them. Know that the Mu'tazila have two statements: the first is their saying: 'Doing al-salah is obligatory upon God Almighty.' And al-salah in this statement is opposed to al-fasad, like faith in contrast to disbelief. The summary of their doctrine according to this statement is that if there are two matters, one being salah and the other fasad, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to do with the servant the salah of the two and to protect him from fasad. The second statement is their saying: 'Doing al-aslah is obligatory upon God Almighty for the servants.' And al-aslah in this statement means the stronger and more intense in suitability, and it is opposed to al-salah, like the servant being in the highest ranks of bliss in contrast to being in its lowest ranks. The summary of their doctrine according to this statement is that if there are two matters, one being salah and the other aslah, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to do the aslah of the two rather than what is salah. And the Mu'tazila have disagreement among themselves regarding the intended meaning of al-salah and al-aslah, and we will explain it to you, God willing.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the Mu'tazilite principle that God must do what is beneficial for His servants. The author first quotes the verse from the poem, which declares their claim false. Then the commentator explains the pronoun reference and clarifies that the Mu'tazila have two distinct formulations. The first statement obligates God to do al-salah (what is good/right) and avoid al-fasad (corruption) when faced with a choice. The second statement goes further, obligating God to do al-aslah (what is best/more beneficial) rather than merely good. The passage also notes internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila about the precise meaning of these terms. The explanation follows the author's order: first the verse, then the identification of the Mu'tazila, then the two statements with their respective summaries, and finally the mention of internal dispute.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The Mu'tazila have two statements: one regarding salah (good) and another regarding aslah (best). This lesson covers the first.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite first statement about God's obligation?
A) God must do what is best (al-Aslah).

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite first statement falsely imposes on God an obligation to choose good over corruption, a claim the author rejects as false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the Mu'tazilite error of imposing obligation on God manifest in your own heart? ?
When you face trials or losses, do you secretly question God's wisdom or justice, thinking He should have chosen something else for you? This lesson shows that God is not obligated to choose what seems beneficial to you. Will you repent of any hidden resentment and submit to His decree with full contentment?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that God must do the good for His servants, but the Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that God does what He wills, and all His actions are good. In your life, are you confident that God's will, even when it contradicts your preferences, is always perfect? Take a moment to thank Him for His sovereignty and ask for strength to accept His plans with patience.

The Mu'tazilite Second Statement: God Must Do al-Aslah
Explanation of the Mu'tazilite second statement and internal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite second statement that God is obligated to do what is best (al-Aslah) rather than merely good, and the internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila regarding these terms.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila escalated their claim: God must not just do good, but the best possible.

This lesson continues the refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle by examining their second statement. Here, they argue that God is obliged to do not merely the good (al-Salah), but the best (al-Aslah). The passage also hints at disagreements among the Mu'tazila themselves about what these terms mean. Understanding this deeper claim reveals the extent of their rationalist approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for the second statement
Read the text beginning with 'والعبارة الثانية' to the end. Note how al-Aslah is defined as 'stronger and more intense in suitability'.
2
Contrast al-Aslah with al-Salah
In this statement, al-Aslah is opposed to al-Salah, like the highest rank of bliss versus the lowest. So God must choose the superior option.
This goes beyond the first statement which only required avoiding corruption.
3
Understand the obligation hierachy
If there are two good options, one being better, God must do the better one. This imposes a strict requirement of optimality on God.
4
Note the internal disagreement
The passage ends by mentioning that the Mu'tazila disagree among themselves about the meaning of al-Salah and al-Aslah. This shows their doctrine is not monolithic.
The commentator promises to explain further later.

And al-salah in this statement is opposed to al-fasad, like faith in contrast to disbelief. The summary of their doctrine according to this statement is that if there are two matters, one being salah and the other fasad, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to do with the servant the salah of the two and to protect him from fasad. The second statement is their saying: 'Doing al-aslah is obligatory upon God Almighty for the servants.' And al-aslah in this statement means the stronger and more intense in suitability, and it is opposed to al-salah, like the servant being in the highest ranks of bliss in contrast to being in its lowest ranks. The summary of their doctrine according to this statement is that if there are two matters, one being salah and the other aslah, it is obligatory upon God Almighty to do the aslah of the two rather than what is salah. And the Mu'tazila have disagreement among themselves regarding the intended meaning of al-salah and al-aslah, and we will explain it to you, God willing.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the Mu'tazilite principle that God must do what is beneficial for His servants. The author first quotes the verse from the poem, which declares their claim false. Then the commentator explains the pronoun reference and clarifies that the Mu'tazila have two distinct formulations. The first statement obligates God to do al-salah (what is good/right) and avoid al-fasad (corruption) when faced with a choice. The second statement goes further, obligating God to do al-aslah (what is best/more beneficial) rather than merely good. The passage also notes internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila about the precise meaning of these terms. The explanation follows the author's order: first the verse, then the identification of the Mu'tazila, then the two statements with their respective summaries, and finally the mention of internal dispute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazilite principle is simply that God must do good; but here they demand the best.
Correction: The second statement goes further: God must do the best, not just good, which is even more restrictive.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazilite second statement obligate God to do?
A) God must do what is good (al-Salah).
B) God must do what is best (al-Aslah) rather than merely good.
C) God must give the servant the lowest rank in Paradise.
D) God must do what the servant desires.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite second statement escalates the obligation on God to do the best, and the school itself disagrees on the exact meaning of these terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were obliged to always give you the very best, would you still feel gratitude when you receive less? How does your heart respond to the Mu'tazilite claim that God's hands are tied?
?
Do you ever assume that God owes you the better option in your life—a promotion, health, or ease? Where do you get that sense of entitlement? This passage shows a theological error in expecting God to always provide the best. Search your heart: do you hold any such expectation?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that God must do the best for His servants. If you truly believed that, would you ever complain about your circumstances? Examine your own complaints in light of this. What does your dissatisfaction reveal about your view of God?
