Defending the Author's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Doctrine
Identifies two deficiencies in the author's refutation and defends them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two apparent shortcomings in the author's refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrine are logically defended, and why refuting the general (al-salah) necessarily refutes the specific (al-aslah).

Core Idea
Refuting the general necessarily refutes the specific; the author's aim was refutation, not exposition.

The commentator now raises two objections to the author's handling of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, only to answer them convincingly. First, he notes that the author only refuted one of the two Mu'tazilite claims; second, that he ignored their internal disagreements. But as we will see, both objections are met with sound logical reasoning. The passage demonstrates a careful method of theological critique and defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two criticisms and their answers. Notice the logical argument about generality and specificity.
The text ends mid-sentence with 'اختلفوا=' indicating a continuation elsewhere.
2
Identify the two deficiencies
List the two ways in which the author's refutation is said to be lacking: (1) not addressing the second statement, (2) not discussing the Mu'tazilite internal disagreement.
The first deficiency is about scope; the second about depth.
3
Understand the defense for each
The commentator answers the first using the logical principle that negating the general (al-salah) negates the specific (al-aslah). The second is answered by clarifying the author's goal: refutation, not explanation.
The example of animal and human illustrates the logical relationship.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
Consider how the relationship of al-salah (general) to al-aslah (specific) is like animal to human. Why does negating the general necessarily negate the specific?
This is a common logical tool in kalam known as 'necessity of the more general'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تبين لك هذا الموضوع على الوجه الذي شرحناه علمت أنَّ في كلام المصنف نقصا مِن جهتين:
الأولى: أنه تكلم على إبطال مذهبهم كما تدل عليه العبارة الأولى، ولم يتعرض لإبطاله على ما تدل عليه العبارة الثانية .
والجهة الثانية: أنه لم يتعرض لتبيين الخلاف الواقع بينهم في المراد بالصلاح، فأما الجهة الأولى فيمكن الجواب عن التقصير فيها : بأنه لَمَّا أبطل مذهبهم على ما تقتضيه العبارة الأولى لزمه إبطال مذهبهم على ما تقتضيه العبارة الثانية ، ووجه ذلك أنَّ الصلاح أعمُّ مِن الأصلح ، ونفي الأعم يستلزم البيَّة نفي الأخص ، ألا ترى أنني لو نفيت كون هذا الشيء حيوانا لزم البتة أن ينتفى كونه إنسانًا ، وأما الجهة الثانية فيمكن الجواب عن التقصير فيها: بأنَّ غرض المصنف إنَّما تعلّق بإبطال مذهبهم على كل وجوهه، ولم يتعلق ببيان مذهبهم وتحريره. والحاصل أن المعتزلة قالوا: فعل الصلاح والأصلح واجب على الله تعالى ، وأنهم اختلفوا=
And when this topic is clear to you in the manner we have explained, you will know that there is in the author's speech a deficiency from two aspects: The first is that he spoke regarding refuting their doctrine as indicated by the first statement, and he did not address its refutation according to what the second statement indicates. And the second aspect is that he did not address explaining the disagreement that occurred among them regarding the intended meaning of al-salah. As for the first aspect, it is possible to answer the shortcoming in it by saying: when he refuted their doctrine according to what the first statement requires, the refutation of their doctrine according to what the second statement requires necessarily followed. The reason for that is that al-salah is more general than al-aslah, and negating the more general necessarily entails negating the more specific. Do you not see that if I negate that this thing is an animal, it necessarily follows that it is not a human? As for the second aspect, it is possible to answer the shortcoming in it by saying that the author's aim was concerned with refuting their doctrine in all its aspects, not with explaining and formulating their doctrine. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila said: doing al-salah and al-aslah is obligatory upon God Almighty, and that they disagreed...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage identifies two apparent deficiencies in the author's treatment of the Mu'tazilite doctrine and then defends him against them. First, the author refuted only the first statement (about صلاح) but not the second (about أصلح). The defender argues that since صلاح is more general than أصلح, refuting the general necessarily refutes the specific, just as negating 'animal' negates 'human'. Second, the author did not explain the internal Mu'tazilite disagreement about the definitions. The answer is that the author's goal was refutation, not exposition; he aimed to dismantle the doctrine in all its forms, not to detail their internal debates. The passage concludes by reaffirming the Mu'tazilite claim as stated.

العبارة الأولى
العبارة الثانية

نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's refutation is incomplete because it does not explicitly address the second Mu'tazilite statement (al-aslah) and ignores their internal disputes.
Correction: The refutation of the general (al-salah) logically entails refutation of the specific (al-aslah), so separate treatment is unnecessary. The author's aim was refutation, not detailed exposition of their disagreements.

Checkpoint

B) Because al-salah is more general; negating the general negates the specific.
C) Because the author explicitly includes both in his verse.

Key Takeaway
The author's refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine is logically sufficient because refuting the general (al-salah) necessarily refutes the specific (al-aslah), and the author's aim was refutation, not detailed exposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's argument because they didn't address every nuance, even though their broader point logically covered it? How does this lesson's logical principle call you to examine your own fairness in evaluating others? Will you commit to looking for the general before demanding the specific?
?
The author intentionally omitted internal disagreements because his aim was refutation, not exposition. In your own religious discussions, do you clearly define your purpose (refutation, teaching, learning) before speaking? Or do you often mix intentions, leading to confusion? How might clarifying your aim change your conversations?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement (like the Mu'tazila differing among themselves), do you immediately judge a scholar for not mentioning it, assuming negligence or ignorance? This lesson suggests otherwise. How often do you assume bad faith in others' omissions? Can you today commit to assuming the best intention until proven otherwise?

The Mu'tazilite Doctrine of al-Salah and al-Aslah
Commentator defines the Mu'tazilite two statements on al-salah and al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct Mu'tazilite claims that Allah must do what is beneficial (al-salah) and what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants, and why these claims are pivotal in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claimed Allah is obligated to do what is best for us.

Before engaging with the Sunni refutation, we must first precisely grasp what the Mu'tazila actually asserted. This passage from the commentator lays out their two distinct statements, both based on the idea that Allah owes optimal benefit to His creation. Understanding these statements is essential for following the theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is part of a refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazilite principle. Start by reading the Arabic and translation to see how the commentator introduces the two statements.
The author uses a short line of poetry as a reference point.
2
Identify the two statements
Find the two separate statements attributed to the Mu'tazila: one regarding al-salah (mere benefit) and one regarding al-aslah (greater benefit). Notice how each is defined.
The first statement pairs salah with fasad (corruption); the second pairs aslah with salah.
3
Analyze the logical implications
Consider what each statement implies about Allah's freedom and obligation. The Mu'tazila assert that failing to do salah or aslah would make Allah blameworthy—a claim Sunni theologians reject.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: does Allah owe any creature the best possible outcome? This question lies at the heart of the Sunni-Mu'tazilite debate on divine justice.
The commentator hints that further explanation of the terms will follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض قاعدة الصلاح والأصلح الاعتزالية
والأصلح ، فقال : (وَقَوْلُهُمْ) أي : المعتزلة ، وإن لم يتقدَّم لهم ذكرٌ ؛ لِشُهرة هذا
المذهب عنهم (إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ) يعني فعله بالعباد وَاجِبٌ عَلَيْهِ تعالى ؛ فتركُه بُخل وسَفهُ يَستحق به الذَّمَّ، وفعله حكمةٌ ومصلحةٌ يَستحق به المَدْحَ (زُورٌ ) (۱)
(۱) الضمير في قول المصنف: «وقولهم» عائد إلى المعتزلة كما بينه الشارح، وإن لم يتقدَّم في الكلام ذكرهم ؛ لشهرة هذا المذهب عنهم . واعلم أن للمعتزلة :عبارتين: أولاهما قولهم: فعل الصلاح واجب على الله تعالى» والصلاح في هذه العبارة يقابل الفساد، كالإيمان في مقابل الكفر .
وحاصل مذهبهم على ما تؤديه هذه العبارة : أنه إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر فساد وجبَ على الله تعالى أن يفعل مع العبدِ الصلاح منهما وأنْ يُجنّبه الفساد ، والعبارة الثانية قولهم: فعل الأصلح واجب على الله تعالى للعباد والأصلح في هذه العبارة معناه: الأقوى والأشد في الصلاحية ، ويقابله الصلاح، ككون العبد في أعلى مراتب النعيم في مقابلة كونه في أول مراتبه .
وحاصل مذهبهم على ما تؤديه هذه العبارة : أنه إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر أصلح منه ؛ وجب على الله تعالى أن يفعل الأصلح منهما دُونَ ما هو صلاح. وللمعتزلة فيما بينهم خلاف في المراد بالصلاح والأصلح ، وسنبينه لك إن شاء الله تعالى
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle of al-salah and al-aslah. And al-aslah, and He said: 'And their statement' i.e. the Mu'tazila, even though they were not mentioned previously, due to the fame of this doctrine among them: 'Indeed al-salah' meaning His doing it for the servants is obligatory upon Him, may He be exalted; so abandoning it is stinginess and foolishness deserving blame, and doing it is wisdom and benefit deserving praise, 'is falsehood.' (1) (1) The pronoun in the author's statement 'And their statement' refers to the Mu'tazila as the commentator explained, even though they were not mentioned earlier in the discourse, due to the fame of this doctrine among them. And know that the Mu'tazila have two statements: The first is their saying: 'Doing al-salah is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted.' And al-salah in this statement is opposite to al-fasad (corruption/evil), like faith in contrast to disbelief. And the essence of their doctrine according to what this statement conveys: that if there are two matters, one is salah and the other is fasad, then it is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, to do with the servant the salah of the two and to keep him away from fasad. And the second statement is their saying: 'Doing al-aslah (the more beneficial) is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, for the servants.' And al-aslah in this statement means the stronger and more intense in beneficialness, and its opposite is al-salah, like the servant being in the highest ranks of bliss in contrast to being in its lowest ranks. And the essence of their doctrine according to what this statement conveys: that if there are two matters, one is salah and the other is aslah from it, then it is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, to do the aslah of the two rather than what is merely salah. And the Mu'tazila have among themselves a disagreement regarding the intended meaning of al-salah and al-aslah, and we will explain it to you, God willing.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-salah (the beneficial) and al-aslah (the more beneficial). The commentator begins by stating that the author's phrase 'and their statement' refers to the Mu'tazila. He then explains that the Mu'tazila have two distinct statements on this matter. The first statement holds that doing al-salah for the servants is obligatory upon God, where al-salah is the opposite of corruption (al-fasad). Its essence: when faced with a beneficial option and a corrupting one, God must choose the beneficial and spare the servant from corruption. The second statement goes further: doing al-aslah (the most beneficial) is obligatory, meaning the strongest in benefit, with al-salah as its lesser counterpart. Its essence: when faced with a merely beneficial option and a more beneficial one, God must choose the more beneficial. The commentator notes that the Mu'tazila themselves differ over the precise meanings of al-salah and al-aslah, and that this will be explained later. The passage thus lays out the foundational Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to act for the optimal benefit of His servants.

Key Terms
الصلاح (al-salah)
الأصلح (al-aslah)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
واجب (wajib/obligatory)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila only claimed that God must do what is generally good for people, without specifying that He must do the absolute best.
Correction: In fact, the Mu'tazila had two distinct levels: first, God must do al-salah (beneficial) over corruption; second, He must do al-aslah (the most beneficial) over mere benefit. Their doctrine thus imposes a higher obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite second statement, what must God do when there are two options, one beneficial and the other more beneficial?
A) He must do the beneficial only.
B) He must do the more beneficial.
C) He may choose either.
D) He must abstain from both.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held that Allah must do not just what is good, but what is best for His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Mu'tazilite view were true, that Allah must always do the most beneficial for you, how would you react when you face a trial or loss that seems utterly harmful? Would you believe Allah has failed in His obligation, or would you reinterpret the trial as secretly more beneficial? Examine your own heart: have you ever felt entitled to God's favor because of your deeds? How does the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated challenge or comfort you? Commit to surrendering your expectation of what is 'best' to God's wisdom, even when it hurts.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God must do what is wise and beneficial, otherwise He would be deserving of blame. Think of a time when you blamed God—either silently or aloud—for something that happened to you. What does this lesson reveal about the danger of imposing our standards of 'good' on God? Are you willing to let go of the assumption that you know what is best for you? Resolve today to trust that God's choice is always the wisest, even when you cannot see it.
?
The passage notes that the Mu'tazila themselves disagreed on the meaning of al-salah and al-aslah. This reminds us that even within a deviant sect, there is confusion. Have you ever been in a state of confusion about God's nature or actions, trying to figure out what He 'should' do? How does this lesson refocus your attention from what you think God ought to do to what He has revealed about Himself? Take a moment to thank Allah that He is not bound by our limited understanding, and ask Him to increase your trust in His perfect wisdom.

Internal Dispute of the Mu'tazila on al-Salah and al-Aslah
Commentator details the disagreement between Baghdad and Basra Mu'tazila on al-salah and al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal disagreements among the Mu'tazila regarding the obligation of al-salah and al-aslah, including the differences between the Baghdad and Basra schools on scope and definition.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila agreed God must do al-salah and al-aslah, but split on its scope and meaning.

The Mu'tazila are often presented as a unified theological school, yet they had significant internal disputes. One key area of disagreement was the obligation of God to do what is beneficial (al-salah) and most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. In this passage, the commentator outlines the two main positions: the Baghdad school and the Basra school differed on both the scope of this obligation and the definition of al-aslah. We also see the shared core of the Mu'tazilite position and the commentator's critique of their method.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the internal dispute. Notice the different positions.
2
Compare the Baghdad and Basra views on scope
The Baghdad school says God must consider both religious and worldly benefit; the Basra school says only religious benefit. Why might they differ?
3
Contrast the definitions of al-aslah
Baghdad defines al-aslah as what is most fitting in wisdom; Basra defines it as the most beneficial. These reflect different understandings of God's actions.
4
Analyze the shared core position
Despite differences, all Mu'tazila agree that God must empower the servant and do everything possible to lead to faith. Note the implication that God cannot do more for disbelievers.
5
Consider the methodological critique
The commentator criticizes the Mu'tazila for relying on analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible. This is a key point in the Ash'ari critique of Mu'tazilite theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن المعتزلة قالوا: فعل الصلاح والأصلح واجب على الله تعالى ، وأنهم اختلفوا في هذه المسألة
فقال معتزلة بغداد: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لعباده بالنظر إلى الدين والدُّنيا جميعاً، وقال معتزلة البصرة: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح بالنظر إلى الدين وحده، واختلفوا في المراد بالأصلح ؛ فذهب أهل بغداد إلى أنَّ المراد به الأَوْفَق في الحكمة والتدبير ، وذهب معتزلة البصرة إلى أنه الأنفع والأكثر فائدة. وحاصل هذه المسألة: أن المعتزلة جميعاً ذهبوا إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى إقدار العبد : وتمكينه وأن يفعل معه أقصى ما يمكن في معلومه سبحانه مما يؤمن عنده المكلَّف ويطِيعُ، وأنه سبحانه فَعَلَ مع كلِّ أحدٍ غاية مقدوره من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعله بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً ، وإلا لكان تركه بُخْلًا منه وسَفَها، وعمدتهم القُصْوَى في قياس الغائب على الشاهد ؛ لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووُفُورِ غلَطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغني المطلق
And the summary is that the Mu'tazila said: Doing al-salah and al-aslah is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, and that they differed on this issue. The Mu'tazila of Baghdad said: It is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, to observe al-salah and al-aslah for His servants with regard to both religion and this world together. And the Mu'tazila of Basra said: It is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, to observe al-salah and al-aslah with regard to religion alone. And they differed regarding the intended meaning of al-aslah: the people of Baghdad held that what is meant is what is most fitting in wisdom and planning, while the Mu'tazila of Basra held that it is the most beneficial and most advantageous. And the summary of this issue is that all the Mu'tazila held that it is obligatory upon Allah, may He be exalted, to empower the servant and enable him, and to do with him the utmost of what is possible within His knowledge, may He be glorified, of what would cause the morally responsible person to believe and obey, and that He, glorified, did with everyone the utmost of His power from al-aslah, and that there is nothing within His power of a kindness that, if He did it to the disbelievers, they would all believe; otherwise, its abandonment would be stinginess and foolishness from Him. And their ultimate reliance is on analogizing the unseen to the witnessed, due to the deficiency of their vision in divine knowledge and hidden lordly subtleties, and the abundance of their errors in the attributes of the True Necessary Being and the actions of the Absolutely Self-Sufficient.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage classifies the internal disagreements among the Mu'tazila regarding the obligation of al-salah and al-aslah. The commentator first states that all Mu'tazila agree that God must do al-salah and al-aslah, but they differ on two fronts: (1) the scope of this obligation—the Baghdad school holds that God must consider both religious and worldly benefit for the servant, while the Basra school limits the obligation to religious benefit only; (2) the meaning of al-aslah—Baghdad interprets it as what is most fitting in wisdom and planning, while Basra interprets it as the most beneficial and advantageous. The commentator then summarizes the shared core of the Mu'tazilite position: God must empower the servant, enable him, and do everything possible within His knowledge to lead the morally responsible person to faith and obedience. God has already done the utmost possible for everyone, and if He had more kindness to give to disbelievers, He would have done it; otherwise, withholding it would be stinginess and foolishness. The commentator criticizes their method as relying on qiyas al-gaib 'ala al-shahid (analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible), which leads to error due to their lack of understanding of divine attributes and actions.

Key Terms
معتزلة بغداد (Mu'tazila of Baghdad)
معتزلة البصرة (Mu'tazila of Basra)
الصلاح (al-salah)
الأصلح (al-aslah)
قياس الغائب على الشاهد (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid/analogizing the unseen to the witnessed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila held the same view on God's obligation to do what is most beneficial.
Correction: In fact, the Baghdad and Basra schools differed on both the scope (religion+world vs religion only) and the meaning of al-aslah.

Checkpoint
What was the view of the Mu'tazila of Basra regarding the scope of God's obligation to observe al-salah and al-aslah?
A) Both religious and worldly matters
B) Religious matters only
C) Worldly matters only
D) Neither religious nor worldly matters

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila were not monolithic; they internally debated whether God's obligation to provide benefit extends to worldly affairs and what 'most beneficial' means, but they all shared the core belief that God must do the utmost to guide every person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God has done everything in His power to guide every person—including those who reject Him—how does that affect your view of your own role in conveying Islam? Are you tempted to think that if God has already done His utmost, your efforts to guide others are unnecessary?
?
Reflect on a time you blamed someone else for their disbelief or sin. After learning that the Mu'tazila held God must do the utmost for guidance, do you find yourself leaning toward justifying disobedience by saying 'God didn't guide them'? How does the commentator's criticism of qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid challenge your own assumptions about God's actions?
?
The Mu'tazila of Basra limited God's obligation to religious benefit alone, while Baghdad included worldly benefit. Which view feels more comfortable to you—one where God owes you worldly success as well as spiritual guidance? Ask yourself: Are you demanding from God things that belong solely to His wisdom and lordship, as if He were a servant obligated to provide for you in this world?

Mu'tazilite Schools on al-Salah wa al-Aslah
The author presents the two Mu'tazilite schools on al-salah wa al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Baghdad and Basra Mu'tazilite schools regarding the obligation of al-salah wa al-aslah and its definition.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites agreed on obligation but disagreed on scope and meaning of 'most proper'.

Before we examine the Sunni refutation, we must first understand what the Mu'tazilites actually claimed. This passage introduces two major internal divisions: the Baghdad school held that God must observe what is proper in both religion and worldly affairs, while the Basra school restricted it to religion alone. They also differed on the meaning of 'aslah'—the former said it is the most fitting in wisdom, the latter said it is the most beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic text and literal translation to get a sense of the author's presentation of the Mu'tazilite positions.
Notice the two groups mentioned explicitly.
2
Identify the two schools
Locate the names 'معتزلة بغداد' and 'معتزلة البصرة'. Note their distinct claims about the scope of obligation (religion and world vs. religion alone).
These are the main branches of Mu'tazilism.
3
Compare the definitions of al-aslah
Find how each school defined 'aslah': one as 'al-awfaq fi al-hikma wa al-tadbir' and the other as 'al-anfa' wa al-akthar fa'ida'. Understand the nuance.
This difference affects the broader argument.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: if God is obliged to do what is 'most proper', does that limit His freedom? The next lesson will address their proof.
This sets up the controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
هذه المسألة
فقال معتزلة بغداد: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لعباده بالنظر إلى الدين والدُّنيا جميعاً، وقال معتزلة البصرة: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح بالنظر إلى الدين وحده، واختلفوا في المراد بالأصلح؛ فذهب أهل بغداد إلى أنَّ المراد به الأَوْفَق في الحكمة والتدبير، وذهب معتزلة البصرة إلى أنه الأنفع والأكثر فائدة. وحاصل هذه المسألة: أن المعتزلة جميعاً ذهبوا إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى إقدار العبد وتمكينه وأن يفعل معه أقصى ما يمكن في معلومه سبحانه مما يؤمن عنده المكلَّف ويطِيعُ، وأنه سبحانه فَعَلَ مع كلِّ أحدٍ غاية مقدوره من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعله بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بُخْلًا منه وسَفَها، وعمدتهم القُصْوَى في قياس الغائب على الشاهد؛ لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووُفُورِ غلَطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغني المطلق، قالوا: نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم بطاعته وقَدَرَ على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك؛ ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء مَعدُودًا في زمرة البخلاء، وكذلك من دعا عدوه إلى الموالاة والرُّجوع إلى الطاعة لا يجوز أن يعامله بالغِلْظَةِ والدِّينِ؛ لأنَّه ينفر ولا يقبل، وإنما يعامله بما أَنْجَعُ في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضا فإنَّ من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره وعلم أنه لو تَلَقَّاه ببشر وطلاقةِ وَجْه دخل وأكل، وإلا فلا؛ فالواجب عليه أن يتلقاه بالبشر والطلاقة والملاطفة، لا بأضدادها.
Section on Divine Matters. This issue: The Mu'tazilites of Baghdad said: It is obligatory upon God Almighty to observe what is proper and most proper for His servants with regard to religion and the world together. The Mu'tazilites of Basra said: It is obligatory upon God Almighty to observe what is proper and most proper with regard to religion alone. They differed regarding the meaning of 'most proper': the Baghdad school held that it means the most fitting in wisdom and administration; the Basran Mu'tazilites held that it means the most beneficial and most advantageous. The conclusion of this issue is that all Mu'tazilites held that it is obligatory upon God Almighty to empower the servant and enable him, and to do with him the utmost possible in His knowledge, such that the responsible person would believe and obey; and that He, exalted, has done with everyone the utmost of His power of what is most proper, and there is no kindness in His power such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would all believe; otherwise, His omission would be miserliness and foolishness from Him. Their ultimate reliance is on analogizing the unseen to the witnessed, due to their deficient perception in divine knowledge and hidden lordly subtleties, and their abundant error in the attributes of the necessary Truth and the actions of the absolutely Independent. They said: We are certain that a wise person, if he is obeyed and is able to give the commanded person what enables him to attain obedience without harm, and then does not do so, he is blamed by the rational and counted among the miserly. Similarly, whoever calls his enemy to alliance and return to obedience, it is not permissible to treat him with harshness and severity because that repels and is not accepted; rather, he should treat him with what is more effective in achieving the desired goal and more conducive to abandoning obstinacy. Also, whoever prepares a banquet for a man and invites him, and knows that if he receives him with cheerfulness and a smiling face he will enter and eat, otherwise not, then it is obligatory upon him to receive him with cheerfulness, openness, and gentleness, not their opposites.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-salah wa al-aslah (what is proper and most proper). The author first distinguishes between the Baghdad and Basra schools: Baghdad holds that God must observe what is proper for both religious and worldly affairs, while Basra limits it to religious affairs. They also differ on the definition of 'aslah': Baghdad says it means the most fitting in wisdom, Basra says it means the most beneficial. Then the author summarizes the common Mu'tazilite position: God must empower the servant and do everything possible to ensure obedience; He has done so already; any failure would be miserliness. Their main proof is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogizing the unseen to the witnessed), comparing God to a human wise person who is obligated to provide means for obedience. The author then lists three analogies: a wise person providing means, a caller to obedience using gentleness, and a host receiving guests with cheerfulness. The Mu'tazilites conclude that God must likewise act. The passage thus sets up the Mu'tazilite argument that the Sunni refutation will address.

Key Terms
الصلاح والأصلح
معتزلة بغداد
معتزلة البصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites held the same view on al-aslah.
Correction: This passage shows a clear internal split: the Baghdad and Basra schools differed on both the scope and definition of al-aslah.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites of Basra, what is the scope of the obligation of al-aslah?
A) Both religion and the world
B) Religion alone
C) The world alone
D) Neither religion nor the world

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites were not a monolithic group; they disagreed on whether God must ensure al-salah wa al-aslah in both religion and world, and on what 'aslah' means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious teaching as unanimous, only to later discover that scholars differed? How does the Mu'tazilite internal disagreement challenge you to dig deeper into theological issues before forming a fixed opinion?
?
This lesson shows that even within one school (Mu'tazilism), there were significant differences. When you encounter a theological issue, do you genuinely seek to understand the range of views, or do you tend to settle for the first opinion you hear? What would it cost you to remain unaware of such nuances?

Mu'tazilite Proof via Analogical Reasoning
The author summarizes the common Mu'tazilite doctrine and presents their proof by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God must do what is most proper, based on analogical reasoning from human behavior.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites compare God to a wise human to argue divine obligation.

After presenting the two schools, the author now gives the common Mu'tazilite conclusion and their main proof. They claim that God has done everything possible to make people believe, and if He had not, He would be miserly. Their proof relies on qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid—analogizing the unseen (God) to the witnessed (human beings). They provide three analogies from everyday life to support their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion first
Read the portion starting from 'وحاصل هذه المسألة' to understand the shared Mu'tazilite claim: God must empower and enable, and He has done the utmost.
Notice they affirm God's power but say He is obliged to use it in a certain way.
2
Identify the proof method
Look for 'قياس الغائب على الشاهد' and understand that this is their central logical method.
This method is controversial in Sunni theology.
3
Examine the three analogies
Find the examples: the wise person providing means, the caller to obedience using gentleness, and the host receiving guests with cheerfulness. Note how each analogy is meant to show that failure to act appropriately would be blameworthy.
The author mentions these to illustrate the Mu'tazilite reasoning.
4
Ask: why is this problematic?
Reflect on the Sunni critique: can we compare God to humans? The next lessons will address the refutation.
This passage sets up the debate.

Source Text

فقال معتزلة بغداد: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لعباده بالنظر إلى الدين والدُّنيا جميعاً، وقال معتزلة البصرة: يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح بالنظر إلى الدين وحده، واختلفوا في المراد بالأصلح؛ فذهب أهل بغداد إلى أنَّ المراد به الأَوْفَق في الحكمة والتدبير، وذهب معتزلة البصرة إلى أنه الأنفع والأكثر فائدة. وحاصل هذه المسألة: أن المعتزلة جميعاً ذهبوا إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى إقدار العبد وتمكينه وأن يفعل معه أقصى ما يمكن في معلومه سبحانه مما يؤمن عنده المكلَّف ويطِيعُ، وأنه سبحانه فَعَلَ مع كلِّ أحدٍ غاية مقدوره من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعله بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بُخْلًا منه وسَفَها، وعمدتهم القُصْوَى في قياس الغائب على الشاهد؛ لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووُفُورِ غلَطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغني المطلق، قالوا: نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم بطاعته وقَدَرَ على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك؛ ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء مَعدُودًا في زمرة البخلاء، وكذلك من دعا عدوه إلى الموالاة والرُّجوع إلى الطاعة لا يجوز أن يعامله بالغِلْظَةِ والدِّينِ؛ لأنَّه ينفر ولا يقبل، وإنما يعامله بما أَنْجَعُ في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضا فإنَّ من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره وعلم أنه لو تَلَقَّاه ببشر وطلاقةِ وَجْه دخل وأكل، وإلا فلا؛ فالواجب عليه أن يتلقاه بالبشر والطلاقة والملاطفة، لا بأضدادها.

Key Terms
الصلاح والأصلح
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites denied God's power or free will.
Correction: They affirmed that God is powerful, but argued that His power is constrained by what is obligatory (al-aslah). They did not deny divine power itself.

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof method the Mu'tazilites use to argue that God must do al-aslah?
A) Direct revelation from the Qur'an
B) Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Logical contradiction from atheism

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that God must do what is most proper for His servants, using analogies from human behavior as proof—a method that Sunni theologians reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or find obedience difficult, do you ever blame Allah for not giving you enough ability or ease? The Mu'tazilites argued that God must do everything possible for you. But this lesson shows their method is flawed. Do you truly believe Allah has granted you sufficient means, and that your struggle is a test of your sincerity? What would change in your daily worship if you took full responsibility for your choices instead of expecting God to remove every obstacle?
?
The Mu'tazilites compared God to a host who must greet a guest cheerfully to ensure he enters. Do you treat your relationship with Allah as if He must accommodate your preferences and make everything easy for you? How does this contradict the reality that Allah sometimes uses hardship to draw you closer? Are you willing to accept that His wisdom may require patience and struggle, even when you don't understand?
?
Consider the analogy of the caller to reconciliation: harshness repels, gentleness attracts. Yet Allah describes Himself as severe in punishment (shadeed al-'iqab). Does this disturb you? Do you trust that His severity is perfectly balanced with His mercy, and that He knows what will ultimately bring you to Him, even if it feels harsh? How can you cultivate trust in His methods rather than demanding that He act according to your human standards of wisdom?

The Two Qadariyya Sects and Their Refutation
Distinguishes two Qadariyya sects and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the two Qadariyya sects and why the author's refutation targets only the first.

Core Idea
The first Qadariyya denied Allah's eternal knowledge; the second only made humans creators of their own acts.

This passage clarifies a common confusion: not all Qadariyya held the same beliefs. Some denied Allah's foreknowledge, leading to kufr, while others affirmed it but overemphasized human agency. Sanusi distinguishes them to precisely address the first sect's error, while noting the second was refuted earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the enumeration markers 'أما إحداهما' and 'وأما الأخرى'.
2
Identify the two sects and their key differences
The passage presents two sects. Find each one and note their specific beliefs regarding Allah's knowledge and human agency.
The first sect denies eternal knowledge; the second affirms it but claims humans create their own actions.
3
Understand the author's refutation intent
The author clarifies that his refutation in this passage is only for the first sect. The second sect was already refuted earlier. Why? Because the first sect's belief is kufr and extinct, while the second's is a lesser error.
The commentator adds that the Ash'ari-Maturidi difference is merely verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما إحداهما: فطائفةٌ نَفتْ علم الله تعالى أزلا بأفعال العباد، وزعمت أنه لا يعلمها إلا حال وقوعها، وللتمييز بينها وبين الطائفة الأخرى أُطلق عليها اسم «القدرية الأولى» ومُعتقد هذه الطائفة كفرُ عند أهل السنة والجماعة ، ويذكر العلماء أنَّ هذه الطائفة قد انقرضت ولم يبقَ من يذهب مذهبها قبل انقضاء القرن الثاني الهجري. وأما الأخرى فطائفةٌ من المعتزلة ، وهم يعترفون بأنَّ الله تعالى يعلم أفعال العباد قبل وقوعها، يعلمها أزلاً قبل أن يخلق الخلق، ولكنَّهم قالوا العبد خالقُ أفعال نفسه الاختيارية، وهذه الطائفة تسمى «القدريَّة الثَّانية» تمييزاً بينها وبين الطائفة الأولى. إذا علمت هذا على هذا البيان فنقول : أراد المصنف بهذا الكلام الرَّدَّ على القدرية الأُولى، ولم يُرِدْ به الرَّدَّ على القدرية الثانية ؛ لأنَّ الرَّدَّ على القدرية الثانية قد مضى في قوله: «وخَالِقٌ لعَبْدِه ومَا عَمِل» وفي قوله : (وَعِنْدَنَا للعَبْدِ كَسْبٌ كُلفا به». وقد بين الشارح - ه - معنى القَدَرِ عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية ، وبيَّن أنه ليس بين الفريقين خلاف حقيقي ، وإنما الخلاف في العبارة التي دلّ بها كل فريق على مراده، فأما المعنى فهم على اتفاق فيه.
As for one of them: a group that denied God's eternal knowledge of the actions of servants, and claimed that He only knows them at the time of their occurrence. To distinguish between them and the other group, they were called 'the first Qadariyya'. The belief of this group is disbelief according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a. Scholars mention that this group became extinct and no one followed their way before the end of the second century AH. As for the other, it is a group from the Mu'tazila, and they acknowledge that God knows the actions of servants before they occur, knowing them eternally before He created creation, but they said that the servant is the creator of his own voluntary actions. This group is called 'the second Qadariyya' to distinguish between them and the first group. If you know this according to this explanation, we say: The author intended by this speech to refute the first Qadariyya, and he did not intend thereby to refute the second Qadariyya; because the refutation of the second Qadariyya has already passed in his statement: 'And Creator of His servant and what he did' and in his statement: 'And with us for the servant is acquisition (kasb) imposed upon him.' The commentator has explained the meaning of al-qadar according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis, and explained that there is no real disagreement between the two groups, rather the disagreement is in the expression by which each group indicated its intention; as for the meaning, they are in agreement on it.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the two Qadariyya sects. The first Qadariyya denied God's eternal knowledge of human actions, claiming He only knows them as they happen. This belief is declared kufr by Ahl al-Sunna. Scholars note this sect became extinct by the end of the second century. The second Qadariyya, from the Mu'tazila, affirmed God's eternal knowledge but held that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. The author clarifies that his refutation here targets only the first Qadariyya, as the second's refutation appeared earlier in the text. The commentator adds that the Ash'ari-Maturidi difference is merely verbal, not real.

Key Terms
القدرية الأولى
القدرية الثانية
الكفر
المعتزلة
علم الله أزلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Qadariyya hold the same belief.
Correction: This passage distinguishes two distinct Qadariyya sects with different beliefs: the first denied Allah's eternal knowledge (kufr), while the second affirmed it but claimed human creation of actions.

Checkpoint
What is the belief of the first Qadariyya sect according to this passage?
A) They deny God's eternal knowledge of human actions.
B) They affirm God's eternal knowledge but say humans create their own actions.
C) They deny God's creation of human actions.
D) They affirm God's eternal knowledge and that God creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
The first Qadariyya's denial of God's eternal knowledge is kufr and the sect is extinct; the second Qadariyya's error (human creation of actions) is addressed elsewhere, and the Ash'ari-Maturidi difference is only verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God does not know your future choices, even for a moment? How does this lesson challenge that attitude and what will you do today to live with the certainty that He knows everything you will do?
?
The first Qadariyya's denial of God's eternal knowledge was deemed kufr. Do you truly believe that Allah knows every detail of your life, including your sins and your obedience? If yes, how should that belief change the way you approach repentance and reliance on Him?
?
The lesson says the disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis on qadar is only verbal. Are there theological disagreements you hold that are more about wording than substance? How can you this week seek unity with a fellow Muslim who differs on a verbal point?

The Meaning of Hawa and Its Consequences on Prophethood
Commentary on hawa and the positions of groups on prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological term 'hawa' as false inclination and how it leads to either denying or requiring the sending of messengers.

Core Idea
Hawa is any false belief that turns one from truth, dictating one's stance on prophethood.

This passage from the commentary on the poem 'Ithāf al-Murīd' explains a crucial term in the discussion of prophethood. The author clarifies that 'hawa' refers to the false beliefs that devils adorn for people, causing them to fall into innovation or disbelief. Understanding this term is key to grasping why some groups reject prophethood entirely while others impose it based on reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author explains the line from the poem.
2
Identify the two positions on prophethood
Look for the two groups mentioned: those who deny sending messengers (Sumaniyya) and those who require it (Mu'tazila and philosophers).
The passage links these positions to the 'hawa' of the people.
3
Define 'hawa' in general
Find the general definition of hawa: inclination towards opposition of truth. Note the Quranic verse cited.
This definition is broader than mere lust.
4
Reflect on the etymology
Consider why hawa is so named: because it causes its possessor to fall (yahwi) into the Fire.
This reinforces its dangerous nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات

عنكَ هَوَى قَوْمِ اتَّبعوه، أي: اعتقادَهُم الباطل الَّذِي زيَّنه الشَّياطين لهم، فإنَّه بِهِمْ قَدْ لَعِبَا الهوى، أي: تلاعب بهم لا بغيرهم؛ فأوقعهم في البدع والمعاصي أو الكفر، فأنكروا الإرسال وأحالوه، كالسُّمَنِيَّة (۱) ، أو أوجبوه كالمعتزلة والحكماء والهوى عند الإطلاق ينصرف إلى الميل إلى خلاف الحقِّ غالبا، نحو: ﴿وَلَا تَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى﴾ [ ص: ٢٦] سُمِّي هوى؛ لأنَّه يهوي بصاحبه في النَّار.

(۱) السُّمَنية بضم السين وفتح الميم ، طائفة منسوبة إلى «سومنات» وهو اسم لصنم عظيم من أصنام الهنود ومعناه: صاحب القمر ، وقد هدمه مرة السلطان محمود الغزنوي عام (٤١٦ هـ) فهم قوم من عبدة الأوثان قائلون بالتناسخ، وبأنَّه لا طريق للعلم سوى الحس، فهم ينكرون حصول اليقين بغير الحس.
Section of prophethood. 'From you, the hawa of a people who followed him' means their false belief that the devils adorned for them, for indeed hawa has played with them, i.e., toyed with them, not with others; so it caused them to fall into innovations, sins, or disbelief. Thus they denied and rejected sending (messengers), like the Sumaniyya (1), or they required it like the Mu'tazila and the philosophers. And hawa, when unqualified, generally refers to inclination towards opposition of truth, as in {And do not follow hawa} [ص: ٢٦]. It is called hawa because it causes its possessor to fall into the Fire.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem, explaining the meaning of 'hawa' (whim/false desire) in the context of those who reject or distort prophethood. The author states that 'hawa' here refers to the false belief that devils have adorned for the people, causing them to fall into innovation, sin, or even disbelief. Consequently, some groups deny the sending of messengers entirely (like the Sumaniyya, who limit knowledge to sensory perception and thus reject prophetic transmission), while others (like the Mu'tazila and philosophers) require it based on their own rational principles. The author then clarifies that 'hawa' in its general usage means inclination away from truth, citing the Quranic verse {And do not follow hawa}. The name 'hawa' is derived from its effect: it makes its possessor fall (yahwi) into the Fire.

Key Terms
هوى
السمنية
المعتزلة
الحكماء
الإرسال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hawa means only sexual lust or mere whimsical desire.
Correction: In this context, hawa refers to any false belief that deviates from truth, specifically leading to rejection or distortion of prophethood. The passage also generalizes hawa as inclination opposite to truth.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'hawa' (هوى) generally refer to?
A) A type of lustful desire
B) Any inclination towards opposition of the truth
C) The belief of the Sumaniyya
D) The denial of prophethood

Key Takeaway
Hawa is any false inclination that deviates from truth, and it is the root cause of both denial and misguided rationalization of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What false belief have you been holding that, like the Sumaniyya, makes you reject a clear truth because it falls outside what your senses or reason can grasp? Are you willing to examine that belief honestly today?
?
Consider a time when you rationalized a wrong action or belief in the name of intellect or reason, like the Mu'tazila and philosophers. How did your hawa deceive you into thinking you were on the right path? Will you repent and submit to the truth even if it contradicts your reasoning?
?
The term 'hawa' comes from 'hawā' meaning to fall into the Fire. If you are honest with yourself, which of your inclinations or desires are leading you away from the straight path? What is one concrete step you can take today to follow truth instead of hawa?

Maturidi Detailed Preference: Humans Over Angels
Maturidi classification and defense of human superiority over common angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that common humans are superior to common angels, and the rationale based on reward for worship under hardship.

Core Idea
Common humans are better than common angels due to greater reward for struggling in worship.

The Maturidis disagree with the Ash'aris on the general preference of angels over humans. They introduce a detailed hierarchy that includes a surprising claim: common believers among humans are superior to the highest common angels. This passage explains their reasoning and answers a key objection about infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the Maturidi classification come through before analyzing.
Notice the four categories and the hierarchy.
2
Identify the four categories in the hierarchy
Find the sequence: human messengers > angel messengers > non-prophet humans (saints) > common angels. Also note the final point: common humans > common angels.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection asks how non-infallible humans can be superior to infallible angels. The answer focuses on reward for worship under hardship.
This is a key theological nuance.
4
Reflect on the criterion of reward under hardship
Consider why hardship increases reward. This explains the Maturidi view without needing infallibility.
Connects to broader kalam discussions on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفضيل بين الملائكة وغير الأنبياء عند الماتريدية
٦٧ - هذا وَقَوْمٌ فَصَّلُوا إذ فَضَلُوا وَبَعْضُ كُلَّ بَعْضَهُ قَدْ يَفْضُلُ
(هَذَا) المذكور من تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة، والملائكة على غيرِ الأنبياء من البشرِ مِن غير تفصيل طريقُ الأشاعرة المرجوحة، وإنَّما جزمَ النَّاظم بها لأنَّه وضعَ منظومته على مختار مذهبهم.
وأشار إلى الطريق الثانية بقوله: (وَقَوْمٌ) من الماتريديَّة لم يقولوا بأفضلية جُملة كلّ فريق ممَّن تقدَّم على جُملة كلّ فريق يليه ، بل (فَصَّلُوا) القولَ (إِذْ فَضَّلُوا) أي حين تعرَّضوا للتفضيل بين الفريقين فقالوا: رسل البشر كموسى أفضلُ مِن رسل الملائكة كجبريل ، ورسل الملائكة كإسرافيل أفضل من البشر ، وهم أولياؤهم غير الأنبياء كأبي بكر وعمر ، وعامة البشر أفضل ، وهم غير الرُّسل منهم كحملة العرش والكروبيين من عامة الملائكة.
(1) جواب عن سؤال مقدر تقديره: لِمَ جزمَ النَّاظمُ بها مع أنها الطريقة المرجوحة؟
(2) قوله: (وهم أولياؤهم) تفسير لعامة البشر، أي فليس المراد هنا بعامة البشر عموم الناس الشامل للفساق، فإنَّ الملائكة أفضل منهم . . الأمير والشنواني.
(3) هذا هو محل الخلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية، فالأشاعرة تفضل جميع الملائكة على جميع البشر، والماتريدية تفضل عوام البشر على عوام الملائكة . فإن قلت: كيف يُفضلُ غير المعصوم على المعصوم ؟ أجيب ليس المراد التفضيل من حيث العصمة وعدمها، بل من حيث أكثرية الثواب على العبادة، فعوام البشر أكثر ثوابا على عبادتهم من عوام الملائكة، الحصول المشقة لهم. الشنواني.
(4) ورد تسمية جماعة من الملائكة بالكروبيين في روايات لا ترقى إلى الصحة، انظر المستدرك للحاكم في كتاب الأهوال رقم: [۸۶۹۹] وشعب الإيمان للبيهقي [رقم /١٤٨. فصل في=
The preference between angels and non-prophets according to the Maturidis. 67 - This, and a group made a detailed distinction when they preferred, and some of each may prefer the other. (This) mentioned preference of prophets over angels, and angels over non-prophet humans without detail is the outweighed Ash'ari way. The author only asserted it because he composed his poem according to the chosen view of their school. And he pointed to the second way by his saying: (And a group) of the Maturidis did not say that the whole of each group is superior to the whole of the next group, but they made a detailed statement when they preferred, i.e., when they engaged in preference between the two groups. They said: human messengers like Musa are better than messenger angels like Jibril; messenger angels like Israfil are better than non-prophet humans, i.e., their saints like Abu Bakr and Umar; and the common humans are better, i.e., those of them who are not messengers, than the common angels, i.e., the bearers of the Throne and the cherubim among the common angels. (1) Answer to an implied question: Why did the author assert it while it is the outweighed way? (2) His saying: (i.e., their saints) is an interpretation of common humans; meaning here that by common humans is not meant the general people including sinners, for angels are better than them. Al-Amir and Al-Shanwani. (3) This is the point of disagreement between the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. The Ash'aris prefer all angels over all humans, while the Maturidis prefer common humans over common angels. If you say: How can a non-infallible be preferred over an infallible? The answer: Preference is not intended from the perspective of infallibility or its absence, but from the perspective of greater reward for worship. For common humans receive more reward for their worship than common angels, due to the hardship they experience. Al-Shanwani. (4) The naming of a group of angels as 'cherubim' is reported in narrations that do not reach the level of authenticity. See Al-Mustadrak of Al-Hakim in Kitab al-Ahwal, no. 8699, and Shu'ab al-Iman of Al-Bayhaqi, no. 148. A section on...
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage discusses the Maturidi view on the preference (tafdil) between angels and non-prophet humans. The author first states that the Ash'ari position (preferred by the author) is that prophets are superior to angels, and angels are superior to all other humans, without further distinction. However, the author notes that this is the outweighed (marjuha) view, but he asserted it because his poem follows the Ash'ari school. Then he explains the second view, held by the Maturidis: they make a detailed distinction. They say that human messengers (like Musa) are superior to messenger angels (like Jibril); messenger angels (like Israfil) are superior to non-prophet humans (like Abu Bakr and Umar); and common humans (non-messenger believers) are superior to common angels (like the Throne-bearers and cherubim). The footnotes clarify: the term 'common humans' in this context excludes sinners, as angels are better than sinful people. The core disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis is that Ash'aris prefer all angels over all humans, while Maturidis prefer common humans over common angels. An objection is raised: how can non-infallible humans be superior to infallible angels? The answer is that the preference is not based on infallibility but on the greater reward for worship due to the hardship of this world. Thus, humans who struggle in worship receive more reward than angels whose worship is effortless. The final footnote discusses the term 'cherubim' (karubiyyun), noting that reports about them are not fully authentic, citing sources. The passage ends incompletely.

Key Terms
رسل البشر
رسل الملائكة
عوام البشر
عوام الملائكة
التفضيل
المشقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume preference between angels and humans is based on sinlessness (isma).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Maturidis base it on reward for worship under hardship, not infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view, what makes common humans superior to common angels?
A) Their infallibility in worship.
B) Their greater reward for worship due to hardship.
C) Their higher rank as messengers.
D) Their ability to disobey.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis argue that common humans, despite not being infallible, can achieve higher rank than common angels due to the difficulty of worship in this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your daily struggles in worship, despite your sins, can elevate you above the angels? If so, how does that change your view of your own efforts in prayer, fasting, and charity?
?
When you sin and feel unworthy, do you remember that the Maturidis argue for human superiority not based on sinlessness but on the effort to obey? Does that give you hope to keep striving?
?
How does the concept of 'hardship in worship' play out in your life? Are you avoiding difficult acts of worship, like night prayer or charity, that could elevate your rank, or are you embracing them?

Ash'ari and Maturidi Views on Angel-Human Preference
Introduction to the Ash'ari view and the author's methodological commitment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between Ash'ari and Maturidi views on the preference between angels and humans, and why the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid chose the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Ash'aris prefer all angels over all humans; Maturidis split hairs and prefer common humans over common angels.

The author of Jawharat al-Tawhid, following the Ash'ari school, asserts the general preference of prophets over angels and angels over all other humans. However, he notes this is the outweighed view and points to the Maturidi alternative. This lesson sets the stage for the detailed contrast.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines and commentary
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the author's statement and the footnote explaining why he chose this view.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position
The Ash'ari view is that prophets are superior to angels, and angels are superior to all non-prophet humans, without further detail.
The author calls this the 'outweighed' (marjuha) way.
3
Understand why the author asserts it
The author states he received it because his poem follows the Ash'ari school's chosen view. This is a commitment to his own school.
The footnote answers an implied question about why he doesn't take the Maturidi view.
4
Note the mention of the Maturidi alternative
The author mentions that a group (the Maturidis) made a detailed distinction, which he will explain later.

Commentary

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author asserts the Ash'ari view as the only valid one and does not acknowledge alternatives.
Correction: The author explicitly mentions the Maturidi view and acknowledges that the Ash'ari view is outweighed, showing he is aware of other opinions.

Checkpoint
Why does the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid assert the Ash'ari view on angel-human preference even though he calls it 'marjuha' (outweighed)?
A) Because he believes it is the only correct view.
B) Because his poem follows the Ash'ari school's chosen view.
C) Because he is a Maturidi pretending to be Ash'ari.
D) Because he wants to please the readers.

Key Takeaway
The author of Jawharat al-Tawhid commits to the Ash'ari view (prophets > angels > non-prophets) because his poem follows Ash'ari doctrine, but he acknowledges the Maturidi alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author follows a view he knows is outweighed (marjuha) because it is his school's doctrine. Is there a belief or practice you hold simply because your teachers or community uphold it, even when you suspect a stronger alternative exists? What would it take for you to investigate that alternative honestly?
?
Imagine you discovered that a cherished theological stance of yours is actually the weaker opinion among scholars. Would you change your position, or would you still adhere to it out of loyalty? What does that reveal about your relationship with truth versus your attachments?
?
If even great scholars disagree on something as significant as the rank of angels over humans, how does that affect your confidence in your own theological opinions? Can you hold your beliefs firmly yet remain open to correction? Examine your heart: is there arrogance masked as certainty?

The Māturīdī Detailed Preference Between Angels and Non-Prophets
Explains the Māturīdī detailed preference between angels and non-prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī view on tafḍīl (preference) between angels and non-prophets, including its nuanced ranking compared to the Ashʿarī general preference.

Core Idea
The Māturīdīs rank in detail: messengers of humans > messengers of angels > non-prophet humans > common angels.

This lesson examines the Māturīdī position on who is better among angels and non-prophets. While the Ashʿarīs hold a general preference of prophets over angels and angels over non-prophets, the Māturīdīs provide a detailed ranking that distinguishes between messengers (rusul) and ordinary humans and angels. The poem's author follows the Ashʿarī view, but the commentary explains the Māturīdī alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice that it first mentions the Ashʿarī position, then the Māturīdī detailed view.
The verse line (67) is the start of the passage.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts the Ashʿarī general preference (without detail) with the Māturīdī detailed preference. Note which one the poem's author chose.
The author follows the Ashʿarī view as the 'weaker' opinion.
3
Understand the Māturīdī ranking hierarchy
The Māturīdīs list four categories in order: (1) messengers of humans (e.g., Musa), (2) messengers of angels (e.g., Jibril, Israfeel), (3) non-prophet human saints (e.g., Abu Bakr, Umar), (4) common angels (e.g., Throne-bearers, Cherubim).
Hear how each category is compared to the next.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this detailed ranking matter? It avoids blanket statements and gives more precise theological distinctions.
This reflects the Māturīdī emphasis on rational detail.

Source Text

67 - هذا وَقَوْمٌ فَصَّلُوا إذ فَضَلُوا وَبَعْضُ كُلَّ بَعْضَهُ قَدْ يَفْضُلُ
(هَذَا) المذكور من تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة، والملائكة على غيرِ الأنبياء من البشرِ مِن غير تفصيل طريقُ الأشاعرة المرجوحة، وإنَّما جزمَ النَّاظم بها لأنَّه وضعَ منظومته على مختار مذهبهم.
وأشار إلى الطريق الثانية بقوله : (وَقَوْمٌ) من الماتريديَّة لم يقولوا بأفضلية جُملة كلّ فريق ممَّن تقدَّم على جُملة كلّ فريق يليه ، بل (فَصَّلُوا) القولَ (إِذْ فَضَّلُوا) أي حين تعرَّضوا للتفضيل بين الفريقين فقالوا: رسل البشر كموسى أفضلُ مِن رسل الملائكة كجبريل ، ورسل الملائكة كإسرافيل أفضل من البشر ، وهم أولياؤهم غير الأنبياء كأبي بكر وعمر ، وعامة البشر أفضل ، وهم غير الرُّسل منهم كحملة العرش والكروبيين من عامة الملائكة
This is what was mentioned of preferring the prophets over the angels, and the angels over non-prophets among humans without detail: the way of the Ash'aris is the weaker one, and the author only stated it decisively because he composed his poem according to the chosen view of their school. And he pointed to the second way by his saying: 'And a group' of the Māturīdiyyah did not say with the preponderance of the entire category of each group over the entire category of the group that follows it, but they detailed the statement when they engaged in comparison between the two groups, saying: The messengers of humans like Musa are better than the messengers of angels like Jibril; and the messengers of angels like Israfeel are better than the humans who are their saints (auliyā') other than the prophets, like Abu Bakr and 'Umar; and the common humans are better, and they are the non-messengers among them, like the bearers of the Throne and the Cherubim (Karubiyyīn) from the common angels.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage defines the Māturīdī position on tafḍīl (preference) between angels and non-prophets. It first states the Ash'ari position, which the poem author follows: a general preference of prophets over angels and angels over non-prophets without further detail, noting this is the weaker opinion. Then it explains the Māturīdī position, which makes a detailed comparison: messengers of humans (like Musa) are better than messengers of angels (like Jibril); messengers of angels (like Israfeel) are better than non-prophet human saints (like Abu Bakr and 'Umar); and common humans (non-prophets among humans) are better than common angels (like the Throne-bearers and Cherubim). This detailed view avoids blanket statements and considers specific categories.

البشر (al-bashar)
الماتريدية (al-Māturīdiyya)
الأشاعرة (al-Ashā'ira)
الرسل (al-rusul)
الكروبيين (al-Karūbiyyīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Māturīdī view is less precise because it contradicts the general Ashʿarī ranking.
Correction: The Māturīdī view is actually more detailed, providing specific comparisons between subcategories rather than a sweeping statement.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī position as described, which group is considered better than common angels?
A) Messengers of angels
B) Common humans (non-prophets)
C) All humans
D) Messengers of humans

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdī view offers a nuanced ranking: messengers of humans are best, then messengers of angels, then non-prophet humans, finally common angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself unconsciously ranking God's creation in your heart? How does this specific Māturīdī ranking confront your assumptions about the status of angels versus human saints? Will you actively check yourself the next time you make a comparison?
?
This lesson shows that messengers of humans are superior to messengers of angels. In your daily life, do you genuinely prioritize following the messengers (prophets) over any worldly leader or even angelic beings? What is one concrete change you can make today to align your reverence with this order?
?
The Māturīdī view demands precise categories rather than vague notions. Are you content to hold a simplistic view of superiority, or will you commit to studying the detailed distinctions that Allah has ordained? Write down one theological distinction you will investigate further this week.

Permissibility of Abrogating Parts of the Sharia and Jewish Sects' Views
Explanation of abrogation of some Sharia parts and listing of Jewish sects' views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that abrogating some rulings of the Sharia by other rulings from the same Sharia is permissible, and you will be familiar with the positions of three Jewish sects on abrogation.

Core Idea
Abrogation of some Sharia rulings by others is permissible, though Jewish sects differed.

The commentator explains that the author's statement about the Sharia not being abrogated by other than itself refers to the permissibility of abrogating some rulings by other rulings from the same Sharia. He then lists three Jewish sects with differing views on abrogation, showing that even among those who deny prophethood, there is no consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then its translation. Notice the structure: first the explanation of the author's words, then the list of sects.
2
Identify the main ruling
Find the command 'أجِزْ' and understand that it means to believe in the permissibility of abrogation of some parts by others.
The commentator emphasizes that this includes even the most fundamental obligations.
3
Examine the three Jewish sects
List each sect and their position: Shama'uniyya (totally forbidden), 'Ananiyya (permissible rationally but forbidden scripturally), 'Isawiyya (permissible but only for Arabs).
All three sects are from the Jews, showing their denial of the Prophet's message.
4
Consider the footnote on tawassulan
Read the footnote explaining that denying abrogation is a means to deny prophethood, as it implies the Prophet was a liar.
This connects abrogation to the proof of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ شَرعَ في بيانِ مفهوم قوله: «فَشَرْعُهُ لَا يُنْسَخُ بِغَيْرِهِ» فقال: (وَنَسْخَ) أي: ووقوع نسخ (بَعْضِ ) أحكام (شَرْعِهِ ) له بالبَعْض) (۲) أي: بأحكام بعض شرعه الآخر (أَجِزْ) أي: اعتقد جواز الوقوع (۳) ، واحْكُم : = به وشَمِل البعض (٤) المنسوخ وجوبَ معرفته سبحانه ، وتحريم الكفر كما : وقالت فرقة يقال لها الشمعونية نسبة إلى شمعون بن يعقوب]: النسخ ممنوع عقلا وسمعا ، وقالت فرقة يقال لها العنانية [نسبة إلى عنان بن داود ، تخالف فرقته سائر اليهود في السبت والأعياد وينهون عن أكل السمك والظباء، ويقال أنه اجتمع مع أبي حنيفة في السجن]: يجوز النسخ عقلا ويمتنع سمعا ، وقالت فرقة يقال لها العيسوية [ أتباع أبي عيسى إسحاق بن يعقوب الأصفهاني]: يجوز النسخ عقلا وسمعا ، ولكنه مخصوص بالعرب. فالفِرَقُ ثلاثة ، وكلها مِن اليهود. انظر الشنواني . (۱) قوله (توسلا أي توصلا للقول بنفي ،نبوته ، و لعل وجهه أنه لا أخبر بنسخه لشرائع من قبله، وهو كذب، فيقولون: الكاذب لا يكون نبيا لعنهم الله تعالى، أو يتدرجون في التكذيب. الأمير والشنواني. (۲) اعلم أن الكلام في مقامين مقام جواز ، ومقام وقوع ، فمن حيثُ الجوازُ يجوز نسخ الشريعة كلا وبعضاً، وأما من حيثُ الوقوع فلا يجوز نسخ الجميع ، إذا علمت هذا فقول المصنف: (أَجِزْ ) من الجواز الوقوعي ، وإلا لما كان للتقييد بالبعض معنى. وقد وقع في الشرح تعارض ، فجرى أولا على أن المراد بالجواز الجواز الوقوعي . الشنواني. (۳) في نسخة: (اعتقد جوازه الوقوعي)
Then he began explaining the meaning of his statement: 'For his Sharia is not abrogated by other than it,' and he said: (And abrogation) i.e., the occurrence of abrogation (of some) rulings (of his Sharia) by some (other part) (2) i.e., by rulings of some other part of his Sharia (you may permit) i.e., believe in the permissibility of occurrence (3), and judge accordingly. And it includes (4) the abrogated part [including] the obligation of knowing Him, Exalted, and the prohibition of disbelief, as: And a sect called the Shama'uniyya (attributed to Shama'un ibn Ya'qub) said: Abrogation is prohibited by reason and revelation. And a sect called the 'Ananiyya (attributed to 'Anan ibn Dawud, which disagrees with other Jews regarding the Sabbath and festivals, and forbids eating fish and gazelles; it is said that he met Abu Hanifa in prison) said: Abrogation is permissible by reason but prohibited by revelation. And a sect called the 'Isawiyya (followers of Abu 'Isa Ishaq ibn Ya'qub al-Isfahani) said: Abrogation is permissible by reason and revelation, but it is specific to the Arabs. So the sects are three, and all are from the Jews. See al-Shanwani. (1) His statement: 'tawassulan' i.e., seeking a means to the claim of denying his prophethood, and perhaps its aspect is that he did not report abrogation of the laws before him, which is a lie, so they say: The liar cannot be a prophet, may Allah curse them, or they gradually proceed in denial. Al-Amir and al-Shanwani. (2) Know that the discussion is in two levels: the level of permissibility and the level of occurrence. As for permissibility, abrogation of the Sharia in whole or in part is permissible. As for occurrence, abrogation of the whole is not permissible. If you know this, then the author's statement 'you may permit' is from the perspective of permissibility of occurrence, otherwise the restriction to 'some' would have no meaning. And there is a contradiction in the commentary, as it first ran that what is meant by permissibility is permissibility of occurrence. Al-Shanwani. (3) In a copy: 'believe in its permissibility of occurrence.'
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the author's line about the Sharia not being abrogated by other than itself. The commentator clarifies that the author's phrase 'and abrogation of some of his Sharia by other parts' means the occurrence of abrogation of some rulings by other rulings from the same Sharia, and the command 'you may permit' means to believe in the permissibility of such occurrence. The commentator then notes that this includes even the obligation of knowing Allah and the prohibition of disbelief, which are the most fundamental obligations, but only in terms of rational possibility. To illustrate the range of opinions, the commentator lists three Jewish sects: the Shama'uniyya who deny abrogation entirely, the 'Ananiyya who allow it rationally but deny it scripturally, and the 'Isawiyya who allow it both rationally and scripturally but restrict it to Arabs. All three are from the Jews. The commentator then adds a footnote explaining the word 'tawassulan' as seeking a means to deny the Prophet's prophethood. He further emphasizes the critical distinction between the level of permissibility (جواز) and the level of occurrence (وقوع). Abrogation of the entire Sharia is permissible in itself, but it has not occurred. The author's phrase 'you may permit' refers to permissibility of occurrence, not mere rational possibility. There was some confusion in the commentary, but the correct understanding is that it is permissibility of occurrence.

Key Terms
نسخ
جواز
وقوع
فرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Jewish sect held that abrogation is permissible by reason but forbidden by revelation?
A) Shama'uniyya
B) 'Ananiyya
C) 'Isawiyya
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Abrogation of some Sharia rulings by others is permissible, and it is not contradicted by the fact that some Jewish sects denied it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Allah abrogates a ruling you were accustomed to, do you submit immediately, or do you find yourself hesitating? What does your reaction reveal about your heart's attachment to your own understanding over Allah's command? Resolve to obey without delay.
?
The Jewish sects denied abrogation to justify rejecting the Prophet. Are there any areas in your life where you deny a clear ruling or reinterpret it to avoid submitting? Be honest with yourself and repent.
?
The commentator notes that denial of abrogation can be a means to deny prophethood. How might your own small denials or hesitations in following the Sharia be steps toward a larger rebellion? Realign your heart today.

Distinguishing Between Permissibility and Occurrence in Abrogation
Clarification of the two levels in discussing abrogation: permissibility and occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between the permissibility of abrogation (جواز) and its actual occurrence (وقوع), and why the author's statement refers to permissibility of occurrence.

Core Idea
Abrogation of the entire Sharia is possible but never occurred; abrogation of parts has occurred.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies an essential nuance: the discussion of abrogation operates on two levels — whether it is permissible in itself, and whether it actually happened. While abrogation of the entire Sharia is rationally possible, it never occurred. The author's phrase 'you may permit' refers to the permissibility of occurrence, not just rational possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two levels
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'اعلم أن الكلام في مقامين' and its translation. Understand that there are two levels: جواز (permissibility) and وقوع (occurrence).
2
Distinguish the two levels
Note that abrogation of the entire Sharia is permissible in itself but has not occurred. Abrogation of some parts, however, is both permissible and occurred.
The author's statement is about the permissibility of occurrence of partial abrogation.
3
Understand the author's intent
The commentator argues that the phrase 'you may permit' must refer to permissibility of occurrence, because otherwise the restriction to 'some' would be meaningless.
This resolves a potential contradiction in the commentary.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Realize that many confuse permissibility with actual occurrence. This passage guards against that error.
The distinction is critical in theology.

?

?

?

Ranking of the Four Caliphs in Virtue
Commentary defines ranking and refutes opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ranking of the four caliphs in virtue is based on their order of succession, as affirmed by Ahl al-Sunnah, and you will recognize the groups that held different rankings.

Core Idea
The caliphs' virtue follows their order of succession.

This lesson explains a key Sunni doctrine: the ranking of the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) in virtue. The author ties their virtue directly to their historical order of caliphate, making the earliest the most virtuous. He then explicitly lists the ranking and refutes several groups that deviate from this order. Prepare to see how the text combines a positive affirmation with refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how the author states the ranking and then lists groups that disagree.
The passage includes a quotation from al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) confirming the consensus.
2
Identify the ranking and its basis
Look for the explicit sequence: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. Note that the basis is 'like the caliphate'—i.e., the order of succession determines the order of virtue.
3
List the refuted groups and their errors
The passage names the Khattabiyya (Umar first), Rawandiyya (al-Abbas first), Shia/Kufans (Ali first), Mu'tazila, and an early opinion of Malik (Ali over Uthman). Understand what each group incorrectly prioritizes.
This shows that the ranking is a settled Sunni position against various innovations.
4
Reflect on the evidence
Al-Sa'd remarks that the early and later generations accepted this ranking, implying there is evidence for it. Ask yourself: what evidence could support this?
The commentator suggests that the ranking is based on revealed evidence, not mere opinion.
