⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أفضل الصحابة الخلفاء الأربعةُ على ترتيب الخلافة (وَأَمْرُهُمْ)، أي: شأنُ الخلفاء الأربعة في تفاوتهم وترتبهم (فِي الفَضْلِ) بمعنى كثرةِ الثَّواب أو العِلْمِ أو الشَّجَاعة، (كَالخِلَافَهُ)، أي: على حَسَبِ تفاوتهم فيها ، فالأسبقُ فيها أكثرهم فضلا ، ثمَّ التَّالي فالتَّالي كذلك ، عند أهل السُّنَّة وإماميهم : أبي الحسن الأشعري، وأبي منصور الماتريدي، فأفضلُهم: أبو بكرٍ (۱) ، ثمَّ عمرُ ، ثمَّ عثمان ، ثمَّ عليّ ، رضي الله تعالى عنهم ، قال السعد: على هذا وجدنا السلف والخلف ، والظَّاهرُ أنَّه لو لم يكن لهم دليل على ذلك لما حكموا به ((٢)) . والنظمُ صريح في الرَّدَّ على الخَطَّابِيَّة (۳) في تقديم عمرَ ، والرَّاوِنْدِيَّة (٤) في تقديم العباس بن عبد المطلب ، والشَّيعة، وأهل الكوفة، وبعض أهل ، وجمهور المعتزلة، وقول مالك الأول بتقديم عليّ على عثمان عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنهما (ه) .
The best of the companions are the four caliphs in the order of succession. (And their matter) i.e., the affair of the four caliphs in their differentiation and ordering (in virtue) meaning abundance of reward or knowledge or bravery, (like the caliphate) i.e., according to their differentiation in it, so the foremost in it is the most abundant of them in virtue, then the next and so on, according to Ahl al-Sunnah and their two imams: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. So the best of them is Abu Bakr (1), then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali, may Allah be pleased with them. Al-Sa'd said: On this we found the early and later generations, and it is apparent that if they had no evidence for that they would not have judged by it. And the poem is explicit in refuting the Khattabiyya in giving precedence to Umar, and the Rawandiyya in giving precedence to al-Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib, and the Shia, and the people of Kufa, and some of the people, and the majority of the Mu'tazila, and the earlier statement of Malik giving precedence to Ali over Uthman, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage defines the ranking of the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) in virtue according to their order of succession in the caliphate. The author explains that the phrase 'like the caliphate' means their virtue corresponds to their order in the caliphate: the earliest in succession is the most virtuous, then the next, and so on. This is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah and their two imams, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. The passage then lists the explicit ranking: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. It cites al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) stating that the early and later generations found this to be the case, and that there must be evidence for it. The passage then notes that the poem (the matn) clearly refutes various groups that hold different rankings: the Khattabiyya who prefer Umar over Abu Bakr, the Rawandiyya (Abbasids) who prefer al-Abbas, the Shia and people of Kufa who prefer Ali, the majority of the Mu'tazila, and an earlier opinion of Imam Malik that preferred Ali over Uthman. Thus, the ranking is settled doctrine.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the ranking is based on the order of succession, not subjective measures. It is a settled doctrine, not open to personal preference.

Checkpoint

B) Their order of succession in the caliphate

Key Takeaway

?

The Six Remaining Companions and No Ranking by Text
Commentary lists the six companions after the four caliphs and states no ranking among them by revealed text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity of the six companions who complete the ten promised Paradise, why no revealed text ranks them among themselves, and how considerations like lineage and precedence can still indicate virtue.

Core Idea
Revealed text does not rank the six, but other virtues distinguish them.

After establishing the four caliphs, the poem turns to the remaining six of the ten promised Paradise. This lesson introduces these noble companions and clarifies a key theological point: no revealed text (nas) differentiates their rank among themselves. Yet, other factors like noble lineage, precedence in Islam, and emigration can still indicate relative virtue. Understanding this nuance helps avoid oversimplifying the status of these great companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the author's style of embedding glosses within the text.
The passage is from a commentary on the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid.
2
Identify the six companions
The passage names six companions: Talha, al-Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah. Note their connection to the Prophet.
These are well-known figures in early Islamic history.
3
Understand the lack of ranking
The passage states no revealed text (nas) differentiates some of these six over others in virtue. Hence, no scholar claims such a ranking. This is due to lack of specification (tawqif).
This is a key principle in Sunni theology regarding the companions.
4
Consider the exceptions
The passage notes that this lack of ranking is when ignoring noble lineage, precedence in Islam, and emigration. If a companion has these merits, he may be considered more virtuous in that respect.
This is indicated by an earlier line of the poem.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this distinction matters: it preserves respect for all companions while allowing recognition of special virtues without relying on non-revealed rankings.
This balances between reverence and theological precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَلِيهِمُ قَومٌ كِرَامٌ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُم سِتٌ تمامُ العَشَرة (يَلِيهِمُ ) ، أي: يلي آخر الأربعةِ الخُلفَاءِ في الأفضلية على الغَيْرِ (قَوْمُ)، أي: رِجال (كِرَامٌ) - جمع كريم جمع كريم ، وهو كريم النَّفس رفيع النَّسب (بَرَرَهُ) جمع بَر ، وهو المُحْسن عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتَّ)، أي: ستَّةٌ (تَمَامُ العَشَرَة) المبشَّرينَ بالجنَّة، الذين من جملتهم المشايخ الأربعة السابقون، وهم: طلحة بن عبيد الله ، والزبير بن العوام ابن عمَّة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص ، وسعيد بن زيد ، وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح، ولم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض في الأفضلية فلا قائل به ؛ لعدم التوقيف وتخصيص هؤلاء العشرة؛ لِشُهرة حديثهم الجامع لهم(١) ، وإن كان المُبشَّرون بالجَنَّة أكثر (٢) . ثمَّ هذا مَعَ قَطْع النَّظرِ عن القرابة الشَّريفة(۳)، والتَّقدُّم في الإسلام والهجرة (٤) ، بدليل قوله آنفًا: «والسابقون فضلهم ؛ نصا عرف .
They are followed by a noble righteous people, their number is six, completing the ten. (They are followed) i.e., they follow the last of the four caliphs in virtue over others, (a people) i.e., men, (noble) plural of karim, and he is noble of soul and high of lineage, (righteous) plural of barr, and he is the benefactor, (their number is six) i.e., six, (completing the ten) who are given glad tidings of Paradise, among whom are the four preceding shaykhs, and they are: Talha ibn Ubayd Allah, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam the uncle's son of the Messenger of Allah, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah. And no text has come regarding the differentiation of some of them over others in virtue, so no one claims it, due to the lack of specification, and the specification of these ten is due to the fame of the hadith that gathered them (1), even though those given glad tidings of Paradise are more (2). Then this is without consideration of noble lineage (3), and precedence in Islam and emigration (4), as indicated by his earlier statement: 'And the foremost in Islam, their virtue is known by text.'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage explains the next six companions after the four caliphs who complete the ten promised Paradise. The author defines each term: 'they are followed' means they come after the last of the four caliphs in virtue; 'noble' refers to nobility of soul and lineage; 'righteous' means beneficent. He then names the six: Talha, al-Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah. The key point is that no revealed text (nas) establishes a ranking among these six in virtue, so no scholar claims such a ranking due to lack of specification. The hadith specifying the ten is well-known, even though there are more than ten promised Paradise. Finally, the author notes that this lack of ranking is only when ignoring considerations of noble lineage, precedence in Islam, and emigration. If one of them has these merits, he may be considered more virtuous in that respect, as the earlier line of the poem indicates: 'And the foremost in Islam, their virtue is known by text.'

Classification of the Companions' Merits
Enumeration of Companion ranking methods and specific orders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways Sunni scholars rank the Companions in merit: individually by name and categorically by group, along with the specific orders given in this text.

Core Idea
The Companions' merits are ranked both individually and by group, starting with the Badr participants.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains how the merits of the Companions are classified. The author first establishes that the virtue of having participated in Badr is unique and cannot be matched by other events. Then, he outlines two methods of ranking: by individual names and by categories of Companions. The text provides specific orders, though note some textual variants exist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Badri uniqueness principle
The passage begins by asserting that the virtue of a Badri (participant of Badr) is not equaled by that of an Uhudi (participant of Uhud), even if the two virtues are in the same category. This establishes a hierarchy of events.
The principle implies that participation in Badr is a higher merit than other events.
2
Distinguish the two ranking methods
The text states that ranking (tafdil) is considered either by individuals or by categories. Identify which names are listed under each method and note the differences.
The individual ranking omits Umar, which may be a textual variant.
3
Examine the specific order in each method
Note the individual order: Abu Bakr, then Uthman, then Ali. For categories: the best (al-afdal), then Umar, then the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten, then the rest of Badr participants, then Uhud participants, then people of the Pledge of Ridwan.
The categorical ranking includes Umar after 'al-afdal', which may refer to the best overall.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدري لا تساويها مزيَّة الأُحدي من حيث هو أحدي مثلا، وإن أتحد محل المزيَّتين ، وكذا الباقي . هو وقد عُلم مِن النَّظم أنّ التَّفضيلَ: إمَّا باعتبار الأفراد، فأبو بكر ثمَّ عثمان ، ثمَّ علي ، وإما باعتبار الأصناف، فأفضلهم: الأفضل ، ثُمَّ عمر ، الخلفاء الأربعة ، ثمَّ السِّتَّة الباقية من العشرة ، ثمَّ بقيَّة البدرين ، ثم بقيَّة أصحاب أحد ، ثمّ بقيَّة أهل بيعة الرضوان بالحديبية ، وهو (۱) في كلام الشَّمس البرماوي (٢) .
A Badri is not equaled by the virtue of an Uhudi in terms of being a single [event] for example, even if the locus of the two virtues is the same. And likewise the rest. It is known from the poem that preference (tafdil) is either by individuals: Abu Bakr then Uthman then Ali, or by categories: the best, then Umar, the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten, then the rest of the Badr participants, then the rest of the participants of Uhud, then the rest of the people of the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyya. And this is in the words of al-Shams al-Barmawi.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of the merits of the Companions. It begins by explaining that the virtue of a Badri (participant of Badr) cannot be equaled by the virtue of an Uhudi (participant of Uhud) in terms of the specific virtue of being a participant in that particular event, even if the two virtues are in the same category. Then it states that from the poem (i.e., the matn) we learn that tafdil (ranking in merit) is considered in two ways: either by individuals, or by categories. In the individual ranking, the order is: Abu Bakr, then Uthman, then Ali. Note that Umar is not mentioned in this individual list, which is unusual in Sunni creed; this may be an OCR error or a textual variant. In the categorical ranking, the text lists: the best (al-afdal), then Umar, then the four caliphs (which presumably includes Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), then the remaining six of the ten (the other six of the ten promised Paradise), then the rest of the Badr participants, then the rest of the participants of Uhud, then the rest of the people of the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyya. The passage attributes this classification to al-Shams al-Barmawi.

The Proper Stance on Disagreements Among the Companions
The commentator explains the ruling on interpreting the Companions' conflicts and avoiding envy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to properly interpret the disagreements among the Companions, why they remain just despite their differences, and the etiquette of discussing such matters.

Core Idea
Companions' disagreements are matters of ijtihad, not grounds for blame; discuss only with knowledge and avoid envy.

This passage addresses a sensitive topic: the conflicts that occurred among the best generation of Muslims. The commentator guides us on how to approach these reports with fairness, understanding that each Companion acted according to his ijtihad. It also warns against envy and restricts discussion to those with proper knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command to interpret the discord
Read the first part of the passage where the commentator explains that any authentically reported dispute among the Companions should be interpreted in a favorable manner. Note the distinction between authentic and unauthentic reports.
Authentic reports require interpretation; unauthentic ones are simply rejected.
2
Recognize the three ijtihad positions
Identify the three groups mentioned regarding the conflict between 'Ali and Mu'awiyah: those who held the truth with 'Ali, those with Mu'awiyah, and those who remained neutral. Understand that all were mujtahids.
No group is declared misguided or sinful.
3
Learn the principle of 'adalah
Read the part where the commentator states that the Companions do not lose their justice ('adalah) due to their disagreements, and that this principle is unique to them, not later generations.
Later generations are judged by apparent defects.
4
Grasp the restrictions and etiquette
Understand the conditions under which one may discuss these matters (teaching, refuting bigots, studying books) and the prohibition for the general public. Also note the obligation to avoid envy.
Envy is a disease that leads to harming the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن خلاف الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم مرجعه الاجتهاد. وأول التشاجر الذي ورد إن خضت فيه واجتنب داء الحسد. لما ذكر أن الصحابة خير القرون احتاج إلى الجواب عما وقع بينهم من المنازعات الموهمة قدحاً في حقهم، وإن لم يكونوا معصومين، فقال: (وأول التشاجر) أي: التخاصم الذي ورد عنهم صحيحا بالسند المتصل، متواترا كان أو لا، مشهورًا كان أو لا، وأما ما لم يصح وروده عنهم، فهو مردود لذاته، لا يحتاج إلى تأويل، والمراد من تأويله: أن يُصرف إلى محمل حسن حيث كان ممكنًا، لتحسين الظن بهم، وحفظهم من أن الحق كان مع علي بن أبي طالب، وأن الحق كان مع معاوية. وفرقة اجتهدت في أن الحق مع علي وأن معاوية من البغاة، فوجب عليها القتال معه. وفرقة أداها اجتهادها إلى أن الحق مع معاوية وأن عليا مخطئ. وفرقة توقفت كعبد الله بن عمر. وكلهم مجتهدون لا يوجب التضليل والتفسيق، كمخاصمة فاطمة لأبي بكر حين منعها ميراثها من أبيها، فتُأول على أنها لم يبلغها الحديث الذي رواه لها الصديق. ولم يخرج واحد منهم عن العدالة بما وقع بينهم؛ لأنهم مجتهدون، ولا يُسلك هذا المسلك في بقية القرون الفاضلة، بل كل من ظهر عليه قادح حكم عليه بمقتضاه من كفر، أو فسق، أو بدعة. وإنما قال: (إن خضت فيه) أي: إن قدر ذلك؛ لأن البحث عما جرى بين الصحابة من الموافقة والمخالفة ليس من العقائد الدينية، ولا من القواعد الكلامية، وليس مما ينتفع به في الدين، بل ربما أضر باليقين، لا يباح الخوض فيه إلا للتعليم، أو للرد على المتعصبين، أو تدريس كتب تشتمل على تلك الآثار. وأما العوام فلا يجوز لهم الخوض فيه؛ لفرط جهلهم وعدم معرفتهم بالتأويل. (واجتنب) أي: ويجب عليك حال خوضك فيما شجر بينهم، كنت محققا أو سائلا، أن تجتنب داء الحسد؛ لقوله: «الله الله في أصحابي، لا تتخذوهم غرضا بعدي، من آذاهم فقد آذاني».
Explanation that the disagreement of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, returns to ijtihad. 'And interpret the discord that has been reported, if you delve into it, and avoid the disease of envy.' When he mentioned that the Companions are the best of generations, he needed to answer what occurred among them of disputes that could imply criticism of their status, even though they were not infallible, so he said: ('And interpret the discord') i.e., the quarrel that has been authentically reported from them with a connected chain, whether it is mutawatir or not, mashhur or not. As for what has not been authentically reported from them, it is rejected by itself and does not need interpretation. The meaning of its interpretation is: that it is directed to a good explanation wherever possible, to think well of them and to preserve them from [the notion] that the truth was with 'Ali ibn Abi Talib and that the truth was with Mu'awiyah. And a group exercised ijtihad that the truth was with 'Ali and that Mu'awiyah was among the rebels, so fighting with him was obligatory upon them. And a group's ijtihad led them to think that the truth was with Mu'awiyah and that 'Ali was mistaken. And a group refrained, like 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar. And all of them were mujtahids; that does not necessitate declaring them misguided or sinful, like the dispute of Fatimah with Abu Bakr when he prevented her from her inheritance from her father, so it is interpreted that she had not heard the hadith that al-Siddiq narrated to her. And not one of them left the state of justice ('adalah) due to what occurred between them, because they were mujtahids. And this path is not taken regarding the remaining virtuous centuries; rather, whoever appears with a defect is judged accordingly as disbelief, sin, or innovation. And he only said: ('If you delve into it') i.e., if it is so, because research into what occurred among the Companions of agreement and disagreement is not from the religious beliefs, nor from the theological principles, nor is it beneficial in religion; rather, it may harm certainty. It is not permissible to delve into it except for teaching, or to refute the bigoted, or to teach books that contain those reports. As for the common people, they are not permitted to delve into it because of their extreme ignorance and lack of knowledge of interpretation. ('And avoid') i.e., it is obligatory upon you, when you delve into what happened between them, whether you are an investigator or a questioner, to avoid the disease of envy; because of his saying: 'Fear Allah, fear Allah regarding my Companions; do not take them as targets after me; whoever harms them has harmed me.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a theological and methodological explanation of the correct stance on the disagreements that occurred among the Companions of the Prophet ﷺ. The commentator begins by linking the discussion to the earlier statement that the Companions are the best of generations, and then addresses the apparent problem of their disputes, which might seem to detract from their virtue. He clarifies that the line of the poem commands two things: first, to interpret (ta'wil) the reported discord, and second, to avoid envy. The commentator explains that 'interpretation' here means directing reports of conflict to a favorable explanation wherever possible, to maintain good opinion of the Companions. He distinguishes between reports that are authentically established and those that are not; the latter are rejected out of hand. He then gives the example of the disagreement over whether the truth was with 'Ali or Mu'awiyah, outlining three ijtihad-based positions: some held that 'Ali was right and Mu'awiyah a rebel, obligating fighting; some held that Mu'awiyah was right and 'Ali mistaken; and some remained neutral, like 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar. All of these are considered mujtahids, so no one is declared misguided or sinful due to their ijtihad. He supports this with the example of Fatimah's dispute with Abu Bakr over inheritance, which is interpreted as her not having heard the relevant hadith. Then the commentator states a key principle: the Companions do not lose their 'adalah (integrity) due to their differences because they were mujtahids, but this principle applies only to them, not to later generations—people after them are judged based on apparent defects. Next, he explains why the poet said 'if you delve into it': because discussing these matters is not from the fundamental beliefs of the religion nor from theological principles, and it may harm certainty rather than benefit. Therefore, it is only permissible for specific purposes: teaching, refuting bigots, or studying books containing these reports. The general public is forbidden from delving into it due to their ignorance and inability to interpret correctly. Finally, the commentator addresses the prohibition of envy (hasad) when discussing these matters, citing the hadith warning against harming the Companions, emphasizing that envy leads to ill-will and is a disease that must be avoided.

Key Terms

تأويل
اجتهاد
عدالة
حسد
صحابة
مُجتهدون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any Companion who disagreed with another must be sinful or misguided.
Correction: They were all mujtahids; ijtihad does not lead to sin, and they remain 'adil.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prohibit the general public from discussing the disagreements among the Companions?
A) Because the public is not allowed to study religious matters.
B) Because the public is too ignorant to interpret the reports correctly, which could harm their faith.
C) Because the disagreements are not authentically reported.
D) Because the public should only learn about the Companions' virtues, not their conflicts.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' disagreements are to be interpreted favorably as ijtihad, and discussing them is only for those with proper knowledge, while avoiding envy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a disagreement between two Companions, do you feel a need to take sides or judge one as wrong? How can you train yourself instead to assume both acted with ijtihad and good intent, as this lesson demands?
?
Have you ever spoken about a Companion’s action with subtle criticism or envy? What would it cost you to repent from that and guard your tongue, especially given the Prophet’s stern warning about harming his Companions?
?
If you are not a scholar, are you tempted to research or discuss the details of conflicts between Ali and Muawiyah for curiosity? Can you honestly say you will refrain from such talk unless it is for a valid reason (teaching or refuting bigotry) as the lesson instructs?

Companions' Disagreements: Ijtihad and Uprightness
The author explains that Companions' disagreements were ijtihad, and contrasts later generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Companions' disagreements were based on sincere ijtihad and did not affect their uprightness, unlike later generations.

Core Idea
Companions were mujtahids; their conflicts do not imply sin.

In this passage, the author addresses a sensitive topic: the disagreements among the Prophet's Companions. He immediately establishes that these conflicts were rooted in ijtihad, meaning each Companion sought the best ruling they could. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows why the same leniency does not apply to later generations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the example of Fatima and Abu Bakr.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand ijtihad
Ijtihad means exerting effort to derive a ruling. The author says all Companions were mujtahids, so even if they disagreed, they are not blameworthy.
3
Analyze the example
Identify the specific dispute mentioned: Fatima's inheritance claim. Note the interpretation that she hadn't heard the relevant hadith.
This shows how apparent conflicts can be explained without accusing anyone of wrongdoing.
4
Compare with later generations
The author then says this approach is not for later centuries; those with clear defects are judged accordingly.
This highlights the unique status of the Companions.
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this principle is important: it protects the reputation of the Companions and helps avoid sectarian disputes.
The lesson connects to broader Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن خلاف الصحابة مرجعه الاجتهاد يُوجب التضليل والتفسيق ، كمُخاصمة فاطمة لأبي بكر حين منعها ميراثها من أبيها ، فَتُؤوَّلُ على أنها لم يبلغها الحديث الذي رواه لها الصِّديقُ ولم يخرج واحد منهم عن العدالة بما وقع بينهم ؛ لأنَّهم مجتهدونَ ، ولا يُسلَكُ هذا المَسلَكُ في بقيَّة القرون الفَاضِلة ، بل كلُّ مَن ظهر عليه قادحٌ حُكم عليه بمُقتضَاهُ مِن كُفَرٍ ، أَوْ فِسقٍ، أو بدعةٍ ، وإنما قال: (إِنْ خُضْتَ فِيه) أي: إِنْ قُدِّرَ ذَلِكَ ؛ لأنَّ البحث عمَّا جَرَى بينَ الصَّحابَة مِن المُوافقة والمُخالفة ليسَ من العقائد الدينية ، ولا من القواعد الكَلامِيَّة ، وليس ممَّا يُنتَفَعُ به في الدِّين، بل ربما أضرَّ باليقين، لا يباحُ الخَوْضُ فيه إلا للتعليم، أو للرَّدِّ على المُتعصّبينَ، أو تدريس كتب تشتمل على تلك الآثار ، وأما العوام فلا يجوز لهم الخوضُ فيه ؛ لفَرْطِ جهلهم وعدم معرفتهم بالتأويل
مُجيبا
(وَاجْتَنِبْ) أي : ويجب عليكَ حال خوضِكَ فيما شَجَر بينهم، كنتَ أو سائلا ، أَنْ تَجتِنِبَ دَاءَ الحَسَدْ) أي: داء هو الحسد؛ لقوله : «الله الله في أصحابي ، لا تَتَّخِذوهم غَرضَا بعدي ، من آذاهم فقد آذاني ، ومن آذاني فقد آذى الله ، ومَن آذى الله يوشك أن يأخذه» وفي رواية: لا تَسبُّوا أصحابي ، مَن سبّ أصحابي فعليه لعنة الله والملائكة والنَّاس أجمعين، لا يقبل الله منه صَرْفًا ولا عَدْلًا
An exposition that the disagreement of the Companions traces back to ijtihad. It incurs misguidance and sinfulness, like the dispute of Fatima with Abu Bakr when he prevented her inheritance from her father. It is interpreted that the report which al-Siddiq related to her had not reached her. None of them departed from uprightness because of what occurred between them, for they were mujtahids. This approach is not followed in the rest of the virtuous centuries. Rather, everyone upon whom a defect appears is judged according to its requirement: disbelief, sinfulness, or innovation. He only said: 'If you engage in it' – meaning if that is supposed – because research into what occurred among the Companions, whether agreement or disagreement, is not part of religious beliefs, nor among the principles of kalam, nor is it beneficial in religion; rather it may harm certainty. It is not permissible to engage in it except for teaching, or for refuting bigots, or for teaching books that contain those reports. As for the common people, they are not allowed to engage in it due to their extreme ignorance and lack of knowledge of interpretation. Answering: 'And avoid' – meaning: It is incumbent upon you, while you are engaged in what occurred among them, whether you are a teacher or a questioner, to avoid the disease of envy – i.e., a disease which is envy – because of his saying: 'Allah, Allah concerning my Companions, do not take them as targets after me. Whoever harms them has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah, and whoever harms Allah is likely to be seized.' And in another narration: 'Do not revile my Companions. Whoever reviles my Companions, upon him is the curse of Allah, the angels, and all people. Allah will not accept from him any voluntary act nor obligatory duty.'
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of disagreements among the Companions of the Prophet. It begins by stating that such disagreements were based on ijtihad, meaning that each Companion exerted his best effort to reach a correct ruling, and thus they incur neither misguidance nor sinfulness. The example of Fatima's dispute with Abu Bakr over inheritance is cited; it is interpreted that Fatima had not heard the hadith narrated by Abu Bakr which exempts prophets from leaving inheritance. The author emphasizes that none of the Companions lost their uprightness due to these incidents because they were all mujtahids. However, this leniency does not apply to later generations. For anyone after them who appears with a defect is judged accordingly: disbelief, sinfulness, or innovation. The author then explains that engaging in discussion of what happened among the Companions is not part of religious beliefs nor kalam principles; it may even harm certainty. It is only permitted for teaching or refuting bigots, but the common people are forbidden from delving into it due to their lack of knowledge. Finally, the passage includes a warning from the Prophet: one must avoid envy when discussing these matters, citing a hadith that warns against harming the Companions.

Key Terms
صحابة
اجتهاد
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Companions' disagreements prove that some were sinful or wrong.
Correction: The passage clarifies that all were mujtahids and thus free from blame; their differences are not evidence of sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did none of the Companions lose their uprightness due to their disagreements?
A) Because they were all prophets.
B) Because they were all mujtahids.
C) Because their disagreements were minor.
D) Because the Prophet forgave them all.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' disagreements were based on ijtihad and do not affect their uprightness; this principle is unique to them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among the early Muslims, do you feel the need to take sides or determine who was right? What does this lesson teach you about the necessity of assuming good faith and ijtihad? Are you willing to reexamine your own attitudes toward historical disputes?
?
Think of a religious disagreement you have with a fellow Muslim today. Do you immediately suspect that the other person is wrong or even sinful? How would your heart change if you treated that person with the same assumption of sincere ijtihad that this passage grants the Companions? What would it cost you to do so?
?
This lesson draws a sharp line between the Companions and later generations. Have you ever excused a serious error in a scholar or leader because you admired them, essentially applying the Companion's standard to them? Are you willing to hold yourself and others to the stricter standard for later generations that this passage demands?

Ruling on Discussing Sahaba Conflicts: Permitted Only for Specific Purposes
The author details the ruling on discussing Sahaba conflicts and the prohibition for common people, with a warning against envy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why discussing the Companions' disagreements is restricted and what conditions permit it, along with the requirement to avoid envy.

Core Idea
Discussing Sahaba conflicts is allowed only for teaching or refuting bigots, and common people must avoid it.

After establishing that the Companions' disagreements were based on ijtihad, the author now turns to the practical ruling: who may discuss these events and under what circumstances. He also includes a stern warning against harboring envy or speaking ill of the Companions. This lesson clarifies the boundaries and the underlying wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic and English versions. Note the conditional phrase 'If you engage in it' and the author's explanation.
The passage is longer; read it through once before breaking it down.
2
Identify the three permissible purposes
The author lists three reasons for engaging in discussion: teaching, refuting bigots, and teaching books that contain the reports. List them and understand each.
These are exceptions to the general prohibition.
3
Understand the prohibition for common people
Common people are forbidden due to their ignorance and lack of interpretive skill. Reflect on why this is important.
The harm to certainty is a key concern.
4
Learn the warning against envy
The author quotes two hadiths emphasizing respect for the Companions and prohibition of reviling them. Identify the disease of envy and its consequences.
Envy can lead to harsh criticism and loss of blessing.
5
Reflect on the overall rule
Consider how this ruling protects the faith and maintains the respect due to the Companions. Ask yourself: does this contradict scholarly discussions of Sahaba in books of history?
Scholars writing about Sahaba for legitimate purposes are exempt.

Refuting the Denial of Saintly Miracles
Refutation of the denial of karamāt based on two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on karamāt and how the author refutes the objection that they cause confusion with prophethood.

Core Idea
Karamāt do not confuse with prophethood because only mu'jiza involves a claim and challenge.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection raised by some scholars who deny saintly miracles, fearing they might blur the line between prophets and saints. He shows that the distinction lies in the claim of prophethood and the challenge (tahaddī) that accompanies prophetic miracles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objectors and their arguments
Read the passage to find who denies karamāt (al-Ustadh, Abu Abdullah al-Halimi, Mu'tazila) and their two arguments: confusion with prophethood and loss of extraordinariness.
The objectors are from both Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the author's command to reject their view
Note the emphatic phrase 'Inbidhan kalamahu' (cast aside his speech) and understand it as a strong dismissal of the objection.
This shows the certainty of the Sunni position.
3
Grasp the two refutations
First refutation: mu'jiza is distinguished by a claim of prophethood and a challenge, absent in karāma. Second refutation: the occurrence of many wonders does not make them habitual; they remain extraordinary because they break natural laws.
The second refutation addresses the concern about proliferation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات: رَدُّ شُبه مَن أنكرَ الكَرامات. ومَن نَفَاها يعني الكرامة وقال بعدم جوازها كالأستاذ وأبي عبد الله الحَلَيمي مِن أهل السُّنَّة، وجمهور المعتزلة تمسكا بأنه لو ظهرت الخوارق مِن الأولياء لالتبس النَّبِيُّ بغيره؛ لأنَّ الفَارِقَ إِنَّما هو المُعجزة، ولأنَّها لو ظهرت لكثُرت بكثرة الأولياء، وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة، والفرْضُ كونها كذلك. (إِنْبِذَنْ كَلَامَهُ) أي: اطرَحَنَّهُ عن اعتقادك؛ إذ ليس في وقوعها التِبَاسُ النَّبيِّ بغيره للفرق بين المُعجزة والكرامة باعتبار دعوى النُّبوة والتّحدّي فى المُعجزة دونَ الكرامة. وأما قولهم: إنَّها لو ظهرت لكثرت بكثرة الأولياء... إلخ. فجوابه: المنع؛ لأنَّ غايته استمرار نقض العادات، وذلك لا يوجب كونَه عادَةٌ.
Section on prophethood: Refutation of the doubt of those who deny the karamāt. And those who deny it, meaning the karāma, and say that it is not permissible, like al-Ustadh and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi from Ahl al-Sunna, and the majority of the Mu'tazila, adhering to the argument that if extraordinary events were to appear from the saints, a prophet would be confused with someone else; because the distinguishing factor is only the mu'jiza, and because if they appeared, they would become numerous due to the abundance of saints, and would cease to be extraordinary, while the assumption is that they are such. 'Cast aside his speech!' i.e., discard it from your belief; for in their occurrence there is no confusion of a prophet with someone else due to the difference between the mu'jiza and the karāma with regard to the claim of prophethood and the challenge in the mu'jiza, not in the karāma. As for their statement: if they appeared, they would become numerous due to the abundance of saints... etc. The answer is: denial; because its end is the continuous breaking of habits, and that does not necessitate it being a habit.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the possibility of karamāt (saintly wonders). The author first states who denies it: some Ash'aris like al-Ustadh and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi, and the majority of the Mu'tazila. Their objection is that if extraordinary events occurred through saints, it would cause confusion between a prophet and a non-prophet, because the only distinguishing sign is the miracle (mu'jiza). Moreover, if such wonders were to appear, they would become numerous due to the many saints, thereby ceasing to be extraordinary. The author responds: 'Cast aside his speech!' because there is no confusion, as the difference between a mu'jiza and a karama lies in the claim of prophethood and the challenge (tahaddi) that accompanies a mu'jiza but not a karama. As for the second argument about proliferation, the answer is that this is denied (al-man'): the occurrence of wonders does not stop being extraordinary simply because they are many; the point is that such events are a continuous breaking of habits, and multiplicity does not turn them into a habit.

Key Terms
كرامة (karāma)
معجزة (mu'jiza)
ولاية (wilāya)
خارق للعادة (khāriq li-l-ʿāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamāt negate the uniqueness of prophethood by making it impossible to distinguish prophets from saints.
Correction: Prophets are distinguished by their claim to prophethood and the challenge (tahaddī) that accompanies their miracles; saints do not make such claims.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a prophetic miracle (mu'jiza) from a saintly wonder (karāma) according to this passage?
A) Mu'jiza benefits many people, karāma only one.
B) Mu'jiza is accompanied by a claim of prophethood and a challenge, while karāma is not.
C) Mu'jiza is always greater in magnitude.
D) There is no distinction.

Key Takeaway
The difference between a prophetic miracle and a saintly wonder is the claim of prophethood and the challenge that accompanies the former but not the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who performs remarkable acts or has spiritual charisma, do you instinctively assume they are divinely guided? How will you now use the criteria of 'claim to prophethood' and 'challenge' to test such claims in your own life?
?
The objectors feared that karamāt would cause confusion between prophets and saints. Is there any person, scholar, or spiritual leader you have followed without verifying their core message? Are you willing to re-examine that loyalty in light of the distinction this lesson teaches?
?
The second argument worried that karamāt would become commonplace and lose their wonder. Have you grown numb to the extraordinary signs of God in your daily life—like the sunrise, your own existence, or answered prayers? How can you train your heart to see every blessing as a breaking of habit that should increase your awe?

Two Mu'tazilite Views on the Term of the Slain
The author recounts two Mu'tazilite views on whether the slain dies by his ajal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on whether a slain person dies by his appointed term (ajal) and how they differ from the Sunni doctrine.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites disagree: some say the slain has two ajals, others say the killer cuts the ajal short.

In addition to the Sunni view that every person has a single, invariable ajal determined by Allah, some Mu'tazilites proposed alternative theories. This passage presents two such positions — one from al-Ka'bi and another held by many Mu'tazila. Understanding these differing views helps clarify the Sunni insistence on a single ajal and the theological issues at stake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice that two distinct Mu'tazilite views are presented, each introduced by 'such as the doctrine of...'
2
Identify the two views
Locate the view of al-Ka'bi (first) and the view of 'many of the Mu'tazila' (second). Notice how each view defines the relationship between killing, death, and the term.
Al-Ka'bi distinguishes between killing (act of servant) and death (act of God).
3
Compare the two views
Al-Ka'bi posits two ajals (killing and death), while the other view says the killer cuts off the victim's single ajal. How do these differ?
The first view implies the slain would have lived to a natural death if not killed; the second says the slain was deprived of his preordained term.
4
Reflect on the significance
Both views oppose the Sunni doctrine of a single, unalterable ajal. Ask yourself: why does it matter whether the ajal can be changed by human action?
This connects to divine predestination (qadar) and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغير هذا من مذاهب المخالفين ، كمذهب الكعبي من المعتزلة: أن المقتول ليس بميت؛ لأن القتل فعل العبد، والموت فعله تعالى وأثر صنعه، فالمقتول له أجلان القتل والموت وأنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أجله الذي هو الموت. وكمذهب الكثير من المعتزلة أن القاتل قطع على المقتول أجله، وأنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أمد هو أجله الذي علم الله موته فيه.
And other than this from the doctrines of the opponents, such as the doctrine of al-Ka'bi from the Mu'tazila: that the slain is not dead because killing is the act of the servant, and death is His act and effect, so the slain has two terms: the term of killing and the term of death, and that if he were not killed he would live to his term which is death. And like the doctrine of many of the Mu'tazila that the killer cut short the slain's term, and that if he were not killed he would live to a limit which is his term that God knows his death therein.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents two opposing Mu'tazilite views on the ajal. The first, attributed to al-Ka'bi, distinguishes between killing (act of the servant) and death (act of God), so the slain has two ajals: one for when he is killed, and another for when he would have died naturally. If not killed, he would live to his natural death. The second view, held by many Mu'tazila, asserts that the killer actually cuts short the ajal: the slain had a fixed term but was deprived of it by the killer, so if not killed he would have lived to that term. Both views oppose the Sunni doctrine of a single, invariable ajal.

Key Terms
الكعبي
المعتزلة
أجلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agree on the ajal of the slain.
Correction: This passage shows internal division: al-Ka'bi proposes two ajals, while many others say a single ajal is cut short.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi's view, how many ajals does a slain person have?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites themselves were divided on whether the slain has two ajals (al-Ka'bi) or one ajal cut short by the killer (majority view).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of someone dying suddenly or unjustly, do you feel their life was 'cut short'? Which Mu'tazilite view—al-Ka'bi's two terms or the majority's cut-short term—resonates more with that feeling? How does that expose your heart's trust in Allah's decree? Commit today to say 'Allah's decree is perfect' even when it hurts.
?
Al-Ka'bi separates killing (human act) from death (divine act). Do you ever secretly separate events into 'what people did to me' and 'what Allah decreed', as if they are independent? How does this lesson challenge you to see everything—even human wrongdoing—as under Allah's control? Make a concrete intention to thank Allah for a trial you previously blamed on someone.
?
The Mu'tazila debated the ajal because they wanted to defend human responsibility. But does your own desire to understand suffering sometimes lead you to question Allah's justice? This passage shows even scholars disagreed. What is one area of life where you need to stop analyzing and simply submit to Allah's decree? Write down one step to increase your trust this week.

Refuting Denial of the Prophet's Intercession
Refutation of those who deny the Prophet's intercession for major sinners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the Prophet's intercession for major sinners and the refutation of the Mu'tazila denial, including the status of a relevant hadith.

Core Idea
The Prophet's intercession benefits major sinners; a hadith to the contrary is fabricated.

This passage from the commentary addresses a critical point of Sunni creed: the reality and scope of the Prophet's intercession. It directly refutes the Mu'tazila and others who restrict intercession to only those without major sins. The commentator also deals with a problematic narration, showing that sound doctrine does not rely on weak or fabricated texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This lesson addresses a sectarian issue: the Mu'tazila deny the Prophet's intercession for major sinners. Recognize that this is a refutation of their position.
The Mu'tazila are a theological school known for rationalism.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic carefully. Notice the key terms: مانعي الشفاعة, المعتزلة, موضوع, أهل الكبائر.
The passage is concise; each part serves a purpose.
3
Analyze the refutation
The passage has three parts: (1) explaining the poem's line, (2) refuting the Mu'tazila, (3) critiquing a hadith. Understand how each part builds the argument.
4
Reflect on the hadith critique
The hadith denying intercession is declared fabricated. Even if it were authentic, it would apply only to apostates. This protects the doctrine of intercession for all Muslims.
Consensus (ittifāq) is important in hadith criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسكات مانعي الشفاعة وردها وقصد بقوله : (لَا تَمْنَع) أي: لا تعتقد امتناع شفاعته صلى الله عليه وسلم في أهل الكبائر ،وغيرهم لا قبل دخولهم النَّار ولا بعده.. الرَّدَّ على المعتزلة ومن وافقهم، وحديث: «لا تَنالُ شفاعتي أهل الكبائرِ مِن أُمَّتِي» مَوْضوع باتفاق ، وبتقدير صحته : هو محمول على من ارتدَّ منهم.
Arguments of the deniers of intercession and their refutation. And he intended by his statement 'do not prevent' meaning: do not believe the prevention of his intercession, peace be upon him, for the people of major sins and others, neither before their entry into the Fire nor after. The refutation of the Mu'tazila and those who agree with them. And the hadith: 'My intercession will not reach the people of major sins of my ummah' is fabricated by consensus, and if it were authentic, it is interpreted as referring to those who apostatized from them.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage explains a line from the poem about not preventing (lā tamna‘) intercession. The commentator clarifies that this means one should not believe that the Prophet's intercession is prevented for people of major sins or others, whether before or after entering Hell. This is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazila and their followers who deny such intercession. Then it addresses a problematic hadith that claims the Prophet's intercession does not reach major sinners. The commentator declares it fabricated by consensus. Even if it were authentic, it would be interpreted as applying only to apostates (those who have left the religion), not to sinners who remain Muslims. This preserves the doctrine that intercession benefits major sinners.

Key Terms
مانعي الشفاعة
المعتزلة
موضوع
أهل الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's intercession does not reach major sinners.
Correction: The hadith claiming this is fabricated; the correct belief is that the Prophet's intercession benefits all Muslims, including major sinners.

Checkpoint
How do the commentators treat the hadith 'لا تنال شفاعتي أهل الكبائر من أمتي'?
A) They accept it as authentic
B) They declare it fabricated and if authentic, it applies to apostates
C) They say it applies only to disbelievers
D) They ignore it

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's intercession is not prevented for any Muslim, and a hadith claiming otherwise is unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson refutes those who deny intercession. Is there any part of your heart that resists accepting mercy for sinners? How can you actively cultivate hope in Allah's mercy through the Prophet's intercession?

The Ash'ari Position on 'Thing' as 'Existent'
The author defines 'thing' as existent and affirms coextensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of 'thing' as synonymous with 'existent' and why this distinguishes them from the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
In Ash'ari theology, 'thing' means 'existent' — nothing more.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari stance on the term 'thing' (shay'). The author clarifies that for the Ash'aris, 'thing' is equivalent to 'existent' (mawjud), referring only to what is established in reality. This sets the stage for distinguishing Ash'ari ontology from that of the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text along with its translation. Notice the key terms: شيء, موجود, ثابت.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Find where the author states 'the thing is the existent' (الشيء هو الموجود). Understand that this is a definition.
This is a doctrinal position, not a linguistic claim.
3
Understand 'coextensive'
The passage says 'they are coextensive in truth' (متساويان صدقًا). This means they refer to the same set of objects.
Coextensive does not mean identical in meaning; it means same reference.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this matters: the Mu'tazila considered non-existent things as 'things'. The Ash'aris restrict 'thing' to existents.
This shapes debates about attributes and God's knowledge of non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ شرعَ في مسائلَ ينفعُ عِلمُها، ولا يَضرُّ جهلها في العقيدة لدعاء الحاجة إليها، فقال : (وَعِنْدَنَا) مَعاشِر أهل الحقِّ مِن الأشاعرة (الشَّيء هُوَ المَوْجُودُ ) أي : اسمٌ للموجود الكائن الثابت، أنَّ الثابت، يعني: (الشَّيء) ومدلوله هو معنى الموجود ومدلوله ; فهما متساويان صدقًا;
Then he embarked upon issues whose knowledge is beneficial and ignorance of them does not harm in creed due to the need for them, and he said: 'And according to us, the community of the people of truth from the Ash'aris: The thing is the existent.' That is, a name for the existent, the being, the established. That the established, meaning: the thing and its referent is the meaning of the existent and its referent; thus they are coextensive in truth.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'thing' (shay') from an Ash'ari perspective. The author introduces a new set of issues that are beneficial to know but not essential for creed. He then states the Ash'ari position: 'the thing is the existent.' He explains that 'thing' is a name for what is existent (mawjud), being (ka'in), and established (thabit) in external reality. He clarifies that the term 'thing' and its referent are the same as the term 'existent' and its referent; they are coextensive in terms of truth (i.e., they apply to the same objects). This position distinguishes the Ash'aris from the Mu'tazila, who considered non-existent things (ma'dum) as 'things' in some sense.

Key Terms
شيء (shay')
موجود (mawjud)
ثابت (thabit)
تساوي (tasawi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'thing' can refer to non-existent objects, such as possible but not yet created entities.
Correction: The Ash'aris assert that 'thing' is synonymous with 'existent', so only what exists in reality is a 'thing'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between 'thing' and 'existent'?
A) They are identical in meaning.
B) They are coextensive in reference.
C) They are unrelated.
D) 'Thing' is broader.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school defines 'thing' as synonymous with 'existent', restricting it to what actually exists in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one fear or worry you have about something that has not yet happened and may never happen. According to this lesson, that non-existent outcome is not even a 'thing' in reality. How does this perspective free you to focus only on what truly exists right now? Commit today to consciously dismiss this worry for one day and see how it affects your peace.
?
Your actions, words, and intentions are 'things' because they exist in reality. If they are truly existent and recorded in Allah's knowledge, how should this certainty transform the way you approach your next interaction or task? Name one specific action you will take today to make that existence beneficial.

The Sophist Argument and Its Refutation
Presents the Sophist argument and the Sunni refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sophist argument denying external reality and how the People of the Sunnah refuted it by choosing the option of finite division.

Core Idea
The Sophist denial is refuted by accepting finite division and rejecting their proofs as specious.

Before we can affirm the reality of external things, we must address the most radical denial: the Sophists who claim nothing exists at all. In this passage, the author presents their argument—that any claim of existence leads to absurdity—and then gives the Sunni reply. Understanding this debate strengthens our conviction in the objective reality of the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sophist position
Read the description of the 'stubborn' Sophists who deny all realities and claim they are illusions. Notice their assertion that no existent exists at all, even in the mind.
There are three groups, but only the first is detailed here.
2
Examine the Sophist proof
The Sophists argue that any existent must be either divisible into finite parts or infinite parts. They claim both lead to falsehoods—atomism or infinite regress—so nothing is real.
They rely on the denial of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).
3
See the Sunni response
The People of the Sunnah reply by accepting the first option (finite division) and rejecting the Sophists' proofs against the atom as mere specious arguments (shubah), not valid proofs.
This is a direct refutation that preserves the reality of existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات. بيان فرق السُّوفُسْطَائية أصحاب الحكمة المُموهة. وقصده الرَّدُّ على فِرق السُوفسطائية الثَّلاث: العِنادِيَّةِ : الذين ينكرون حقائق الأشياء، ويزعمون أنَّها أوهام وخيالات، جزموا بأنَّه لا موجود أصلا. قوله: (جزموا بأنه لا موجود أصلا) أي لا ذهنا ولا خارجا، قالوا: نجزم بعدم تحقيق نسبة أمر إلى آخر في نفس الأمر، واحتجوا على أهل السنة بأنَّ ما يقولون فيه أنه موجود إما أنْ يقولوا بانقسامه إلى حد محدود وأجزاء متساوية، أو يقولوا بانقسامه إلى أجزاء لا تتناهى، وكل منهما باطل، أما الأول فللأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد، والثاني باطل لما يلزم عليه من تساوي الأجسام، وأن الحجر الصغير قدر الجبل، وما أدى إلى الباطل وهو إثبات الموجودات باطل. وردَّ عليهم أهل السنة : بأنَّا نختارُ الشَّقَ الأول، وقولكم: إنه لا يصح، للأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد لا نُسلّم أنها أدلة بل هي شُبَةٌ. حُكي أن سوفسطائيا أتى إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة ليناظره وكان على بغلة فأمر الإمام بعض تلامذته أن يذهب بالبغلة، فلما خرج السوفسطائي لم يجد البغلة، فطلبها وسأل عنها ، فقال له الإمام : لم يكن لبغلتك حقيقة فلا تطلبها، فرجع عن معتقده ورُدَّت إليه.
The section on transmitted matters. A statement of the sects of the Sophists, the people of deceptive wisdom. Its purpose is the refutation of the three sects of the Sophists: (1) The stubborn ones: those who deny the realities of things and claim that they are illusions and fancies, and they assert that there is no existent at all. His saying: 'they asserted that there is no existent at all' i.e., neither in the mind nor externally. They said: We assert that no attribution of one thing to another is realized in the thing itself. They argued against the People of the Sunnah that what you say is existent is either divisible into a limited limit and equal parts, or divisible into infinite parts, and both are false. As for the first, due to the proofs establishing the negation of the indivisible atom; the second is false because it entails the equality of bodies, that a small stone equals a mountain, and whatever leads to falsehood—which is the affirmation of existences—is false. The People of the Sunnah replied: We choose the first alternative, and your statement that it is not valid due to the proofs establishing the negation of the indivisible atom we do not accept that they are proofs; rather they are specious arguments. It is narrated that a Sophist came to Imam Abu Hanifa to debate him while he was on a she-mule, so the Imam ordered one of his students to take the mule away. When the Sophist came out and did not find the mule, he demanded it and asked about it. The Imam said to him: 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he recanted his belief, and it was returned to him.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sophist (sufastai'iyya) sects, specifically the 'stubborn ones' (al-'inadiyya) who deny the reality of all things, claiming they are mere illusions. The author first states the purpose: to refute their three groups, though only the first is described here. He then presents their argument: that any claim about existence leads to absurdities (either atomism or infinite regress). The author then gives the People of the Sunnah's reply: they accept the first option (division into finite parts) and reject the Sophists' proofs against the indivisible atom as mere sophistry. Finally, a story is recounted to illustrate the practical refutation: Imam Abu Hanifa uses the Sophist's own denial of reality against him when the Sophist's mule is taken. The Imam says, 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it,' causing the Sophist to recant. This lesson reinforces the objective reality of external things from the previous lesson.

Key Terms
سوفسطائية
عنادية
جوهر فرد
حقائق
أوهام
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sophist argument that all claims of existence lead to logical absurdities is valid.
Correction: The Sunni reply shows that the Sophists' dilemma is false because their proofs against the indivisible atom are not valid; they are mere specious arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative that the People of the Sunnah choose in response to the Sophist dilemma?
A) That existents are divisible into infinite parts
B) That existents are indivisible
C) That existents are divisible into a limited limit and equal parts
D) That existents have no reality

Key Takeaway
The Sunni refutation of the Sophist denial is based on accepting finite division and dismissing their proofs against the atom as specious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever denied a truth because the arguments for it seemed weak, only to later realize you were using specious reasoning yourself? What truth are you avoiding today because you refuse to accept the 'first alternative' of clarity and accountability? Will you face it now?
?
The Sophists dismissed reality with clever arguments. In your daily life, do you dismiss your own faults or the rights of others using specious justifications? Write down one such specious argument you have used and commit to acting against it this week.
?
The Sunnis rejected the Sophist proofs as mere shubah. What 'shubah' do you rely on to avoid doing what you know is right—whether in worship, relationships, or self-discipline? Resolve to abandon one specious excuse today and take a concrete step toward the truth.

Abu Hanifa's Practical Refutation
Narrates Imam Abu Hanifa's practical refutation of a Sophist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Abu Hanifa used the Sophist's own denial of reality against him in a practical encounter, leading to his recantation.

Core Idea
A practical demonstration can refute denial more effectively than abstract argument.

After examining the logical refutation of the Sophist argument, we now see a powerful practical example. Imam Abu Hanifa encounters a Sophist and uses the Sophist's own premise—that nothing is real—to expose the absurdity. This story reinforces the objective reality of external things in a memorable way.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the story of the Sophist coming to debate Imam Abu Hanifa while riding a mule. Notice the Imam's clever tactic.
2
Analyze the Imam's strategy
The Imam has the mule taken and then uses the Sophist's own denial of reality to argue that the mule never existed, so the Sophist cannot demand it.
This is a reductio ad absurdum applied to the Sophist's own situation.
3
Observe the outcome
The Sophist recants his belief and the mule is returned. This shows the power of a practical contradiction to change a person's mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The section on transmitted matters. A statement of the sects of the Sophists, the people of deceptive wisdom. Its purpose is the refutation of the three sects of the Sophists: (1) The stubborn ones: those who deny the realities of things and claim that they are illusions and fancies, and they assert that there is no existent at all. His saying: 'they asserted that there is no existent at all' i.e., neither in the mind nor externally. They said: We assert that no attribution of one thing to another is realized in the thing itself. They argued against the People of the Sunnah that what you say is existent is either divisible into a limited limit and equal parts, or divisible into infinite parts, and both are false. As for the first, due to the proofs establishing the negation of the indivisible atom; the second is false because it entails the equality of bodies, that a small stone equals a mountain, and whatever leads to falsehood—which is the affirmation of existences—is false. The People of the Sunnah replied: We choose the first alternative, and your statement that it is not valid due to the proofs establishing the negation of the indivisible atom we do not accept that they are proofs; rather they are specious arguments. It is narrated that a Sophist came to Imam Abu Hanifa to debate him while he was on a she-mule, so the Imam ordered one of his students to take the mule away. When the Sophist came out and did not find the mule, he demanded it and asked about it. The Imam said to him: 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he recanted his belief, and it was returned to him.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Sophist (sufastai'iyya) sects, specifically the 'stubborn ones' (al-'inadiyya) who deny the reality of all things, claiming they are mere illusions. The author first states the purpose: to refute their three groups, though only the first is described here. He then presents their argument: that any claim about existence leads to absurdities (either atomism or infinite regress). The author then gives the People of the Sunnah's reply: they accept the first option (division into finite parts) and reject the Sophists' proofs against the indivisible atom as mere sophistry. Finally, a story is recounted to illustrate the practical refutation: Imam Abu Hanifa uses the Sophist's own denial of reality against him when the Sophist's mule is taken. The Imam says, 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it,' causing the Sophist to recant. This lesson reinforces the objective reality of external things from the previous lesson.

Key Terms
سوفسطائية
أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Abu Hanifa refuse to acknowledge the mule?
A) He wanted to keep the mule
B) He genuinely believed it was not real
C) He was using the Sophist's own denial of reality against him
D) He was playing a trick

Key Takeaway
A practical application of a philosophical principle can expose its absurdity and lead to its rejection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any area of your life where you act as if something is not real (like the Sophist with the mule) until you need it? When you need comfort, you assume God's mercy is real; when you want to sin, you downplay His awareness. Reflect on this inconsistency. How will you reconcile your belief with your actions starting today?
?
The Sophist denied reality until it affected him personally. In what practical way have you recently demanded from Allah something you claim not to be fully convinced of? How can you use this story to align your beliefs with your daily behavior?
?
Imam Abu Hanifa forced the Sophist to face the absurdity of his own worldview. What is one belief you hold that becomes untenable when you apply it to a concrete situation in your life? What step will you take to abandon that inconsistency?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
7 min
Refuting al-ʿInādiyya: The Denial of Existence
Definition, argument, and refutation of al-ʿInādiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beliefs of al-ʿInādiyya sect and how Ahl al-Sunna refuted their denial of existence.

Core Idea
Al-ʿInādiyya claim nothing exists; Ahl al-Sunna affirm existence and refute their atomism-based argument.

The first sophist sect, al-ʿInādiyya, takes an extreme position by denying all existence. They argue that the division of atoms leads to absurdity, thus nothing exists. In this lesson, we examine their claim, their proof from atomism, and the response from Ahl al-Sunna, including a famous story of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ʿInādiyya
Understand their core denial of existence—they claim all things are illusions and fantasies.
Note the etymology: 'īnād' means obstinacy.
2
Examine their argument from atomism
They present a dilemma: either the atom divides into finite equal parts or infinite parts, both supposedly impossible.
3
Learn the Ahl al-Sunna response
Ahl al-Sunna choose the finite division alternative and reject the proofs against the atom as specious.
They assert the existence of the indivisible atom.
4
Reflect on the Imam Abu Hanifa story
The story shows a practical refutation: a sophist's mule disappears, and the Imam uses the sophist's own logic against him.
The sophist recants and the mule is returned.

بيان فرق السُّوفُسْطَائية أصحاب الحكمة المُموهة وقصده الرَّدُّ على فِرق السوفسطائية الثَّلاث: العِنادِيَّةِ: الذين ينكرون حقائق الأشياء، ويزعمون أنَّها أوهام وخيالات، جزموا بأنَّه لا موجود أصلا. السُّوْفَسْطَائية بضم السين المهملة فسكون الواو، وفتح الفاء فسكون السين المهملة، نسبة إلى سوف أسطا بقطع الهمزة، وسوف اسم للعلم، وأسطا اسم للفظ المزخرف، فمعناه بلغة اليونان الحكمة المزينة الظاهر الفاسدة الباطن، وهم قوم من حكماء اليونان توغلوا بالرياضة حتى أتوا بالهذيان. الشنواني. العنادية بكسر العين نسبة للعناد، لأنهم عاندوا العقلاء في ثبوت الواجب والممكن، وادعوا أن الحقائق الثابتة خيالات كأحكام النائم. الشنواني. جزموا بأنه لا موجود أصلا أي لا ذهنا ولا خارجا، قالوا: نجزم بعدم تحقيق نسبة أمر إلى آخر في نفس الأمر، واحتجوا على أهل السنة بأن ما يقولون فيه أنه موجود إما أن يقولوا بانقسامه إلى حد محدود وأجزاء متساوية، أو يقولوا بانقسامه إلى أجزاء لا تتناهى، وكل منهما باطل، أما الأول فللأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد، والثاني باطل لما يلزم عليه من تساوي الأجسام، وأن الحجر الصغير قدر الجبل، وما أدى إلى الباطل وهو إثبات الموجودات باطل. وردَّ عليهم أهل السنة : بأنَّا نختارُ الشَّقَ الأول، وقولكم: إنه لا يصح، للأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد لا نُسلّم أنها أدلة بل هي شُبَةٌ. حُكي أن سوفسطائيا أتى إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة ليناظره وكان على بغلة فأمر الإمام بعض تلامذته أن يذهب بالبغلة، فلما خرج السوفسطائي لم يجد البغلة، فطلبها وسأل عنها ، فقال له الإمام : لم يكن لبغلتك حقيقة فلا تطلبها ، فرجع عن معتقده ورُدَّت إليه. الشنواني. الموجود والثابت مترادفان فيلزم انتفاء الحكم في قول المصنف: وثابت في الخارج الموجود، لانتفاء شرط الحكم وهو تغير المحمول والموضوع مفهوما، فأجاب الشارح بمنع انتفاء شرط الحكم ببيان اختلاف مفهومي الموضوع والمحمول، لأن الموضوع وهو الموجود لوحظ فيه اعتقاد ثبوته، والمحمول وهو ثابت في الخارج وما في نفس الأمر. الشنواني. والعِنْدِيَّةِ: الذين ينكرون ثبوت حقائق الأشياء في نفسها، وتقررها على ما تُشاهد عليه، زعموا أنَّها تابعة للعند والاعتقاد. تابعة للعند والاعتقاد عطف تفسير، فإذا اعتقدوا بأن الحجر نار صار نارا، والآدمي حجرا أو نارا صار حجرا أو نارا، واستدلوا على ذلك بأن الصفراوي يجد الحلو مرا إذا اعتقده، واعتُرضَ: بأنَّ مرارة الحلو إنما حصلت من أمر عارض وهي هيجان الصفراء، لأنها من حيث الاء الاعتقاد، ألا أنه تری لو زالت غلبة الصفراء زالت المرارة، بخلاف ما لو زال الاعتقاد دون غلبتها فإن المرارة ما زالت باقية الشنواني فالحاصل أن العندية هم الذين يقولون : العبرة بما عندنا وباعتقادنا. واللَّا أَدْرِيَّةِ: الذين يُنكرون العِلم بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته، زعموا أنَّهم لا درايةَ لهم بحقيقة من الحقائق، وهم قوم كُفَّار. واللاأدرية بفتح الهمزة بعد اللامين، فسكون الدال وكسر الراء وتشديد التحتية، نسبة لـ لا أدري، لأنهم يزعمون أنهم لا يدركون الحقائق، وجه دخولهم في المتن: أن حكمهم بثبوت حقائق الموجودات فرع للعلم بثبوتها، فلا يقال إن المصنف تركهم في المتن، والذي تَعرَّض لهم صريحا السعد حيث قال: والعلم بها متحقق. الشنواني. بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته أي عدم ثبوته بأن قالوا لا ندري بثبوته ولا عدم ثبوته، وشبهتهم في ذلك أنه لما تطرق البطلان للحاكم بالأمور الحسية بالنظر للعندية، وبالأمور العقلية بالنسبة للعنادية .. وجب التوقفُ، فيقولون: لا ندري الثبوت أو العدم، فلو قيل لشخص منهم : أنت حي أو ميت ؟ لقال : لا أدري. الشنواني. وهم قوم كفار أي السوفسطائية الثلاث. الشنواني.
An explanation of the sects of the Sophists, the people of deceptive wisdom. And his intention is to refute the three sects of the Sophists: al-ʿInādiyya: those who deny the realities of things, and claim that they are illusions and fantasies; they categorically assert that there is no existent at all. al-Sūfisṭāʾiyya: with dammah on the sīn, then sukūn on the wāw, then fatḥah on the fāʾ, then sukūn on the sīn, a relative noun to Sūf Asṭā with the hamzah cut; Sūf is a name for knowledge, and Asṭā is a name for embellished speech; its meaning in the language of the Greeks is wisdom whose exterior is adorned and whose interior is corrupt; they are a people among the Greek philosophers who delved into asceticism until they produced nonsense. al-Shanwānī. al-ʿInādiyya: with kasrah on the ʿayn, a relative noun to ʿinād (obstinacy), because they obstinately opposed the rational people regarding the affirmation of the Necessary and the contingent, and claimed that the established realities are fantasies like the perceptions of a sleeping person. al-Shanwānī. They categorically asserted that there is no existent at all, meaning neither mentally nor externally. They said: we affirm with certainty that there is no verification of a relation of one thing to another in reality. And they argued against Ahl al-Sunna that what they say about it being existent is either that they say it is divided into a limited boundary and equal parts, or that they say it is divided into infinite parts; and both are invalid. As for the first, because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom; the second is invalid because it necessitates equality of bodies, and that a small stone equals a mountain; and whatever leads to falsehood—which is the affirmation of existents—is false. And Ahl al-Sunna replied to them: we choose the first alternative; and as for your saying that it is not valid because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom, we do not concede that they are proofs; rather they are specious arguments. It is related that a Sophist came to Imam Abū Ḥanīfah to debate him while he was on a mule; the Imam ordered one of his students to take the mule. When the Sophist came out, he did not find the mule, so he sought it and asked about it. The Imam said to him: 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he retracted his belief, and the mule was returned to him. al-Shanwānī. The existent and the fixed are synonymous, thus the judgment would be nullified in the author's statement: 'and fixed in the external, the existent', because the condition of judgment—which is the change of the predicate and subject in concept—is absent. So the commentator answered by denying the absence of the condition for the judgment, by explaining the difference in concept between the subject and the predicate: because the subject, which is 'the existent', is considered with the belief in its affirmation, while the predicate, which is 'fixed in the external', is what is in reality. al-Shanwānī. And al-ʿIndiyya: those who deny the affirmation of the realities of things in themselves, and their being established as they are observed; they claim that they are dependent on opinion and belief. subservience to opinion and belief is an explanatory conjunction; if they believe that a stone is fire, it becomes fire; if they believe that a human is a stone or fire, he becomes a stone or fire. And they argued for that by saying that the bilious person finds something sweet as bitter if he believes it to be so. And it was objected: that the bitterness of something sweet only arises from an accidental matter, which is the agitation of bile, because it is from the perspective of belief; indeed you see that if the predominance of bile goes away, the bitterness goes away, unlike if the belief goes away without the predominance going away, the bitterness remains. (al-Shanwānī) The upshot is that al-ʿIndiyya are those who say: the consideration is according to what is with us and our belief. And al-Lā Adariyya: those who deny knowledge of the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation; they claim that they have no knowledge of any reality; and they are a people of disbelievers. al-Lā Adariyya: with fatḥah on the hamzah after the two lāms, then sukūn on the dāl, kasrah on the rāʾ, and tashdīd on the yāʾ, a relative noun to 'lā adrī' (I do not know), because they claim that they do not perceive realities. The reason for including them in the text: because their judgment of the affirmation of the realities of existents is a branch of the knowledge of their affirmation; so it cannot be said that the author left them out of the text. Al-Saʿd explicitly addressed them when he said: 'and knowledge of them is verified.' al-Shanwānī. [They deny] the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation, i.e., its non-existence; they said: we do not know its affirmation nor its non-existence. Their sophism in that is that since falsity has entered upon judgment regarding sensory matters by considering al-ʿIndiyya, and regarding intellectual matters with respect to al-ʿInādiyya, then suspension of judgment became necessary; so they say: we do not know affirmation or non-existence. If one were to say to one of them: 'Are you alive or dead?', he would say: 'I do not know.' al-Shanwānī. And they are a people of disbelievers, i.e., the three Sophist sects. al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of the three Sophist sects. The commentator begins by stating the intention to refute the three Sophist sects. The first is al-ʿInādiyya, who deny the realities of things and assert that nothing exists at all. A footnote explains the etymology of 'Sufistā'iyya' and 'Inādiyya', and then gives a detailed argument: the Sophists argued that the atom cannot be divided into either finite or infinite parts, both leading to absurdities. The Ahl al-Sunna respond by choosing the first alternative and rejecting that the proofs against the atom are valid, calling them specious. A story about Imam Abu Hanifa is told to illustrate the refutation. Then there is a short note on the words 'existent' and 'fixed' being synonymous, addressing a potential objection about the wording of the matn. The second sect is al-ʿIndiyya, who deny the fixed realities of things and claim that things are dependent on belief. The footnote gives an example: if they believe a stone is fire, it becomes fire. They argue from the experience of a bilious person tasting sweet as bitter. The objection is that the bitterness arises from an accidental cause (bile), not from belief alone, as shown by the fact that removing the bile removes the bitterness. The third sect is al-Lā Adariyya, who deny knowledge of whether anything is affirmed or not, claiming they have no knowledge of any reality. The footnote explains that they are included in the text because their judgment is a branch of knowledge, and al-Sa'd explicitly addressed them. Their sophism is that since doubt has entered sensory and intellectual judgments, they suspend judgment. The passage concludes that all three sects are disbelievers.

Key Terms
العنادية
السوفسطائية
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-ʿInādiyya only deny God, but they deny all existence entirely, including the world and souls.
Correction: They assert that nothing exists at all, not even the self.

Checkpoint
What is the core claim of al-ʿInādiyya?
A) Things depend on belief for their reality.
B) There is no existent at all, neither mentally nor externally.
C) We cannot know whether anything exists or not.
D) Only the necessary being exists.

Key Takeaway
Al-ʿInādiyya's denial of existence is refuted by affirming existence and rejecting their atomism-based argument as specious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life have you been stubbornly refusing to accept reality, clinging to denial despite clear evidence? What would it cost you to surrender to the truth today?
?
Imam Abu Hanifa forced the sophist to admit the absurdity of his denial by pointing out the contradiction in his complaint about a mule. Is there a belief you hold that, if followed consistently, would lead to a similar absurdity in your actions?
?
Al-ʿInādiyya denied all existence out of obstinacy. When you are faced with a difficult truth about yourself or your relationship with Allah, do you find yourself subtly denying its reality to avoid change? What step can you take this week to confront that denial?
