Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Refuting al-ʿIndiyya: Reality Depends on Belief
Definition, example, and refutation of al-ʿIndiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the belief of al-ʿIndiyya that realities depend on belief, and how it is refuted by the example of the bilious person.

Core Idea
Al-ʿIndiyya claim that belief creates reality; reason shows that accidental causes, not belief, produce changes.

The second sophist sect, al-ʿIndiyya, asserts that things have no fixed reality but are subject to belief. They argue from the experience of a bilious person tasting sweet as bitter. This lesson examines their claim, their evidence, and the rational refutation that distinguishes accidental causes from belief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of al-ʿIndiyya
They say that the realities of things are not fixed in themselves but are dependent on one's belief (al-ʿind wa al-iʿtiqād).
2
Examine their example of the bilious person
They argue that if a bilious person believes something sweet is bitter, it becomes bitter for them.
3
Learn the objection and refutation
The objection is that the bitterness arises from an accidental cause (bile), not from the belief itself. When the bile goes away, the bitterness disappears.
If belief alone were the cause, removing belief would remove bitterness even if bile remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

السُّوْفَسْطَائية بضم السين المهملة فسكون الواو، وفتح الفاء فسكون السين المهملة، نسبة إلى سوف أسطا بقطع الهمزة، وسوف اسم للعلم، وأسطا اسم للفظ المزخرف، فمعناه بلغة اليونان الحكمة المزينة الظاهر الفاسدة الباطن، وهم قوم من حكماء اليونان توغلوا بالرياضة حتى أتوا بالهذيان. الشنواني. العنادية بكسر العين نسبة للعناد، لأنهم عاندوا العقلاء في ثبوت الواجب والممكن، وادعوا أن الحقائق الثابتة خيالات كأحكام النائم. الشنواني. جزموا بأنه لا موجود أصلا أي لا ذهنا ولا خارجا، قالوا: نجزم بعدم تحقيق نسبة أمر إلى آخر في نفس الأمر، واحتجوا على أهل السنة بأن ما يقولون فيه أنه موجود إما أن يقولوا بانقسامه إلى حد محدود وأجزاء متساوية، أو يقولوا بانقسامه إلى أجزاء لا تتناهى، وكل منهما باطل، أما الأول فللأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد، والثاني باطل لما يلزم عليه من تساوي الأجسام، وأن الحجر الصغير قدر الجبل، وما أدى إلى الباطل وهو إثبات الموجودات باطل. وردَّ عليهم أهل السنة : بأنَّا نختارُ الشَّقَ الأول، وقولكم: إنه لا يصح، للأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد لا نُسلّم أنها أدلة بل هي شُبَةٌ. حُكي أن سوفسطائيا أتى إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة ليناظره وكان على بغلة فأمر الإمام بعض تلامذته أن يذهب بالبغلة، فلما خرج السوفسطائي لم يجد البغلة، فطلبها وسأل عنها ، فقال له الإمام : لم يكن لبغلتك حقيقة فلا تطلبها ، فرجع عن معتقده ورُدَّت إليه. الشنواني. الموجود والثابت مترادفان فيلزم انتفاء الحكم في قول المصنف: وثابت في الخارج الموجود، لانتفاء شرط الحكم وهو تغير المحمول والموضوع مفهوما، فأجاب الشارح بمنع انتفاء شرط الحكم ببيان اختلاف مفهومي الموضوع والمحمول، لأن الموضوع وهو الموجود لوحظ فيه اعتقاد ثبوته، والمحمول وهو ثابت في الخارج وما في نفس الأمر. الشنواني. والعِنْدِيَّةِ: الذين ينكرون ثبوت حقائق الأشياء في نفسها، وتقررها على ما تُشاهد عليه، زعموا أنَّها تابعة للعند والاعتقاد. تابعة للعند والاعتقاد عطف تفسير، فإذا اعتقدوا بأن الحجر نار صار نارا، والآدمي حجرا أو نارا صار حجرا أو نارا، واستدلوا على ذلك بأن الصفراوي يجد الحلو مرا إذا اعتقده، واعتُرضَ: بأنَّ مرارة الحلو إنما حصلت من أمر عارض وهي هيجان الصفراء، لأنها من حيث الاء الاعتقاد، ألا أنه تری لو زالت غلبة الصفراء زالت المرارة، بخلاف ما لو زال الاعتقاد دون غلبتها فإن المرارة ما زالت باقية الشنواني فالحاصل أن العندية هم الذين يقولون : العبرة بما عندنا وباعتقادنا. واللَّا أَدْرِيَّةِ: الذين يُنكرون العِلم بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته، زعموا أنَّهم لا درايةَ لهم بحقيقة من الحقائق، وهم قوم كُفَّار. واللاأدرية بفتح الهمزة بعد اللامين، فسكون الدال وكسر الراء وتشديد التحتية، نسبة لـ لا أدري، لأنهم يزعمون أنهم لا يدركون الحقائق، وجه دخولهم في المتن: أن حكمهم بثبوت حقائق الموجودات فرع للعلم بثبوتها، فلا يقال إن المصنف تركهم في المتن، والذي تَعرَّض لهم صريحا السعد حيث قال: والعلم بها متحقق. الشنواني. بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته أي عدم ثبوته بأن قالوا لا ندري بثبوته ولا عدم ثبوته، وشبهتهم في ذلك أنه لما تطرق البطلان للحاكم بالأمور الحسية بالنظر للعندية، وبالأمور العقلية بالنسبة للعنادية .. وجب التوقفُ، فيقولون: لا ندري الثبوت أو العدم، فلو قيل لشخص منهم : أنت حي أو ميت ؟ لقال : لا أدري. الشنواني. وهم قوم كفار أي السوفسطائية الثلاث. الشنواني.
An explanation of the sects of the Sophists, the people of deceptive wisdom. And his intention is to refute the three sects of the Sophists: al-ʿInādiyya: those who deny the realities of things, and claim that they are illusions and fantasies; they categorically assert that there is no existent at all. al-Sūfisṭāʾiyya: with dammah on the sīn, then sukūn on the wāw, then fatḥah on the fāʾ, then sukūn on the sīn, a relative noun to Sūf Asṭā with the hamzah cut; Sūf is a name for knowledge, and Asṭā is a name for embellished speech; its meaning in the language of the Greeks is wisdom whose exterior is adorned and whose interior is corrupt; they are a people among the Greek philosophers who delved into asceticism until they produced nonsense. al-Shanwānī. al-ʿInādiyya: with kasrah on the ʿayn, a relative noun to ʿinād (obstinacy), because they obstinately opposed the rational people regarding the affirmation of the Necessary and the contingent, and claimed that the established realities are fantasies like the perceptions of a sleeping person. al-Shanwānī. They categorically asserted that there is no existent at all, meaning neither mentally nor externally. They said: we affirm with certainty that there is no verification of a relation of one thing to another in reality. And they argued against Ahl al-Sunna that what they say about it being existent is either that they say it is divided into a limited boundary and equal parts, or that they say it is divided into infinite parts; and both are invalid. As for the first, because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom; the second is invalid because it necessitates equality of bodies, and that a small stone equals a mountain; and whatever leads to falsehood—which is the affirmation of existents—is false. And Ahl al-Sunna replied to them: we choose the first alternative; and as for your saying that it is not valid because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom, we do not concede that they are proofs; rather they are specious arguments. It is related that a Sophist came to Imam Abū Ḥanīfah to debate him while he was on a mule; the Imam ordered one of his students to take the mule. When the Sophist came out, he did not find the mule, so he sought it and asked about it. The Imam said to him: 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he retracted his belief, and the mule was returned to him. al-Shanwānī. The existent and the fixed are synonymous, thus the judgment would be nullified in the author's statement: 'and fixed in the external, the existent', because the condition of judgment—which is the change of the predicate and subject in concept—is absent. So the commentator answered by denying the absence of the condition for the judgment, by explaining the difference in concept between the subject and the predicate: because the subject, which is 'the existent', is considered with the belief in its affirmation, while the predicate, which is 'fixed in the external', is what is in reality. al-Shanwānī. And al-ʿIndiyya: those who deny the affirmation of the realities of things in themselves, and their being established as they are observed; they claim that they are dependent on opinion and belief. subservience to opinion and belief is an explanatory conjunction; if they believe that a stone is fire, it becomes fire; if they believe that a human is a stone or fire, he becomes a stone or fire. And they argued for that by saying that the bilious person finds something sweet as bitter if he believes it to be so. And it was objected: that the bitterness of something sweet only arises from an accidental matter, which is the agitation of bile, because it is from the perspective of belief; indeed you see that if the predominance of bile goes away, the bitterness goes away, unlike if the belief goes away without the predominance going away, the bitterness remains. (al-Shanwānī) The upshot is that al-ʿIndiyya are those who say: the consideration is according to what is with us and our belief. And al-Lā Adariyya: those who deny knowledge of the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation; they claim that they have no knowledge of any reality; and they are a people of disbelievers. al-Lā Adariyya: with fatḥah on the hamzah after the two lāms, then sukūn on the dāl, kasrah on the rāʾ, and tashdīd on the yāʾ, a relative noun to 'lā adrī' (I do not know), because they claim that they do not perceive realities. The reason for including them in the text: because their judgment of the affirmation of the realities of existents is a branch of the knowledge of their affirmation; so it cannot be said that the author left them out of the text. Al-Saʿd explicitly addressed them when he said: 'and knowledge of them is verified.' al-Shanwānī. [They deny] the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation, i.e., its non-existence; they said: we do not know its affirmation nor its non-existence. Their sophism in that is that since falsity has entered upon judgment regarding sensory matters by considering al-ʿIndiyya, and regarding intellectual matters with respect to al-ʿInādiyya, then suspension of judgment became necessary; so they say: we do not know affirmation or non-existence. If one were to say to one of them: 'Are you alive or dead?', he would say: 'I do not know.' al-Shanwānī. And they are a people of disbelievers, i.e., the three Sophist sects. al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of the three Sophist sects. The commentator begins by stating the intention to refute the three Sophist sects. The first is al-ʿInādiyya, who deny the realities of things and assert that nothing exists at all. A footnote explains the etymology of 'Sufistā'iyya' and 'Inādiyya', and then gives a detailed argument: the Sophists argued that the atom cannot be divided into either finite or infinite parts, both leading to absurdities. The Ahl al-Sunna respond by choosing the first alternative and rejecting that the proofs against the atom are valid, calling them specious. A story about Imam Abu Hanifa is told to illustrate the refutation. Then there is a short note on the words 'existent' and 'fixed' being synonymous, addressing a potential objection about the wording of the matn. The second sect is al-ʿIndiyya, who deny the fixed realities of things and claim that things are dependent on belief. The footnote gives an example: if they believe a stone is fire, it becomes fire. They argue from the experience of a bilious person tasting sweet as bitter. The objection is that the bitterness arises from an accidental cause (bile), not from belief alone, as shown by the fact that removing the bile removes the bitterness. The third sect is al-Lā Adariyya, who deny knowledge of whether anything is affirmed or not, claiming they have no knowledge of any reality. The footnote explains that they are included in the text because their judgment is a branch of knowledge, and al-Sa'd explicitly addressed them. Their sophism is that since doubt has entered sensory and intellectual judgments, they suspend judgment. The passage concludes that all three sects are disbelievers.

Key Terms
العندية
العند
الاعتقاد
الصفراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-ʿIndiyya are relativists who say truth is relative to individuals, but they actually claim physical objects change with belief.
Correction: They claim that if you believe a stone is fire, it literally becomes fire.

Checkpoint
What is the distinguishing belief of al-ʿIndiyya?
A) They deny existence altogether.
B) They claim that realities are subservient to belief and opinion.
C) They suspend judgment on all knowledge.
D) They affirm the indivisible atom.

Key Takeaway
Al-ʿIndiyya's claim that belief alters reality is refuted by showing that accidental causes, not belief, produce perceived changes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself assuming that your beliefs about a situation make it true, even when evidence shows otherwise? Where in your life are you clinging to a belief that contradicts reality, and what would it cost you to let go?
?
The bilious person's false taste was due to an internal imbalance. What internal imbalances—pride, fear, desire—might be distorting your perception of truth? Are you willing to seek correction?
?
If you truly believed that your belief could turn a stone into fire, would you test it? Why do we sometimes treat our subjective opinions as if they were reality without checking? What would it mean for your faith if you submitted to objective truth rather than your own inclinations?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Refuting al-Lā Adariyya: The Skeptics
Definition, sophism, and ruling of disbelief for al-Lā Adariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptical position of al-Lā Adariyya and why their suspension of judgment is refuted as disbelief.

Core Idea
Al-Lā Adariyya claim we cannot know anything; Islam affirms knowledge of realities.

The third sophist sect, al-Lā Adariyya, advocates complete skepticism, denying knowledge of any reality. They argue that since both sensory and intellectual judgments have been doubted, one must suspend judgment. This lesson explains their position, why they are included in the text, and why they are considered disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-Lā Adariyya
They deny knowledge of whether anything is affirmed or not; they claim to have no cognition of any reality.
The term comes from 'lā adrī' (I do not know).
2
Understand their sophism
They argue that since the senses can be deceived (as al-ʿIndiyya show) and reason can be faulty (as al-ʿInādiyya show), we must suspend all judgment.
They use the existence of other sophist sects as evidence for doubt.
3
Note the conclusion that they are disbelievers
The passage explicitly states they are disbelievers, because they deny the possibility of knowing truth.
This includes all three sophist sects as disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان فرق السُّوفُسْطَائية أصحاب الحكمة المُموهة وقصده الرَّدُّ على فِرق السوفسطائية الثَّلاث: العِنادِيَّةِ: الذين ينكرون حقائق الأشياء، ويزعمون أنَّها أوهام وخيالات، جزموا بأنَّه لا موجود أصلا. السُّوْفَسْطَائية بضم السين المهملة فسكون الواو، وفتح الفاء فسكون السين المهملة، نسبة إلى سوف أسطا بقطع الهمزة، وسوف اسم للعلم، وأسطا اسم للفظ المزخرف، فمعناه بلغة اليونان الحكمة المزينة الظاهر الفاسدة الباطن، وهم قوم من حكماء اليونان توغلوا بالرياضة حتى أتوا بالهذيان. الشنواني. العنادية بكسر العين نسبة للعناد، لأنهم عاندوا العقلاء في ثبوت الواجب والممكن، وادعوا أن الحقائق الثابتة خيالات كأحكام النائم. الشنواني. جزموا بأنه لا موجود أصلا أي لا ذهنا ولا خارجا، قالوا: نجزم بعدم تحقيق نسبة أمر إلى آخر في نفس الأمر، واحتجوا على أهل السنة بأن ما يقولون فيه أنه موجود إما أن يقولوا بانقسامه إلى حد محدود وأجزاء متساوية، أو يقولوا بانقسامه إلى أجزاء لا تتناهى، وكل منهما باطل، أما الأول فللأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد، والثاني باطل لما يلزم عليه من تساوي الأجسام، وأن الحجر الصغير قدر الجبل، وما أدى إلى الباطل وهو إثبات الموجودات باطل. وردَّ عليهم أهل السنة : بأنَّا نختارُ الشَّقَ الأول، وقولكم: إنه لا يصح، للأدلة القائمة على نفي الجوهر الفرد لا نُسلّم أنها أدلة بل هي شُبَةٌ. حُكي أن سوفسطائيا أتى إلى الإمام أبي حنيفة ليناظره وكان على بغلة فأمر الإمام بعض تلامذته أن يذهب بالبغلة، فلما خرج السوفسطائي لم يجد البغلة، فطلبها وسأل عنها ، فقال له الإمام : لم يكن لبغلتك حقيقة فلا تطلبها ، فرجع عن معتقده ورُدَّت إليه. الشنواني. الموجود والثابت مترادفان فيلزم انتفاء الحكم في قول المصنف: وثابت في الخارج الموجود، لانتفاء شرط الحكم وهو تغير المحمول والموضوع مفهوما، فأجاب الشارح بمنع انتفاء شرط الحكم ببيان اختلاف مفهومي الموضوع والمحمول، لأن الموضوع وهو الموجود لوحظ فيه اعتقاد ثبوته، والمحمول وهو ثابت في الخارج وما في نفس الأمر. الشنواني. والعِنْدِيَّةِ: الذين ينكرون ثبوت حقائق الأشياء في نفسها، وتقررها على ما تُشاهد عليه، زعموا أنَّها تابعة للعند والاعتقاد. تابعة للعند والاعتقاد عطف تفسير، فإذا اعتقدوا بأن الحجر نار صار نارا، والآدمي حجرا أو نارا صار حجرا أو نارا، واستدلوا على ذلك بأن الصفراوي يجد الحلو مرا إذا اعتقده، واعتُرضَ: بأنَّ مرارة الحلو إنما حصلت من أمر عارض وهي هيجان الصفراء، لأنها من حيث الاء الاعتقاد، ألا أنه تری لو زالت غلبة الصفراء زالت المرارة، بخلاف ما لو زال الاعتقاد دون غلبتها فإن المرارة ما زالت باقية الشنواني فالحاصل أن العندية هم الذين يقولون : العبرة بما عندنا وباعتقادنا. واللَّا أَدْرِيَّةِ: الذين يُنكرون العِلم بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته، زعموا أنَّهم لا درايةَ لهم بحقيقة من الحقائق، وهم قوم كُفَّار. واللاأدرية بفتح الهمزة بعد اللامين، فسكون الدال وكسر الراء وتشديد التحتية، نسبة لـ لا أدري، لأنهم يزعمون أنهم لا يدركون الحقائق، وجه دخولهم في المتن: أن حكمهم بثبوت حقائق الموجودات فرع للعلم بثبوتها، فلا يقال إن المصنف تركهم في المتن، والذي تَعرَّض لهم صريحا السعد حيث قال: والعلم بها متحقق. الشنواني. بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته أي عدم ثبوته بأن قالوا لا ندري بثبوته ولا عدم ثبوته، وشبهتهم في ذلك أنه لما تطرق البطلان للحاكم بالأمور الحسية بالنظر للعندية، وبالأمور العقلية بالنسبة للعنادية .. وجب التوقفُ، فيقولون: لا ندري الثبوت أو العدم، فلو قيل لشخص منهم : أنت حي أو ميت ؟ لقال : لا أدري. الشنواني. وهم قوم كفار أي السوفسطائية الثلاث. الشنواني.
An explanation of the sects of the Sophists, the people of deceptive wisdom. And his intention is to refute the three sects of the Sophists: al-ʿInādiyya: those who deny the realities of things, and claim that they are illusions and fantasies; they categorically assert that there is no existent at all. al-Sūfisṭāʾiyya: with dammah on the sīn, then sukūn on the wāw, then fatḥah on the fāʾ, then sukūn on the sīn, a relative noun to Sūf Asṭā with the hamzah cut; Sūf is a name for knowledge, and Asṭā is a name for embellished speech; its meaning in the language of the Greeks is wisdom whose exterior is adorned and whose interior is corrupt; they are a people among the Greek philosophers who delved into asceticism until they produced nonsense. al-Shanwānī. al-ʿInādiyya: with kasrah on the ʿayn, a relative noun to ʿinād (obstinacy), because they obstinately opposed the rational people regarding the affirmation of the Necessary and the contingent, and claimed that the established realities are fantasies like the perceptions of a sleeping person. al-Shanwānī. They categorically asserted that there is no existent at all, meaning neither mentally nor externally. They said: we affirm with certainty that there is no verification of a relation of one thing to another in reality. And they argued against Ahl al-Sunna that what they say about it being existent is either that they say it is divided into a limited boundary and equal parts, or that they say it is divided into infinite parts; and both are invalid. As for the first, because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom; the second is invalid because it necessitates equality of bodies, and that a small stone equals a mountain; and whatever leads to falsehood—which is the affirmation of existents—is false. And Ahl al-Sunna replied to them: we choose the first alternative; and as for your saying that it is not valid because of the proofs established for the negation of the indivisible atom, we do not concede that they are proofs; rather they are specious arguments. It is related that a Sophist came to Imam Abū Ḥanīfah to debate him while he was on a mule; the Imam ordered one of his students to take the mule. When the Sophist came out, he did not find the mule, so he sought it and asked about it. The Imam said to him: 'Your mule had no reality, so do not seek it.' So he retracted his belief, and the mule was returned to him. al-Shanwānī. The existent and the fixed are synonymous, thus the judgment would be nullified in the author's statement: 'and fixed in the external, the existent', because the condition of judgment—which is the change of the predicate and subject in concept—is absent. So the commentator answered by denying the absence of the condition for the judgment, by explaining the difference in concept between the subject and the predicate: because the subject, which is 'the existent', is considered with the belief in its affirmation, while the predicate, which is 'fixed in the external', is what is in reality. al-Shanwānī. And al-ʿIndiyya: those who deny the affirmation of the realities of things in themselves, and their being established as they are observed; they claim that they are dependent on opinion and belief. subservience to opinion and belief is an explanatory conjunction; if they believe that a stone is fire, it becomes fire; if they believe that a human is a stone or fire, he becomes a stone or fire. And they argued for that by saying that the bilious person finds something sweet as bitter if he believes it to be so. And it was objected: that the bitterness of something sweet only arises from an accidental matter, which is the agitation of bile, because it is from the perspective of belief; indeed you see that if the predominance of bile goes away, the bitterness goes away, unlike if the belief goes away without the predominance going away, the bitterness remains. (al-Shanwānī) The upshot is that al-ʿIndiyya are those who say: the consideration is according to what is with us and our belief. And al-Lā Adariyya: those who deny knowledge of the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation; they claim that they have no knowledge of any reality; and they are a people of disbelievers. al-Lā Adariyya: with fatḥah on the hamzah after the two lāms, then sukūn on the dāl, kasrah on the rāʾ, and tashdīd on the yāʾ, a relative noun to 'lā adrī' (I do not know), because they claim that they do not perceive realities. The reason for including them in the text: because their judgment of the affirmation of the realities of existents is a branch of the knowledge of their affirmation; so it cannot be said that the author left them out of the text. Al-Saʿd explicitly addressed them when he said: 'and knowledge of them is verified.' al-Shanwānī. [They deny] the affirmation of a thing or its non-affirmation, i.e., its non-existence; they said: we do not know its affirmation nor its non-existence. Their sophism in that is that since falsity has entered upon judgment regarding sensory matters by considering al-ʿIndiyya, and regarding intellectual matters with respect to al-ʿInādiyya, then suspension of judgment became necessary; so they say: we do not know affirmation or non-existence. If one were to say to one of them: 'Are you alive or dead?', he would say: 'I do not know.' al-Shanwānī. And they are a people of disbelievers, i.e., the three Sophist sects. al-Shanwānī.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of the three Sophist sects. The commentator begins by stating the intention to refute the three Sophist sects. The first is al-ʿInādiyya, who deny the realities of things and assert that nothing exists at all. A footnote explains the etymology of 'Sufistā'iyya' and 'Inādiyya', and then gives a detailed argument: the Sophists argued that the atom cannot be divided into either finite or infinite parts, both leading to absurdities. The Ahl al-Sunna respond by choosing the first alternative and rejecting that the proofs against the atom are valid, calling them specious. A story about Imam Abu Hanifa is told to illustrate the refutation. Then there is a short note on the words 'existent' and 'fixed' being synonymous, addressing a potential objection about the wording of the matn. The second sect is al-ʿIndiyya, who deny the fixed realities of things and claim that things are dependent on belief. The footnote gives an example: if they believe a stone is fire, it becomes fire. They argue from the experience of a bilious person tasting sweet as bitter. The objection is that the bitterness arises from an accidental cause (bile), not from belief alone, as shown by the fact that removing the bile removes the bitterness. The third sect is al-Lā Adariyya, who deny knowledge of whether anything is affirmed or not, claiming they have no knowledge of any reality. The footnote explains that they are included in the text because their judgment is a branch of knowledge, and al-Sa'd explicitly addressed them. Their sophism is that since doubt has entered sensory and intellectual judgments, they suspend judgment. The passage concludes that all three sects are disbelievers.

Key Terms
اللاأدرية
لا أدري
السوفسطائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think skepticism is an intellectually honest position, but Islam holds that knowledge of realities is possible and necessary.
Correction: The passage declares them disbelievers because they deny the possibility of knowledge, which contradicts revealed truth.

Checkpoint
What is the sophism of al-Lā Adariyya?
A) They claim that existence is an illusion.
B) They argue that since sensory and intellectual judgments can be mistaken, we must suspend judgment.
C) They say that belief creates reality.
D) They assert that nothing exists at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Lā Adariyya's skepticism is refuted by affirming that knowledge of realities is possible, and their position is deemed disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted something that Islam affirms as known, even for a moment? What was it, and what does this lesson say about such doubt? Now, make a firm resolve to treat that matter with certainty from today.
?
If someone asked you 'Are you alive or dead?', you would answer with certainty. But are there Islamic truths you treat with less certainty than your own heartbeat? Name one right now, and ask yourself: why do I grant more certainty to my physical existence than to the words of my Creator?
?
The skeptic's claim leads to absurdity. In what area of your life do you habitually suspend judgment about a clear Islamic ruling, perhaps using 'I don't know' as an excuse? What would it cost you to stop being a 'lā adrī' there and seek knowledge until you know?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
4 min
The 'Indiyya and La'dariyya Sects: Denial of Objective Reality
Sanusi defines two sophist sects that deny objective reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of two sophist sects that deny objective reality and why they are considered disbelievers in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Some deny reality is fixed; others claim it is unknowable—both are kufr.

In Islamic theology, the debate about the nature of reality includes rebuttals to skeptical philosophies. Among these are two groups of sophists (sufasṭā'iyya): al-'Indiyya and al-La'dariyya. This lesson defines each and explains why their positions lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise definitions and the concluding ruling.
2
Identify the two sects
The passage presents al-'Indiyya and al-La'dariyya. Find each definition and note what each group claims about reality.
3
Understand the claim of each sect
Al-'Indiyya say realities are dependent on perception and belief. Al-La'dariyya say they have no knowledge of whether anything is real or not.
These are two different forms of skepticism.
4
Note the theological conclusion
The passage declares both groups as disbelievers (kafir). Reflect on why this is a serious charge.
Denying objective reality undermines the basis of divine attributes and revelation.
5
Ask: why does this matter?
Consider how these views conflict with Islamic creed, which affirms that things have fixed realities known to Allah.
This connects to the broader topic of epistemology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في زيادة الوجود على الماهية وعدمه. والعندية: الذين ينكرون ثبوت حقائق الأشياء في نفسها، وتقررها على ما تشاهد عليه، زعموا أنها تابعة للعند والاعتقاد. واللاأدرية: الذين ينكرون العلم بثبوت شيء ولا ثبوته، زعموا أنهم لا دراية لهم بحقيقة من الحقائق، وهم قوم كفار (ه).
The dispute about whether existence is additional to essence or not. And al-'Indiyya: those who deny the establishment of the realities of things in themselves, and their being fixed as they are witnessed, claiming that they are dependent on perception and belief. And al-La'dariyya: those who deny knowledge of the establishment of something or its non-establishment, claiming that they have no knowledge of any reality, and they are a people of disbelievers (h).
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage defines two of the three sophist sects. It first states the broader context: the dispute about whether existence is added to essence or not. Then it defines al-'Indiyya: they deny that things have fixed realities in themselves; rather, they claim that realities are relative to one's belief and perception. Then it defines al-La'dariyya: they suspend judgment, claiming ignorance of whether anything is real or not. The passage concludes that these sects are disbelievers (kufar).

Key Terms
العندية
اللاأدرية
السوفسطائية
كفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These sects are just philosophical skeptics.
Correction: The text explicitly states they are disbelievers (kafir) because they deny the fixed realities of things, which undermines the basis of divine attributes and revelation.

Checkpoint
What do the al-'Indiyya assert about the reality of things?
A) They exist independently of perception.
B) They are dependent on belief and perception.
C) They are nonexistent.
D) They are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Both al-'Indiyya and al-La'dariyya deny objective reality, and this denial makes them disbelievers in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a confusing or contradictory situation, do you ever find yourself tempted to say 'truth is relative' or 'it's all a matter of perspective'? How does this lesson challenge that impulse, and what will you do the next time that temptation arises?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never truly examined. Could you be unknowingly acting like al-'Indiyya by making that belief dependent on your own perception rather than on the objective reality of what Allah has revealed? What step will you take this week to verify that belief against the sources?
?
This lesson declares that denying objective truth is kufr. Is there any area of your life where you have suspended judgment—refusing to decide whether something is true or false—because it was inconvenient or uncomfortable? What would it cost you to commit to seeking the truth instead?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Refutation of Mu'tazila on Nullification of Tawbah
Al-Shanwani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that returning to sin invalidates previous tawbah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that returning to sin after tawbah does not invalidate the previous repentance.

Core Idea
A valid tawbah is not undone by a later sin; each sin is a separate moral act.

The Mu'tazila argued that a valid tawbah requires never repeating the sin; if repeated, the tawbah is nullified and past sins return. This passage refutes that view, establishing that tawbah, once accepted, remains valid. The new sin is a separate violation requiring its own repentance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Learn what the Mu'tazila considered a condition for tawbah: that one never returns to the sin. If they return, tawbah is nullified.
This sets up the opposing view.
2
Read the passage and refutation
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the author's direct refutation: 'There is no nullification'.
The author uses a concise statement.
3
Distinguish tawbah from new sin
Understand that returning to sin does not revive old sins; it is a new transgression that requires its own tawbah.
Each sin is independently accounted for.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this view encourages continuous repentance without despair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمَّا ذهبَ المعتزلة إلى أنَّ مِن شروط صحة التَّوبة أنْ لا يُعاود الذَّنب بعد التوبة ، فإن عاوده انتقضت توبته وعادت ذنوبه .. رَدَّ عليهم بقوله: (وَلَا انْتِقَاضَ) لتوبة التائب الشَّرعيَّة (إِنْ يَعُدْ لِلْحَال) أي: إِنْ للحالة الأولى التي كانَ عليها مِن التَّلبس بالذنوب، ولا تعود ذنوبه التي تابَ مِنها عليه، بل عَودُه ونقضُه مَعصيةٌ أخرى ، يجب عليه أن يُجدّد منها توبة أخرى ، كما أشار إليه بقوله:
And when the Mu'tazila held that among the conditions for the validity of tawbah is that one does not return to the sin after tawbah, and if he returns, his tawbah is nullified and his sins return, he refuted them by his saying: (There is no nullification) of the tawbah of a legitimate penitent (if he returns to the state), meaning if he returns to the first state that he was in of being engrossed in sins, and his sins that he repented from do not return to him; rather, his return and violation is another sin, for which he must renew a different tawbah, as he indicated by his saying:
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that a valid tawbah is conditional on never committing the sin again. The author states the Mu'tazila position: if one repeats the sin, the previous tawbah is nullified and the sins return. Then he refutes them by stating that tawbah, once valid, is not nullified (lā intiqāḍ). If the person returns to the same state of sinfulness, it does not reinstate the forgiven sins; rather, the new sin is a separate offense requiring its own tawbah. The author emphasizes that each sin is an independent act, and tawbah addresses each individually. This preserves the integrity of repentance and opposes the Mu'tazilite notion of retroactive nullification.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
انتقاض
توبة
حالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I sin again after tawbah, my previous repentance is nullified and all sins return.
Correction: Tawbah, once valid, remains valid. The new sin is a separate offense that requires its own tawbah, not a nullification of the first.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position refuting the Mu'tazila, what happens to the previous tawbah if one commits the same sin again?
A) It becomes null and void
B) It remains valid and the new sin is a separate transgression
C) It is partially invalid
D) It requires renewal of all past tawbah

Key Takeaway
A valid tawbah is not nullified by subsequent sin; each sin is independently repented for.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a past tawbah was futile because you sinned again later? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what does it demand you believe about Allah's acceptance of your repentance?
?
If you are currently struggling with a sin you have repented from before, does this lesson give you the courage to see that struggle not as a failure of your earlier repentance but as a new opportunity for a fresh tawbah? What specific step will you take today to turn back to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila condition for tawbah can lead to despair: if one fails, all is lost. How has this lesson exposed a hidden pride or fear in your own heart about your relationship with Allah? Will you now repent more freely, knowing each sin is independent?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Following the Salaf and Avoiding Blameworthy Innovation
Command to follow the Salaf and avoid innovation, with definition of Sunnah and bid'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to follow the righteous predecessors (Salaf) and to avoid blameworthy innovations, and the criteria for distinguishing Sunnah from bid'ah.

Core Idea
True faith requires conformity to the Sunnah, which includes consensus and clear analogy.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Sunni methodology: the necessity of following the righteous predecessors and avoiding innovation. The text explains that faith, action, and intention are only complete when aligned with the Sunnah, which is defined by the Quran, Hadith, consensus, and clear analogy. Understanding this helps prevent both extreme innovation and neglect of sound scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poetic verse is embedded within the commentary.
2
Identify the two commands
The passage begins with two imperatives: follow the righteous Salaf and avoid innovation from later people. Find them and see how they are supported by hadith.
3
Analyze the logical sequence
Trace the author's reasoning: faith is not complete without action, action without intention, and none of these without conformity to the Sunnah. Understand this dependency.
This sequence shows why avoiding innovation is essential.
4
Define the sources of Sunnah
The passage lists four sources that make a practice Sunnah: the Book, Hadith, consensus, and clear analogy. Memorize these and note that anything outside them is blameworthy innovation.
This definition is key to distinguishing acceptable practice from bid'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبُ اتِّباعِ السَّلفِ واجتناب البدع] ١٣٩ فَتَابِع الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ مِمَّنْ خَلَفَـا (تابع) في عقائدكَ وأقوالِكَ وأفعالك الفَريقَ (الصَّالِحَ مِمَّنْ سَلَفَا) لِشدَّةِ محافظتهم على ذلك دون غيرهم ; لقوله : (عليكم بسُنَّتي وسُنَّة الخلفاء الراشدينَ مِن بعدي ، عضوا عليها بالنواجذ) والصَّالح هو : القائم بحقوقِ الله تعالى وحقوق العباد (وَجَانِبِ البِدْعَةَ) المذمُومَة مِمَّنْ خَلَفَا) أي: مِن الفريق الذي جاءَ بعد خواص الصَّحابة وعُلمائهم; لأنَّ الأمر بالاقتداء بالصحابة في قوله : «أصحابي كالنُّجوم بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم» محمول على العلماء منهم ، وإنَّما طلبت مجانبة البدعة بعد الأمر بمتابعة الصَّالح; لأنَّه لا يكمل قول الإيمان إلا بالعمل، ولا يَكمُل قول ولا عمل إلَّا بنيَّة، ولا يكمل قول ولا عمل ولا نيَّةٌ إِلَّا بموافقة السُّنَّة ، وكلُّ ما وافق الكتاب أو الحديث أو الإجماع أو القياس الجليَّ فهو سُنَّةٌ ، وما خرجَ عن ذلك فهو بدعة مذمومةٌ.
[The obligation of following the Salaf and avoiding innovations] Verse 139: So follow the righteous among those who preceded (the Salaf) and avoid innovation from those who came later. (Follow) in your beliefs, words, and deeds the group (the righteous among those who preceded), because of their intense preservation of that, unlike others; due to his saying: 'Hold fast to my Sunnah and the Sunnah of the rightly-guided caliphs after me, cling to it with your molars.' And the righteous is the one who fulfills the rights of Allah Most High and the rights of the servants. (And avoid innovation) the blameworthy (from those who came later), i.e., from the group that came after the elite of the Companions and their scholars; because the command to follow the Companions in his saying: 'My Companions are like stars; whichever of them you follow, you will be guided' is applied to the scholars among them. And I only asked to avoid innovation after the command to follow the righteous, because the statement of faith is not complete except with action, and statement and action are not complete except with intention, and statement, action, and intention are not complete except with conformity to the Sunnah. And everything that agrees with the Book, the Hadith, consensus (ijmāʿ), or clear analogy (qiyās jalī) is Sunnah, and whatever deviates from that is blameworthy innovation (bidʿa madhmūma).
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is a combined verse and commentary that defines the obligation to follow the righteous Salaf (predecessors) and to avoid blameworthy innovations (bidʿa). It opens with the heading 'Obligation of following the Salaf and avoiding innovations' and then quotes verse 139 of the poem: 'So follow the righteous among those who preceded, and avoid innovation from those who came later.' The commentary first explains the clause 'follow the righteous among those who preceded': it means to imitate them in beliefs, words, and deeds because of their strict adherence to the truth. The proof is the hadith commanding adherence to the Prophet's Sunnah and the Sunnah of the rightly-guided caliphs, with the metaphor of 'biting with molars' indicating firmness. The righteous are defined as those who fulfill the rights of Allah and the rights of people. Then the commentary explains 'avoid the blameworthy innovation from those who came later': i.e., those who came after the elite Companions and their scholars. The command to follow the Companions is understood as applying to their scholars, not all individuals. The author then provides a logical sequence: he first commanded following the Salaf and then commanded avoiding innovation because faith is perfected only through action, action through intention, and all three through conformity to the Sunnah. Finally, he defines Sunnah as everything that agrees with the Quran, Hadith, consensus (ijmāʿ), or clear analogy (qiyās jalī); whatever falls outside these sources is blameworthy innovation.

Key Terms
اتِّباع السَّلف
بدعة مذمومة
سُنَّة
إجماع
قياس جلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every practice not explicitly found in the Quran or Sunnah is automatically bid'ah.
Correction: The author includes consensus and clear analogy as valid sources of Sunnah, so practices based on these are not blameworthy innovation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered Sunnah?
A) What agrees with the Quran
B) What agrees with clear analogy
C) What agrees with personal opinion (ra'y) without basis
D) What agrees with the Hadith

Key Takeaway
Following the Salaf and adhering to the Sunnah—defined by the Quran, Hadith, consensus, and clear analogy—is essential for complete faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific practice in your life—perhaps in speech, worship, or daily conduct—have you assumed is acceptable because your intention is sincere, yet you have never verified its conformity to the Sunnah as defined in this lesson (Quran, Hadith, consensus, or clear analogy)? Will you investigate it today, and if you find it lacking, will you have the courage to abandon it?
?
The passage defines the righteous as those who fulfill both rights of Allah and rights of people. Which right of another person—a family member, a neighbor, or a colleague—are you neglecting while you focus on ritual acts? What concrete step will you take before your next prayer to restore that right?
?
You claim to follow the Salaf, but many of your religious habits were taught by someone ‘from those who came later.’ Name one such habit you are unsure about. How will you determine its source—will you search the Quran, Hadith, consensus, or clear analogy to confirm it is Sunnah, and if not, will you abandon it even if it feels comfortable?
